T he Red Page
Fiction
Nathan Ivussy makes a remarkable firs
appearance in fiction with The Abyss (Ma
millan, New York, per Dymock’s Library
He proclaims that this big volume is only t
first part of a contemplated trilogy, dealin
with the life of his hero. If the c-ontinuatio
is as absorbing as the present instalmen
Nathan Kussy will have pulled off a ver;
big thing. The Abyss is the story of a poo'
Jewish boy who is cast into the abyss
the underworld of those United States. Auc
the picture that this long, detailed anc
grimly realistic narrative reveals of tl
horrors of that world of poverty and crim
is almost revolting in its cruelty and pe
simism. Kussy does not need the emphas
of style; he gets his effect by the impressio
his detail evokes of an abiding underlyin
truth. The reader must remember that th
author is treating of the world of the N.Y
crook some thirty years ago. Condition
may have changed since he painted this re
volting picture: the reader piously hopes so
But that picture remains, engraved on th
mind of the reader.
Samuel Gordin was brought up in New
York as a child as well as his hard-workin
mother, a widow, could manage; but sh
died, and the child, hearing that he is to b
sent to an asylum for orphans, and thinkin
in his ignorance that he is to be placed i
the mad-house, flees in terror. The home
less kid is “adopted” by a professiona
beggar, and taught his trade. Th
whole world of the professional begga
—blind men who aren’t blind, anc
dumb men who chafe under the professiona
restriction "of not speaking till they are o!
duty—is revealed to the kid ; but his edu
cation demands more than this knowledge
He has to play his part; and one detail o:
his preparation, the deliberate maiming o:
his hand by a fiend of a woman into whoso
clutches he comes, is but the first of the
horrors that surround him. One episode
that of the death of a monkey, eclipses ir
its pathos and horror almost any “thrill” in
English literature. The reader would like to
believe that such a thing could not have hap
pened, even in the environment in which the
author declares it took place. But one is
almost forced to believe it true.
“Before many days have passed I realise
that I am in a den where children are
maimed and crippled so that they may more
readily appeal to the sympathies of the
tender-hearted, when they are sent out to
solicit alms upon the public street.... One
evening a big, stout villain comes to us, anc
brings with him a boy of ten whose arm has
been broken and improperly set, causing ii
to be bent and deformed. And Mamma
Winnikin examines the arm critically
(though the child starts back in terror ai
her touch), and the big villain tells her
that she made a good job of it and that he’l
always advise jockers to bring their pru-
shuns (children adopted by professiona]
beggars) to her for repairs. On anothei
evening a man comes in with a boy of If
who is lame. Mamma Winnikin deplores
the fact that he was so careless as to fali
from the window; but he retorts with an
oath that he knows who pushed him, anc
looks so ferociously at her that his jocker
roars in amusement.”
The boy flees from the scene of indescrib-
able horror at the monkey’s death, and falls
again into the hands of his “jocker.” Then
follows a series of sordid experiences as a
hobo, in which, as always throughout this
book, the author displays a detailed know-
ledge of this particular department of life.
Ultimately he is arrested as a vagrant, and
he discovers that life within a gaol is just
as callous and unjust and cruel as life out-
side. A ferocious murder takes places in
his cell, and the boy is accused of it by the
murderers. But though warned by the war-
den, who fears for his job, to tell a prepared
tale, the boy had the truth dragged from him
by a lawyer. He is told that he is innocent,
but is detained in gaol on the pretext that
he is required as a witness for a trial of the
"'’irderers, who have been allowed to escape.
He makes his “getaway” from the warder
with a well-known pickpocket known as
Red Bill, and the two make their way to the
only world they know, the world of the New
York crooks. There the lad has a vague
stirring towards a life of honesty and work.
“I am not repelled by vice, for I, too,
have wallowed in the dirt with the other
beasts of my species; but of late there has
been a strange seething within me, a strange
murmur which I cannot define, or explain,
or understand. Something within me has
changed... .What ails me? What has come
over me? I do not know. I only know that
something binds me to my past; that the
ties which I thought broken were not broken
after all; that, whether I will or bo, I feel
the tug of my childhood upon my sleeves, and
cannot shake it off. And this after years of
degradation, of evil companionship and evil
ways! (And yet that tug ©f baby fingers
upon my sleeves: why can’t I shake it off?).”
He determines that he will not become a
pickpocket, for which trade Reel Bill decides
his slim fingers are specially designed. Like
the hero of romance he shakes off his environ-
ment, and sets forth to seek for honest work.
But he finds it much harder than stealing.
A chance gives him a situation with a good
employer, but the New York detectives, who
have seen him with Red Bill, and who appa-
rently never forget a face—this clichd of the
American crook drama takes on an aspect of
truth by the author’s detailed explanation of
the police methods of impressing a crook’s
features upon the detectives —turn up and
inform his employer of the character of his
employee. The result is the sack for Sam
Gordin. Follow more experiences of seeking
for work; but in every case a detective
arrives, and the lad is turned away. At last
Gordin finds an employer who is willing to
give him a chance, though knowing his
antecedents. This one “Christian” employer
is a Hebrew pawnbroker ; and the boy feels
his feet safely upon the upward path. But
the road is not quite as easy as the roman-
cers glibly assert. And the story closes with
the lad, helpless before the police theory:
“Once a crook, always a crook,” turning
again to the underworld of crime.
In the sequel, however, Ivussy promises to
tell how his hero “walked in darkness,” how
again he groped for light, and how he
acquired an education—for at the end of the
first book Gordin speaks the same ignorant
dialect with which he began—and how he
emerged from the abyss.
The only criticism that the reader will
make of this story is not a distrust of the
truth of the conditions revealed, but a feel-
ing that chance would not have struck so
heavily whenever an opportunity arose for
the boy to escape his fate. In the last
phase of the story, when accused of a theft,
surely he could have proved his innocence by
a frank statement! And it is a wonder that
the boy never thought of finding a way out
by shipping as a sailor.
After all, why not write an occasional
novel for men? There are men who read
novels. And Gappy Ricks, by Peter B.
Kyne (H. K. Fly, New York, per Sydney
Book Club), is just the sort of stuff that
that poor worm, the man who reads novels,
is yearning for. It is a story of the sea,
told as a seaman would tell it. It is also a
story of ship-broking told by a ship-broker.
And it is an absorbing yarn. There is a
girl in it, but the author mercifully omits
all the love-making, and after she marries
the hero the author forgets all about her.
Matt Peaseley, the hero, is a Yankee cap-
tain who is quite sure of himself. Cappy
Ricks, the owner of the shipping line, deter-
mines to fire this impertinent youth, but
Matt Peaseley is not the sort that can be
fired. The telegrams that the two exchange
are full of mordant humor, and the struggle
between Matt and the irascible but capable
Cappy is full of surprises and humor
Then, finding that this youth is a capable
seaman, too good to lose, Cappy decides to
educate him by giving him the worst jobs in
the shipping business. A cargo of creosote
piles, another of green hides, and other
abominations worry Matt, but he survives;
and for every trick of the ingenious Cappy
he has a winning trump. Then the old man
changes his tactics. He sees that this boy is
in love with his daughter, and determines
to educate him for the post of his successor.
Thus he cunningly disapproves of Matt’s
suit, and continues to make a heap of
trouble for the youth. In the end Matt is
educated just as Cappy had meant him to
be. The latter part of the book, dealing
with Matt’s experience's in the shipping busi-
ness ashore, is not too complicated for the
business man.
Peter B. Kyne writes with a keen sense
of humor, and his story is full of neat char-
acter studies, done with the slick touch of
this portrait of one of the chief characters:
“Every large business office has its Skinner
—a. queer combination of decency, honesty,
brains and brutality, a worshipper at the
shrine of Mammon in the temple of the
great god Business, a reactionary Republi-
can, treasurer of his church and eventually a
total loss from diabetes, brought on by lack
of exercise and worry over trifles.”
&
There is much thought and much honest
character-drawing in Mrs. Victor Rickard’s
The Light Above the Gross Roads (Duck-
worth, per Angus and Robertson). This
is a story of the war. The hero, like-so
many heroes conceived by women-writers, is
a hero. Educated by a wise father for the
diplomatic service, fate decides that he must
serve his country as a spy. The mere fact
that he was an Irishman, and educated in
Germany, and the pretence that he had been
dismissed and disgraced by the British Em-
bassy in Berlin, hardly seem to account for
the Germans taking him into their service;
but, though suspected, Marcus is much too
clever a spy to be found out. The situa-
tion of a man who has to appear to his
friends as disgraced and a traitor to his
country, especially as he is in love with an
Irish girl, is “worked” for all it is worth
by _ the author; but the final scenes, in
which the man and the girl and the delight-
ful German who also loves the girl are
brought together on the battlefield, savors
too much of the conventional melodrama.
The reader feels that he is reading fiction,
not viewing life; but it is excellent fiction.
The characters of the decent German officer,
of a typical Prussian general, and of a
demimondaine spy are triumphs of expert
delineation; but the author writes of the
glamor of war, not of its mlity.
AMERICAN VERSE.
You are calling upon me,
Fashionably clothed,
Properly prepared with small talk.
I sit sedately and help build up
The stone wall between us, with my little
bricks of yes and no.
There are hothouse flowers on the table,
Kelt' ' York is outside the window—and
inside —-
The housemaid has set the chairs as care-
fully in their spheres as God could ever
have placed the stars.
Within the grate
There is a fire burning;
It has nearly gone out.
It is only a smouldering red thing now.
But as we look at it
Suddenly ages crumple,
The room vanishes,
You and I are a man and a woman in a cave
With fire—
—Mary Carolyn Davies.
AMBITION.
Kenton and Deborah, Michael and Rose,
These are fine children, as all the world
knows.
Btit into my arms in my dreams every
night
Come Peter and Christopher, FaPh and
Delight.
Kenton is tropical, Rose is pure white,
Deborah shines like a star in the night,
Michael’s round eyes are as blue as the sea
And nothing on earth could be dearer to
me.
But tvhere is the baby with Faith can com-
pare?
What is the color of Peterkin’s hair?
Who can make Christopher clear to my
sight,
Or show me the eyes of my daughter
Delight?
When people inquire I always just state:
“/ have four nice children and hope to
have eight.
Though the first four tire pretty and certain
to please,
Who knows but the rest may be nicer than
these?”
—Aline Kilmer, in Philadelphia Ledgeb.
AN OPERA HOUSE.
Within the gold square of the proscenium
arch
A curtain of orange velvet hangs in stiff
folds,
Its tassels jarring slightly when someone
crosses the stage behind.
Gold carving edges the balconies,
Rims the boxes,
Runs up and down fluted pillar*.
Little Icnife-stabs of gold
Shine out ivhenever a box-door is opined,
Gold clusters
Flash in soft explosions
On the blue darkness,
Suck back to a point,
And disappear.
Hoops of gold
Circle necks, wrists, fingers,
Pierce ears,
Poise on heads,
And fly up above them in colored sparkle*.
Gold!
Gold!
The opera house is a treasure-box of gold.
Gold in a broad smear across the orchestra-
pit:
Gold of horns, trumpets, tubas.
Gold — spun-gold, twittering-gold, snapping-
gold
Of harps.
The conductor raises his baton,
The brass blares out
Crass, crude,
Parvenu, fat, poiverful,
Golden.
Rich as the fat, clapping hands in the boxes.
Cymbals, gigantic, coin-shaped,
Crash.
The orange curtain parts
And the prima donna steps forward.
One note,
A drop: transparent, iridescent,
A gold bubble,
It floats... .floats....
And bursts against the lips of a bank-
president
In the grand tier.
By Amy Lowell, in The Independent.
WHAT BILLZAC READS.
“Gunner Ginger” : Apparently the idea of
Billjim having a taste of his own never
occurs to those who send him reading matter.
Ponder this list taken at random from the
various institutes at Maribyrnong Artillery
Camp, Vic.: Horner's Sunday Stories, vari-
ous yellowbacks, Sunday Times, illustrated
war magazines. The last are as unsuitable
as any of the others.
Muddy View Dugout, somewhere in
France, records its votes thus: For the
war novel: Nil. For humor: nine votes.
And the residents ask for something of this
sort: Billy Hughes, the Dinkum Melbourne
Anzac.
Wanted: Vers Libre
Upon this page are printed some charac-
teristic examples of vers Hire, a form o
poetry that has possibilities, as yet almos
unexploited by our own bards. The rules o
this new form are few, but vague, and han
to define. The assistance of rime and
strict metrical form is denied the vers libret
tist; the appeal of this free verse lies solely
in the control of rhythm and in the ters
expression of emotion or thought. The tru
vers Hire is not prose chopped into arbitrary
lengths, nor is it unrimed metrical verse. I
functions between these two extremes, am
must prove its right to an independent exist
ence by its inherent appeal.
This page offers a guinea for the bes
piece of vers libre received by May 5 ; entrie
to be addressed to this page. The subject fo
this competition is War—this war in an;
of its innumerable aspects and repercussions
not necessarily written by one who ha
taken part in it. For those of our bards
who are somewhere in France or somewhere
in Sinai or somewhere on the North Sea
another guinea will be offered for the bes;
piece of vers libre dealing with war sub
mitted by August 1.
•
Though there is only one English humor
ous artist who has been taken to the Britisl
army’s heart, there are others beside Bairns
father who deserve mention. Angus anc
Robertson (Sydney) forward a cheerfu
batch of illustrated war-humor. Aus
tralians find W. Heath Robinson’s Huntikely
inventions rather childish; but Lawson
Wood’s Splinters (Is.) is delightful work
full of character and brilliant drawing
Some of his jokes are famous, and some were
famous before he illustrated them. In his
illustration to such a joke as this: “Officer
‘Really, Smithers, your figures are damnable
Just look at this 3, anyone would take it foi
a 5.’ Smithers: ‘lt is a 5, sir.’ Officer
‘Good God, I should have sworn it was a
8!’ ” the artist’s types are just those from
whom such a dialogue would be expected
The Bosch Boole , by Alfred Leete (Is.) is
more commonplace fooling: it is so much
easier for the English to discover absurdities
in the Hun than in the Tommy. In Al
the Rumors, Leete has the collaboration oi
Reginald Arkell, who writes a rather obvious
verse commentary. Ragamuffins, by G. L.
Stampa (2s. Gd.), is much more human
humor. Stampa’s understanding of the
London kid is remarkable, and his humor
hovers close to pathos. An instance is
the illuminative illustration of the little girl
standing in front of a huge wall that has
fallen down. Suddenly noticing a policeman,
she cries: “I fahnd it like that. I never
done it, Mister; straight I never!”
William Watson, grown more bitter with
the years, aptly titles his latest collection
of little verses, Retrogression and Other
Poems (Lane, per Sydney Book Club). He
still labors over those too-perfect lines
that wallow in their sonorousness and are
packed so neatly that they leave the mind
nothing to wonder over. Of Dryden he says:
Who tore from the entrails of the mine
The metal of his iron line —
Iron that oft all molten rolled,
Hec ed to a billow, and crashed to g01d...,
and this of Gray:—
Who on worn thoughts conferred
That second youth, the perfect word,
The elected and predestined phrase
That had lain bound, long nights and days,
To wear at last, when once set free,
Immortal pellucidity
and this of Nature:—
Nature! whose lapidary ceas
Labor a pebble without ease,
Till they unto perfection bring
That miracle of polishing:
Who never negligently yet
Fashioned an April violet,
Nor would forgive, did June disclose
Unceremoniously the rose....
All crisply done, but about as appealing
as cold buttered toast. In his quest for
form in poetry Watson has not had room to
put in the heart. And this book is dis-
figured by bitter snarls against his critics
and foolish epigrams on.his enemies.
According to Wilfrid Wilson Gibson, the
English poet, literary criticism in England is
now done by maiden aunts. All the young
reviewers, who are mostly poets trying to
make a living, have gone to the war. This
possibly explains the belief that the war has
changed _ the character of the poetry and
prose being written in England, stopping all
the new .movements and causing a reaction
to mid-Victorianism. It is not the poets who
have changed: it is the reviewers. Being
maiden aunts or maiden uncles they can see
no beauty in modernity; hence the early Vic-
torians have come again into their own, and
the new poets are quietly suppressed. Before
the war, as the reviewers were preponder-
young, they were always sympathetic
with new movements in art and literature.
Luckily Australasian literature is safe. Our
young reviewers stick close to their desks
and never go to see.

health, artificial teeth cannot be compared with a natural set.
pe ? p eWI accordingly try to preserve their teeth as long as possible by
t i hen r> Cle u D ' 9° d wa * er and soa P and water are useful, but somewhat

VARIOS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Wind-Folk.
The wind-folk are casual —they scamper here
and there,
Plucking at a petticoat, dancing o’er a pate,
Stirring into gay pools all the heated air;
Swaying with a rose-spray, swinging with a
gate.
Oh, the jocund wind-folk perched upon the
slats!
Oh, the merry light legs a-dance because they
must,
Putting nimble white toes to sombre silken
hats
Rolling them and kicking and clouding them
with dust!
Flinging into hard eyes clouds of flying
leaves,
Roaring up an alley-way, whining down a
lane,
Dropping through the dim dust, scuffling in
the eaves,
Whistling through a front door and out the
back again!
Tramping up the steep hill where the horses
browse,
Tugging at their brown manes, toying with
their tails;
Down and up and down again to kiss a
maidep’s blouse,
Through a street and o’er a pier to punch
the sagging sails!
Stepping through a dim church, there to
snatch a prayer,
Teasing it and tossing it with many a merry
shout;
Sorting out the frail words and mixing them
with air ;
Dancing up the altar stairs and blowing
candles out!
Ballooning in the cassock of a prating,
rating priest;
Plucking at the orange-blossom of the
bridal-wreath ;
Preaching all the logic that the best is but
the least—
The robe but blinds the vision to the flesh
that is beneath.
And yet to-night-the wind-folk walk many a
darkened room,
Placing cool and pallid hands upon the
fevered cheek ;
They stroke the streaming hair of Grief all
dank within the gloom,
And lull to sleep the moaning word the lips
of Pain would speak.
Into dirty alley-ways, into garrets bare,
The wind-folk are busy folk and noisy with
commands,
They kick the buttocks of Disease down
many a reeking stair
And batter at the face of Grime with tiny
knuckled hands.
Grey doors and fastened doors, fumbling for
the latch,
Whimpering through keyholes, shouting
down the flue;
Tugging at a casement and lugging at the
catch,
Blowing up a drawn blind to let the world
come .through !
Dancing, prancing wind-folk, singing birds’
songs,
Tones that gird the harbor-side, tunes that
crown the hills,
Drowning with a blithe stave the sordid City
wrongs,
Mocking at the drab coins snared within the
till!
Swing the dusty door wide, lift the window
high ;
Draw the chair and palm the chin above the
window sill!
The wind-folk are frolicking between the
earth and sky
Bringing Life and Joy and Love and
Laughter when you will!
MAORILAND.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
The Fairies’ Fair.
Who’s that dancing on the moonlit air,
Heel-toe tapping,
Toe-heel rapping?
Oberon opening the fairies’ fair
To jig away Sorrow on the grave of Care.
Come along, old folk, cold folk, bold folk,
Drop your shears at the midnight stroke.
Elves are crying:
“Who’ll come buying
Jugs of Joy from a fairy’s cloak?”
Mab is sitting on a silver shoe,
Bright eyes laughing,
Light lips quaffing
Airy bubbles from a cup of dew';
Her bracelets tinkle with delights for you.
Come along, tall folk, small folk, all folk.
Race the stream where the fat frogs croak.
Buy a bobbin !
There goes Robin
Tying Time to a daisy’s yoke!
What’s for sale at the fairies’ fair?
Tiny trumpets,
Nuts and crumpets,
Gossamer gowns that the spiders wear,
And w r ee red ribbons from the Poppy’s hair!
Coine, buy a moonbeam slender, tender,
Posset pans or a witch’s spur,
Pixie packets,
Goblin jackets,
Stifling Age in their elfin fur.
AH you pay is a dream or two.
Fancies reeling.
Toe and heeling,
Purchase the pleasures that the fairies woo,
For hone want money as the Grey-folk do.
Come for the wee ware, free ware, glee ware,
Holiday smiles in your loosened hair.
Take your fiddle,
Trip the middle.
Buy back Youth at the fairies’ fair.
jST.S.W.

The Bulletin

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 fa’s. 6'd. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, to any
address in Australasia, post paid.
Post free to any address abroad, £1 10s. per
annum.
Cheques, drafts, .etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS . when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS., to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

after the arrival of each mail, from Messrs. Gordon
and Gotch, Ludgate Circus.

THURSDAY, MARCH 29, 1917.
THE GREAT EVASION.
How much reliance can the Australian elec-
tors place in any promises the Liberals may
make to change the Commonwealth tariff
from being a mere instrument of taxation
into what it ought to be —-a wall against the
importation of manufactured goods which
should be produced by Australian workers?
By this tariff over £13,000,000 is being
raised in revenue each year. The search
for money by the seven Governments is now
so desperate, the Liberal habit of what it
calls “equally distributing the burdens of
responsibility” so deeply ingrained, that
hope of an effective tariff emerging from
that direction has not been causing the
importers any loss of sleep. What of the
promises that Labor may make?' Judged
by what happened to the promises it made
three years ago a further instalment would
be entitled to less admiration than a yarn
about a Fiji uncle were it not that the Free-
trade influences which controlled Labor
policy in the past have been tossed over-
board, and that Labor sees the certainty of
nearly all it stands for going to the scrap-
heap unless the tariff is put upon a proper
basis. Between the two parties in the last
Parliament Australia lias been badly dam-
aged. This paper, judging strictly on per-
formances, is not going to pretend to believe
that the present choice is all it should be.
But it believes that Labor is much less
likely than the other' crowd to sell the elec-
tors a gold brick.
Meanwhile there are a few things to be
said about a tariff. One is that the system
of flinging a schedule down in front of Par-
liament for parties to wrangle over like
hungry pups around a bone is an easy but
sure way of making a hash of the whole busi-
ness. Another is that .a wild lumping of
duties on anything and everything does not
constitute a Protection that is worth having,
A third fact incidental to the consideration
of a tariff is that, where the State offers pro-
tection to an industry, that industry must
make a responsive development. And, finally,
external borrowings amount to the same
thing as wholesale purchases of outside
manufactures. A continuation of borrowing
by Australia will therefore discount such
protection as the tariff may afford. It is to
this that the enormous imports of the last
few years are due. Our foreign loans have
been converted into goods. This must always
occur, tariff or no tariff; and anyone who
comes along with promises to “develop Aus-
tralian industries by the application of a
truly Protective tariff” and also to “main-
tain a vigorous policy of construction by
means of loans” is either a blockhead or a
sharper. Borrowing means Bloat. Protec-
tion means Work—the encouragement of
enterorise and skill. It contemolates an
Australia subsisting on production, not an
Australia loafing on the production of other
people. It means keeping out of debt, pay-
ing the bills, making a nation capable of
standing up on both feet —an Australia de-
pendent only on Australia and self-respect-
ing because self-reliant. If anyone imagines
that Australia can ever be this in reality
without the protection of her growing in-
dustries he imagines a vain thing. So does
the citizen who has the idea that a tariff
against imports can be effective - without
work. So also does the misguided person
who persists in believing that loans “bring
capital into the country” in the shape of
cash. Loans bring goods. Nothing but pro-
hibition would keep goods out; and if aspara-
gus and millinery were put on a black list,
double quantities of complexion powder and
scenic stockings' would arrive instead.
It is, of course, a tragedy that in a coun-
try like Australia —heavily in debt, sparsely
peopled and moth-eaten by party politics—
the tariff should be the sport of groups who
are on deck at one moment and overboard
the next. If the tariff could be taken out of
politics the benefits to the country would be
illimitable. As it is now, the tariff is framed
with one eye on the revenue and the other
half-shut unless there are votes to be cap-
tured. Consequently duties are imposed
upon many articles, not for purposes of pro-
tecting an industry, but to yield cash for the
Treasury—articles which no sane man or
woman believes anyone will even dream of
manufacturing in Australia within the life-
time of people now living. There is no justi-
fication for this —at any rate, when the goods
are such as enter into common use. Duties
are imposed on articles which are often
enough the raw material of another indus-
try, and industries which might reasonably
supplv the whole local market have to com-
pete against imported goods, of which the
raw material had been drawn to the country
of manufacture from /Australia. There is
chaos at almost every point—no concentra-
tion on the achievement of definite ends in
specified directiops; an abandonment of
everything to the off-chance of the public
getting through somehow; problems of the
greatest complexity, demanding continual in-
vestigation, left to be decided by the party
yell or the private wirepull. This sort of
thing is not Protection —scarcely even a bad
imitation of it. The sooner the tariff is
based on a clear conception of what the Aus-
tralian people want to have done instead of
what political parties conceive to be expedi-
ent for themselves, the better. The Com-
monwealth might then have Protection to
carry out the national policy, and protection
also against hot-air merchants who rant
about a tariff as if it were a new thing in
patent medicines.
The issue the Australian Parliament has
to deal with is not going to be determined by
the mere defeat of Freetraders and revenue-
tariffists at the polls. No intelligent Protec-
tionist believes that a Customs duty is a
cure-all, nor listens without 'a good deal of
contempt to much of the talk that flows from
the political mouth on this subject. Unless
the Protectionists returned to Parliament
abandon 90 per cent, of the commonplace
blither about Protection being “essential to
enable Australia to meet the competition of
cheap foreign labor” the electors will have
made a poor selection. All foreign labor is nqt
low-priced. Some of it is paid at a consider-
ably higher rate than Australian labor, and
when the local loan-mongers have done their
worst, and Australians get down to dealing
with the facts and realities of life, this will
probably apply more widely than it does to-
day. To the infant industrv of a new coun-
try the products of the highly-specialised labor
of an older country, working with the latest
technical equipment and receiving generous
rates of pay, are just as great a menace as
the goods issuing from places where the
worker is cheap. Australian engineers re-
quire to have their industry protected against
workers who are paid £1 a day as much as
against those who get 24d. Measured by the
purchasing power of money there are groups
of industrial workers in Europe whose pay
is as high as that of Australians; but unless
the Australian market is held for the local
inhabitant, the factory at the distance, because
of the size of its output and its bigger equip-
ment. will capture the local trade. The
simple question is whether Australia shall
manufacture for herself, people the country,
and work towards achieving an economic in-
dependence. Whether foreigners who are
doing this pay high or low wages has nothing
to do with the matter. That is their busi-
ness. The business of Australia is to produce
enough to live upon and pay her way; and
the standard of living must in the end
depend upon the ability of the Australian
people to make use of their opportunities, the
material at their hands and the market which
other people are now finding in their midst.
[S. H. Prior. F. J. Kirby and John Barr, 214
George Street, Sidney.]
SOME INTERESTING EXPERIMENTS
IN PUB LEGISLATION.
After 10 years’ vigorous work the Victorian
Licenses Reduction Board has-had its job
changed. The State’s Act of 1906 was the
most scientific attempt yet made in Aus-
tralia to reduce the number of pubs and
improve the quality of those that remained
without doing violence to those men who
■were pushed out of the business. Under the
earlier Victorian law local option was given
a chance. But it was both uncertain and
unfair in its results. Under every licensing
law r which had thus far embodied the prin-
ciple of compensation, every closure made
it more difficult to close any other pub,
because the pub that was left became more
valuable. That difficulty was got over by
fixing the value of the license, both to the
owner and to the licensee, on the basis of
the pub’s position in 1903-4-5 in the case
of licensees and in 1904-5-6 in the case of
owners. The compensation to be paid might
be less than the figures thus arrived at, but
not more. Two other things were done:
the number of licenses to which any licens-
. ing district was entitled was fixed on the
basis of population, and a Compensation
Fund was created by an annual levy of 3
per cent, of the cost of the liquor purchased
by the hotelkeeper. At the end of 1906 there
were 3448 hotels open in Victoria, and this
was 1368 in excess of the number to which
the State was entitled on the population
basis adopted. The job of the Licensing
Reduction Board was to use the Compen-
sation Fund, year by year, to buy out as
many of that 1368 as the money in hand
would allow. The experiment was to be
tried for 10 years.
The 10 years were up on December 31,
and the results have astonished even those
who were responsible for the experiment. No
.less than £545,045 was paid into the Compen-
sation Fund; and with £540,851 of this
money 1054 hotels have been delicensed. In
the vast majority of cases the Reduction
Board has decided as between various pubs
which should be closed. The natural con-
sequence has been a competition among the
hotels in an overstocked district to so im-
prove themselves and their conduct that the
axe should not fall on their own necks. The
Board’s general policy has been to cut out
the pub that was a mere drinking shop;
and the importance of that incidental reform
can hardly be over-estimated. Yet because
of the cast-iron rigidity of the law, the
Board had no authority whatever over the
pubs, no matter how evil in themselves or
how badly conducted, in districts which
were not over-pubbed according to law. In
the few under-pubbed districts the position
was not improved by the difficulty of getting
authority through a poll of the electors to
increase the licenses. In only three districts
in 10 years was a poll successful.
* * * , #
An Act passed, last year, in view of the
pending expiry of the 10-year period, has
removed some of the comparatively few
defects exposed by the operation of the old
law. -On the expiry of the 10-year period
Parliament provided that local option
should come into use again, the first poll
to be taken at the first general election after
January 1, 1917. But once more there was
a score for Cold Tea, who has been fighting
his campaign with an intelligence and a
cunning quite unexpected; for, using the
war as a pretext, he pushed the first local
option poll on to the second general election
after January, 1917, the newly-created
Licensing Court being meantime entitled
to proceed “as if reduction had been carried
in every licensing district throughout the
State.” The possible, not to say highly
probable, effects of this are very far-reach-
ing. Again and again the old Board,
which has now been constituted a Licensing
Court for the entire State, has complained
Of its lack of authority over pubs in dis-
tricts either under-pubbed or owning just
the statutory number of beer pumps. JL'he
assumption that the district has voted for
reduction removes that grievance; and the
pub which has been disreputable because it
has felt safe may soon find itself sprawling
outside in the gutter. This dreadful possi-
bility is a whip that is going to, drive many a
pub in Victoria to call in the carpenter and
the painter in order to avoid a visit from
the undertaker. A lot can be done in regard
to the closure of such pubs at no great
cost, though so far as money is concerned
the Fund will be in a stronger position now,
probably, than ever it was. At any rate
a new basis of contribution has been estab-
lished. In Victoria, as in most other
States, there has been a fixed license fee,
having no relation to the business trans-
acted. Under the new law this fixed fee
is abolished, and a total contribution of 6
per cent, of the cost of liquor purchased is
payable, three-eighths of it by the owner
and five-eiglitlis by the licensee. This basing
of the license fee upon the liquor consumed
will admirably suit the useful little country
pub, which was so harshly treated under the
old law that many a license, desirable from
the point of view of public convenience, was
voluntarily surrendered.
* * * *
At the second general election after
January, 1917. the State will vote for reduc-
tion. continuance or no license; and if no
license is carried there may be a subsequent
vote for restoration. In the latter case
probably, and in connection ivith a special
brand of license that may be granted in
the Mallee certainly, a new principle of tre-
mendous consequence has been introduced.
It. was shown during the hearing of a case
in N. S. Wales the other day that for the
goodwill of a pub in a not-very-populous sub-
urb of Sydney £lO,OOO was paid to the lessor.
That was the value of the license for a
possible maximum of 21 years, and was
apart altogether from the heavy rent of the
premises. The new Victorian law lays
down the principle, in effect, that that
£lO,OOO belongs to the State which grants
the license. The Licensing Court has cast
upon it the duty of ascertaining the “added
value” given to the premises year by year;
and that monopoly value is to be paid to
the licensing fund. It is an interesting
attempt made to obtain for public or semi-
public purposes a little of that unearned
increment whose appropriation for private
purposes is one of the curses of the curious
thing we like to call our modern civilisation.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Dardanelles Inquiry.
It is not only the friends of the pilloried
people who will feel resentment at the action
of the British Government in connection
with the report of the Dardanelles Commis-
sion. Any man who makes the Fair Deal a
rule of personal conduct must feel discom-
fort if not disgusted because of this crude
attempt to “disguise political rancor in the trap;
pings of public duty. The reflections upon and
allegations against Kitchener and AsquiTh
and Churchill and others may or may not
be deserved. That is not the point, for the
best of all reasons —that if it was there
are no means by which it can be tested. This
paper was always disposed to discount the
popular ideas about Kitchener and did so
many times. It has never been able to feel
enthusiastic over Asquith. But it has always
recognised that they were both remarkable
men and is prepared to believe now that,
taking all the circumstances of the hour into
consideration, their services to their country
were given with a singleness of purpose and
devotion to duty which more than condone
those minor errors that may be charged
against them. Whether this view or any
other will have to be revised in the light of
a greater knowledge matters nothing. The
certain thing is that to put these men in the
dock now, read a censored indictment against
them and on that alone pass a verdict of
condemnation is a monstrous abuse of the
Fair Thing. The dead Kitchener and the
living Asquith and Churchill and a
dozen others have been given a cooked deal.
Who is to know whether the evidence pro-
duced before the Commission justifies the
report? Where can a word be found to
answer Asquith’s protestation that to with-
hold the evidence on which a public report
is based is grossly unfair? This thing leaves
a nasty taste in the mouth. It will do
Lloyd George, ■ Carson and Bonar Law no
good. That the High Commissioner for
Australia should have been associated with
a Commission which appears to have been
used as a poison-gas retort is a lamentable
circumstance. The Bulletin permitted it-
self the luxury of several derisive words
when the Commission was appointed and
had the satisfaction of hearing later that
Fisher -was not appointed as a represenV-
tive of Australia. Had this not been made
clear there would be an immediate obliga-
tion on the Commonwealth Government to
disown Australia’s connection with the low
down trick of some venomous partisan of the
two-penny variety.
The Yearning of Hughes for Hush.
When Hughes was in Sydney last week a
Telegraph reporter put it to him: —
In the event of the Ministry being returned with a
majority at the elections, will the next delegation to
London be the same —yourself, Irvine, and Forrest?
Hughes : Of course, I will say nothing about that.
At the risk of being prosecuted under the
War Precautions Act for talking in a man-
ner calculated to prejudice recruiting, The
Bulletin interposes that this addition to
the things that Hughes wants to say noth-
ing about is not good enough. Weeks ago
Hughes told Australia that it was a matter
of “vital necessity” that the Commonwealth
should be represented at the Conference.
His fuglemen have been weeping tears of
blood that Australia is not represented.
Cook has been explaining once every 24
hours that the Labor party is to blame.
Brother Bean has forwarded dispatches
from the trenches announcing the disgust of
the army. Every issue of the S. M. Herald
has a sob over the situation “in which every
citizen is deeply interested.” This paper
agrees to some extent with all this. Every
Australian is interested in the representation
of Australia, and has a just cause of quarrel
with the Ministry for not appointing Fisher
to go on with. The representation of this
country—for there will be more Conference
after the one now sitting—enters sharply
into the issues to be decided at the election.
Is the spokesman to be Hughes alone or
Hughes fortified by Big.toiin and Irvine?
What is the Government’s policy on this
matter ? Hughes ought to say. It he does not,
the only possible conclusion is that he wants
to keep the subject covered up. In either case
the Australian citizen who objected to the
posturing wind-baggery of the last visit can
help to save his country from having to en-
dure any more of it. The opportunity will
come on May 5.
[S. H. Prior, F. J. Kirby and John Barr, 211
George Street, Sydney.]

Neglected Millions.
Within less than a hundred miles of Syd-
ney Harbor there are the most important
known beds of oil-sliale in the world. They
are probably the largest; in view of then
exceptional richness they certainly contain
the greatest quantity of oils. The value of
the contents runs into tens of millions--
possibly hundreds of millions. Yet, though
no country in the world wants millions more
than Australia does, and though no countri
is so badly in need of the thousands of men
to whom these deposits might give employ-
ment, we continue to import all our fuel and
lubricating mineral oils, all our paraffin wax
and most of the other articles which the
Wolgan and Capertee shale-beds could sup-
ply. Though our requirements have enor-
mously increased, not only because of the
growth of our population but also because or
the vast multiplication of machines that use
liquid fuel, we actually do less with our
own resources than we did 30 years ago,
when little Hartley was made busy by the
N. S. Wales ShahTand Oil Co. Our great
misfortune was that that little co. was
bought put by the most scandalously mis-
managed concern that ever made a pretence
of developing this country with foreign capi-
tal. But that does not affect the essentials
of the matter. Here we have these vast
potentialities; we need population and work
for the people to do, and shall need it more
than ever when we have to meet our heavy
war expenditure; and we have a big market
which foreign' capital and labor are now
supplying. A few months ago the Tas-
manian Government was prepared to open
up comparatively small and poor shale
deposits on the island under an agreement
with the C jmmonwealth Government, which
is the lr gest individual consumer of fuel
oil in A itralia. That scheme lapsed because
the Sr ck’s Leg. Council was afraid to face
the ' rpital outlay. N. S. Wales can do
better; and the Government, having a pro-
blem of employing its people ahead of it,
ought to lose no time in seeing whether it
cannot find a way of doing this better thing.


Ryan Talks About Prices.
Blowing off tropical atmosphere at' Sydney
Town Hall last week about Conscription,
Bananaland Premier Ryan started arguing
about the efficiency of Labor Governments and
“quoted the figures of Statistician Knibbs
showing that the cost of living was less in
Bananaland than in any other State.” As
everyone in the audience had left Ivnibbs's
figures at home. Ryan had the calculations
to himself; and all hands burst into frantic
applause at the finish. Of course the “effici-
ency” of any Government is good to hear
about. So The Bulletin, thirsting after
joy, turns to the arithmetic of Knibbs. This
sets out the cost of goods and shelter at vari-
ous periods and discloses that what could be
acquired for £1 in 1911 could be bought in
the cities named during 1916 for the follow-
ing money:—•
Sydney .. .. 275. lOd.
Melbourne .. 265. 2d.
Adelaide .. .. 255. Bd.
Perth .. .. 255. 4d.
Hobart .. .. 255. 7d.
Brisbane .. .. 235. 9d.
All of which would at least seem to be a
useful story if Ryan had been getting his
■ efficiency to work over that period. He
might then claim that the Labor Govern-
ment in Sydney was more inefficient than
the Peacock outfit in Victoria, and that his
own efficiency in relation to the efficiency of
J. Scaddan had been as ss. 4d. is to 3s. 9d.
But even that dubious victory is forbidden
him. Ryan has only been in charge of the
cost of living and Imperial meat and im-
ported beetle-catchers since 1915, and the
story told by Knibbs of what has taken
place during the period of Ryan’s efficiency
is this: —
1914. 1916. Rise.
Sydney . . 245. Id. 275. lOd. 3s. 9d.
Melbourne . . 225. Id. 265. 2d. 4s. Id.
Adelaide . . 225. lOd. 255. Bd. 2s. lOd.
Perth . . 225. lOd. 255. 4d. 2s. 6d.
Hobart . . 21s. lOd. 255. 7d. 3s. 9d.
Brisbane .. 19s. lid. 235. 9d. 3s. lOd.
Thus under Ryan’s very efficient manage-
ment Knibbs has discovered a bigger rise
in the Bananaland figures than in those for
any other State except Victoria, which had
experienced a very horrible drought. Knibbs
finds also that the Bananaland figures have
the advantage of rent being lower in Bris-
bane than in any other of the cities. Ryan
ought to talk about something else. The
fact that in the two years before he came
into office there was a move upwards of
only 4d. and that since he has been there
the" advance is 3s. lOd. is hardly a matter to
cackle about.

Germany’s Smash in China.
“The Pariah’s” story of the Hun and the
Chow
It was not until 1861 that Germany, succeeded in
scouring a commercial treaty with China similar to
those concluded long before by Britain, France and
Russia. An amplification of this treaty, in 1880,
gave the Teuton advantages over his rivals; but he
was handicapped in trade by having no jumping-off
place of his very own. He proceeded to get one.
In 1897 the chance he was looking for came, when
two of his missionaries were murdered in Shantung.
The demands then served on China were more degrad-
ing and grasping than those which Austria made on
Servia in 1914, but the unhappy Manchu Court had to
agree to them all. Among them was the cession of
Kiauchau. What German officialdom thought of
Kiauchau can easily be gauged by a translation of
a report made by Lieut-colonel Wagner in 1898:
“No portion of the Chinese Empire can be so easily
acquired by an enemy as the Shantung Peninsula. Its
recapture, however, would be most difficult, while
whoever held it could make use of it for a further
advance. .. .No invasion of the country could be
attempted without the capture of Kiauchau.” The
Colonel also pointed out to his hungry countrymen
that the complete line of hills 400 to 600 feet high
and the narrow entrance “make a fortified Kiauchau
impossible of capture, and at the same time the
port mal;cs the positions of Port Arthur (.Jap) and
]Vei-hai-wei (British) precarious.”
Having thus “captured” Kiauchau, Germany began
a commercial war that was compounded of honest
and dishonest thoroughness. She encouraged by rich
prizes the study of Oriental conditions and languages,
and successful students were given posts at good
salaries in commercial houses in the “East.” Of the
“slim” tactics adopted, perhaps the most efficacious
were worked through the Imperial Customs. . This
service is of a peculiar texture, being carried on by,
and for, the benefit of European nations, which have
various claims on China. The - staff is polyglot, and
is scattered to the furthest limits of the Empire.
The German tidewaiters used every means to delay
in transit goods dispatched from British houses, thus
putting the British merchant under a serious disabil-
ity. Moreover, where railways existed the German
was again in evidence. His agents played upon the
superstitious natives of the districts served by Brit-
ish lines, with the result that exasperating delays,
etc., hampered operations.
- One way and another it is not therefore to be
wondered at that, in 15 years, from 1897 on-
wards, German trade increased enormously in volume,
while British business diminished. Not only in the
North was this so, but in the Middle and South as
well, though British influence predominates there.
Now the conservative Englishman gets a fresh start;
and if he springs off the mark and makes the pace a
welter, the good old days of “the merchant princes
of Canton” may return. But on present appearances
he will do nothing of the kind.

The Eternal Prayer of Fat—“O Lord,
Let Me Shirk.”
At a meeting of the Associated Chambers
of Commerce at Adelaide last week it was
moved by Braddon, of Sydney :
That the proposed levy on wealth is subversive to
the well-recognised principles of equitable taxation.
Thereupon Brother Kerr, of Melbourne; —
The proposed levy is an insidious attack upon the
sacred security of capital. It is dangerous to let the
suggestion go forth that capital is insecure in Aus-
tralia.
“The sacred secuiyty of capital” ; the “sacred
security” of Kerr’s capital; the “sacred
security of the money-bags of every land-
shark and boodler and profiteer and interest-
monger on the continent—and Australia
heard this shocking proposition at the very
hour when her sons were fighting their way
into Bapaume, and while in agonised
Europe the security of human life was less
than that of a weed! There is a War Pre-
cautions Act in force in Australia, and it
has at times been used bowellessly against
obscure individuals and publications to meet
the use of language allegedly prejudicial to
recruiting. Yet in no .case that this papei
has heard of was the language so open to a
just censure as this. Where in _ the name
of the everlasting pawnshop is capital
“secure” in the sense of being kept on de-
posit underneath an altar which it . Avould
be impious to touch? The possession of
capital rests entirely upon, social consent.
Everywhere it is .called on to meet such
requirements of society as the wisdom or
lunacy of the State decides. To claim a
sacredness for capital is almost blas-
phemous. To give it out to the world that
capital is profane in Australia and sacred
everywhere else is almost criminal. Whether
the wealth levy was framed on lines that
were socially prudent is another thing. But
on general principles such a levy is indis-
putably legitimate. There were details of
it that The Bulletin objected to and will
continue to oppose. But that was not
because capital is sacred; it was because a
fair deal is.
Patriotism —and Other Things.
War-born industries don’t stop at the mak-
ing of businesses for Repatriation Commit-
tees to buy. There is another very flour-
ishing one in the founding of patriotic
leagues. Their objects, when not for the
elucidation of the obvious, are generally for
the augmentation of the superfluous, like
National Federations and Win-the-War Min-
istries. They are generally run by a sole
founder or an only-official organiser, and
the foundation-stone and the king-pin of all
of them is an annual subscription. Some
issue certificates of patriotism or British-
ness ; some “reject” badges or “relation”
buttons; some, the more Machiavellian, only
issue receipts for the subscriptions. At the,
start these affairs asked only., a shilling
entrance fee, a lot of faith and a rew prayers
for Allied victory ; but they are now grow-
ing up and reaching out with all the busi-
ness method of a new Yankee religion. The
latest asks a five-shiUings-a-month sub. and
proposes to issue “white lists” of its “oatri-
otic” subscribers. Now, these leagues and
such, whatever may sometimes be the high
motive of the founders, are nothing but
genteel blackmail. They practically say to
traders and others: “Be a patriot or we’ll
black-list you—a guinea, please.” They
serve no useful purpose; on the other hand
they deflect a lot of money from the genuine
amelioration funds. For sane people to sign
fool-declarations of patriotism is on a par
with expecting a decent woman to join a
Virtue League, and to go about Lagged with
a button inscribed: “No sir; I will NOT!”
But there’s the subscription—always the
subscription. That often smells a little.
(How deadly humorous it would be if the
cash went into the German secret-service
fund, or for the provision of gramophones
for the internment camps!) Apparently
until we tax directly to liquidate all
national obligations to the poor, the sick,
the needy, th(‘ feckless and the irreclaim-
able. we will have to do circus tricks to
raise money; but at least let us reduce
the societies for promoting circus tricks to
a minimum. And let us know a bit more
about the founders before they are allowed
to roam promiscuously through the country
with no better imprimatur than a receipt
block and a plausible story. Is any balance-
sheet ever issued in connection with this
great (and no doubt lucrative) business of
propping up the Empire? The woman with
the tale of a hypothetical husband and 12
kids works with the shadow of the vag. ever
hanging over her. A sick empire seems to
need no certificate at all.
When Prices Dropped.
A while ago a youth came into a bit of
property. It wanted clearing and develop-
ing, so he borrowed money to put up the
fences, more money to get it grubbed,
ploughed and graded, and still more money
to put up stables and sheds and buy imple-
ments. He saw that borrowing was easy,
so he eased off work and did not produce
enough to meet his interest bill and pay his
way ; and whenever he wanted a new plough
or a more modern cultivator he borrowed
additional money to get it. His overdraft was
fairly solid —£200, or it may have been
£200,000,000 —when a cantankerous neighbor
drove some bullocks through his fences,
devastated his crops and threatened to blot
him out altogether. The repairing of the
fences and the subsequent law cases meant
more borrowing, lashings of it; and though
he beat his opponent in the end that tricky
scoundrel went insolvent and the youth was
saddled with monumental bills of costs and
other dead-horses, so that he had to drag
between £2O and £3O a year—or it may have
been 20 or 30 millions—out of his little
property before he could get a loaf of bread
for himself. .He buckled down then to
solid graft, but a few years later there was
a spell of such prolific production by his
trade competitors that prices of wheat, but-
ter, vegetables, etc., fell 50 per cent, and he
had to bog in something tremendous, so that
even his cherished 8-hour day and minimum-
wage principles went smash. Just about
this time it dawned on him that the borrow-
ing business wasn’t what it was cracked up
to be, and if he could have met the men who
cracked it up he would probably have slain
them.
The Shirkers.
In 1914 Australia elected a Parliament to
which it returned a large majority of mem-
bers pledged individually, their party
organisations and through the leaders they
then were yelping for, to make reform of the
tariff one of t-lieir first undertakings. That
Parliament fell to pieces last week. It had
done nothing with the tariff; and in some
death-bod speeches it was made very clear
that there never was any honest intention
to redeem the pledge the public had been
doped with. Even those members who may
have been originally willing to make good
had to confess their own miserable weakness.
Because it suited the party to leave this sub-
ject alone —because the Cabinet was delud-
ing the public with words of promise in
w hich every syllable was false —these weak-
lings sat dumb. Perhaps they can find some
specious excuse for themselves that their
constituencies will swallow. This paper
does not know anything about that. What it
does know is that these people ought to be
ashamed of themselves —that the result of
their scandalous shirk has been to gravely
jeopardise the financial and industrial stabil-
ity of their country, and that if the next
Federal Parliament is comnosed of the same
material the Commonwealth will be found
disporting itself in the waves of disaster. All
the fumbling about with price-fixing and
wheat-pools and metal-exchanges and sugar
bargains and the prohibition of hide exports
has been mere quackery. While this has
been going on the nation has been bleeding
to death under an avalanche of imports
which in times of peace would have been of
calamitous dimensions, and when super-
iLoposed upon the financial strain of war
can only be accounted for on the basis of
government being in the hanus of madmen.
To say that interference with the tariff was
impossible because it would create trouble
with our Allies is too thin. None of our
Allies are fat-headed enough to squander
their wealth and credit in the way Aus-
tralia has been doing and is continuing fo
do to-day. The relationship between this
country and other peoples does not demand
that the Commonwealth should fling away
millions a year in purchasing goods the
country cannot pay for. No other country
does what Australia is doing, nor expects
Australia to do it. Though the trend of
prices for Australian products has been up-
ward since 1910, the imports to the country
have cost about £36,000-,000 in excess of
what the exports realised. Who is it de-
mands that these leaps and bounds towards
bankruptcy should continue? To say that
our Allies want it is to insult them. The
solemn truth is that nobody wants it con-
tinued except the tricksters and shirkers
who have been encouraging the performance
for their own small temporary gain.
[S. H. Prior, F. ,T. Kirby and John Barr, 214
George-street, Sydney.,]

Australian war-correspondent Bean has
been at his politics again. The other day,
just on the eve of the taking of Bapaume, he
made some earnest remarks about the game-
ness of the Australian troops, “doing Aus-
tralia’s work in the old Australian way,”
with a few . details of attacks, counter-
attacks, shell-and-mortar work and assorted
fighting up and down the countryside. Then
suddenly he burst into tears and finished off
with this (printed by the Argus in bold-
faced type) :
The pitiful part of it is that, while half of Aus-
tralia is doing its work magnificently at the Front,
all they can see across the seas is the other half of
Australia running round distractedly wondering
which leader to follow.
But that’s where Bean goes wrong. “The
other half” of Australia isn’t running round
distractedly wondering which leader to fol-
low. “The other half” is writing paragraphs
and growing wheat and digging copper and
spuds and knitting socks and Balaclavas and
shearing sheep and making butter and jam
and going to picnics. But a little man
named Hughes is rushing round distractedly
telling all these people that if they don’t
knock off work to vote for him they won’t
win the war. If Bean can’t see what is
happening in France better than he sees
what is happening in Australia he’d better
come home.
[S. 11. Prior, F. J. Kirby and John Barr, 214
George Street, Sydney.]
At last week’s struggle for seats in Ma
State’s Assembly Labor was handed what it
expected, and what later on it is going to
feel very much better for getting. By put-
ting the party into opposition and by purg-
ing Parliament, the electors can look back
on a good day’s work. Labor was hungry
for correction. The party that threw into
place and power the men responsible for
the shocking administrative jobbery of the
last six years; that introduced the commu-
nity to Norton Griffiths and “lost money” ;
that tolerated a financial jag of almost in-
credible recklessness and sought to sustain
itself in office by throttling every decent
principle associated with Labor’s birth as
an organised political force—such a party
had no claim to handle the trust it had so
grossly betrayed. The objection that the
other Party is quite as frowsy-looking could
not meet the disinclination of the electors
to swallow the P.L.L. Had Holman gone
to the electors as a Labor man prior to the
happenings associated with the Referendum,
Labor would have been defeated just as it
was on Saturday ; and in this circumstance
alone the P.L.L. ought to be able to detect
several reasons for examining .itself from top
to bottom. While it is making this examina-
tion it may, as a critic of public policy,
again be of great service to the State, after
a period of administrative activity in which
if did the State very great injury.
Sydney Telegraph broke out into an im-
passioned oration on the morning of the
N. S. Wales State elections. It seemed to
lie in a condition of panic and spoke in terms
of remonstrance concerning citizens who
turned themselves into “unfranchised serfs”
and addressed pointed remarks to individuals
burdened with “the natural instinct of a
slave.” The worst of it, groaned the Tele-
graph, was that most of the mean-spirited
excuses for shirking a citizen’s duty were
sure to be on the National side. All of
which sounded strangely at variance with the
yarn put forward by the Telegraph several
times a day for several weeks past that
Liberalism represented the civic virtues of
the community and that around its standard
stood the accumulated social patriotism of a
people outraged by juntas and firebrands!
Yet if during the next month or two someone
proposes to make voting compulsory-—to
actually disfranchise the vote-shirker who
disfranchises himself —the Telegraph can
be depended on to publicly explode with
wrath, unless it is prepared to announce
that its previous 974 explosions were un-
called for. It is obvious enough, however,
that if a man or woman has the instincts of
a slave or prefers to be a serf, such person
is entitled to reformative treatment. People
with “the instincts of slaves” have no moral
right to a vote. They are a peril. If the
Telegraph has reason to suppose that the
Lib. constituencies are infested with them,
its obvious duty is to lead a crusade for
purging the rolls of such political wasters.
But what is the matter, anyhow? Can it
be that vast numbers of Libs, are unable to
get the taste of the Wade-Holm an deal out
of their mouths? If so, what sort of grim-
aces are there going to be when they are
asked to swallow Hughes and the deals he
has made?
“A.M.C.”: I enlisted to fight because my
country asked me to. But when I was safely
in camp I was summarily transferred to tlie
A.M.C., and here I am swearing in Sinai
with scores of other Red Crossers who only
want to be allowed to join a combatant
unit. For 12 months now we’ve been trying
to transfer, but we are politely blocked at
every turn by O.C.’s of units and the medi-
cal head serang. Their favorite excuse is lack
of A.M.C. personnel; but this is a mere suu-
terfuge. reinforcements being plentiful and
casualties small. Again, take the case of
an N.C.0., who to enable him to transfer
agrees to forfeit his rank; his application is
returned from the. medical chief with the
reply that it will be approved only on con-
dition that the applicant can improve his
present status. This on the face of it is
absurd and impossible. It is well known
that there is no promotion in the A.M.C.
beyond non-commissioned rank. Hence appli-
cations from these N.C.O.’s should receive
special consideration. It is only natural
that ambitious men should be eager to
transfer to units where promotion is to be
had.' Regarding promotion generally, why
does not Australia adopt the Maoriland sys-
tem, whereby N.C.O.’s may be sent back
from the Fronts to be trained as officers, to
return with new units or reinforcements?
Men who have had over two years’ active
service are'surely more entitled to a commis-
sion than men without experience who have
taken so long a time to hear their country
call!
T. J. McMahon writes; —
Of all the moral experiments ever tried by a
Government, the keeping of hotels as in the Northern
Territory is the most woeful failure. The object was
to stem the excessive drinking for which the Terri-
tory became notable, or, at least, for which Darwin
did; <but a whole dictionary of adjectives cannot
adequately describe the orgies which are still to
be seen in that town on far too many occasions.
The Commonwealth Government may be making
money out of its gropshops, but, if so, it is at the
expense of the nation’s health.
The leading house, though not the most popular,
is as cheerless as a catacomb. It is run on lines
that make a Chinese puzzle child’s play by compari-
son. At 10s. a day it is just about the limit for
what it does not supply. You get no morning tea,
no boots blacked, no shaving water brought; the staff
occupies the coolest and best rooms, and if you
want a square feed you go to the Dago’s in China-
town. When I was there fresh milk nourished the
staff whilst the boarders perished on tinned cow-
juice. You never knew when the bar would be
open or when it would be shut, and you paid dearly
for your thirst. The first thing the Government
did when going into this business was to raise the
tariff 100 per cent.
Another pub is a structure of galvanised-iron, so
dirty and shabby that no Licensing Board in a
southern State would give it a license. The roof is
just about 2 ft. away from the drinkers’ heads in
the bar, and the room is like a fiery furnace, no
doubt a good aid to persistent drinking, which is
one way of getting back the purchase money.
Vestey’s pay-day once a fortnight gives the cos-
mopolitan population of Darwin a grand chance for
letting itself go. The maddened foreigners, contending
with the few noisy-Britishers, make a show unparal-
leled in the Commonwealth. Police interference is para-
lysed by strange laws that protect hotel managers,
and tolerate drunks who sleep peacefully in the
weeds of the big vacant allotments in the heart of the
town. Occasionally a very obstreperous drunk is run
in, which leaves the Government of Australia in
the unenviable position of encouraging the trans-
gression and then punishing the transgressor—coin-
pound interest on its hotel expenditure.
These hotels “must pay” is the instruction, and
run on departmental lines per favor of union rules
and regulations they constitute about as contemptible
a venture as any Government might stoop to.
' »
If these Win-the-War politicians were not
far more anxious about winning their seats
they would drop the untruth that the Labor
party is opposed to recruiting, and would
put the War Precautions Act in operation
against these people. Because of the con-
stant reiteration of the lie there must be
hundreds of young Labor sympathisers who
begin to believe it; and if to make Labor
men believe that they can’t both enlist and
be loyal to the Labor party isn’t prejudicial
to recruiting, nothing is.
One of the titbits that fell into the maw of
the Commonwealth soon after it was estab-
lished was silver and copper coinage: previ-
ously Britain had allowed us to coin gold, on
which there is no profit, but kept for herself
the minting of silver and bronze “tokens’'
(the Royal Mint refuses to even think of
these things as “coins”). During last finan-
cial year the Commonwealth struck off silver
of a circulation value of £554.300 and
£15,500 worth of “browns” ; and as the cost
of the raw material in the one case was
£207,002, and in the other £3137, the gross
profit on the silver was 160 and oil the
bronze 320 p.c.. It looks good; but the
profit on paper-money is a lot more than
that. And, as Billjim has found—to his
surprise mostly—our silver and bronze are
not currency a bit further than our paper.
“Bile Bowyang” : I see that theorists are
suggesting that a million natives be brought
from Bull’s black colonies. This sort of non-
sense makes practical men tired. The origi-
nators of the idea advertise the past fighting
record of the Zulu and others; but they
forget that these races fought their best
battles on their own ground and under sim-
ple Stone Age conditions. The bit we got
even at Gallipoli made the best Indians curl
up. I have lived amongst and fought beside
colored troops recruited since the commence-
ment of the war, and in my opinion, what
with the cost of their transportation, train-
ing and upkeep (which last is greater than
the cost* of European troops), the employ-
ment of colored men on a big scale would be
a waste of the Empire's money. Although
the majority are of good physique, they
quickly succumb to the variations of tem-
perature experienced in France. In times of
peace recruits for the regular colored regi-
ments are enlisted for a period of 12 years, of
which it takes five to make them efficient
soldiers. We can’t spare five years to get
men ready for this war.

Where is Ivan Going.to be Found?
It had been all carefully figured out that
“decisive” events were to take place during
this European summer on the Western front.
They may. But the uproar in Russia is
not passing unnoticed at Potsdam, and, unless
the cable messages of a day or two ago were
more unreliable than usual, it seems quite
likely that something with a close resemblance
to decisiveness may happen in the vicinity of
the Bear before it occurs anywhere else.
Germans are reported to be assembling in
large numbers on the Dvinsk-Riga sector with
hostile intent, their object being to rush
through in the direction of Petrograd.
General Alexieff has reported that “the
position is serious.” If the Germans suc-
ceed in reaching the Russian capital it will,
of course, be announced that the incident is
of no importance, whereas if they fail the
world will hear that their dreams have again
been shattered. This will be in strict accord-
ance with our apparently settled policy of fool-
ing ourselves. Exactly what a German victory
opening the path to Petrograd would mean
in a military sense may be doubtful, but it
can be taken as highly probable that such an
incident would not lead to great rejoicing
anywhere outside the Fatherland. In the
judgment, then, of this paper the events of
the next month or two on the Eastern front
are going to be more important than the
occurrences in the West. The significance
of the Russian revolution can still hardly
be guessed at. If the result is to
sweep the “dark forces” into oblivion
and strengthen the national resolution
relating to the war, the defeat of Germany
is certain. If the opposite is the case—and
some very ominous news is being published—
the end of the war will be very different
from what the cocksure prophet of yesterday
was talking about. An entirely new factor
has been introduced into the disturbance.
Meanwhile Uncle Sam is still on the verge,
Italy is alleged to be expecting a big push,
and the retirement of Von Hindenberg on
the Somme Front had not, up to the begin-
ning of the week, been explained by the dis-
covery of where he had settled down. But
the ground there will be drying up now, and
as events will move more quickly, the next
phase of horror may arrive before the
hot-cross buns. Yet if the war is going to
reach the knock-out stage this year, the con-
centrated bash will have to be on a scale so
prodigious that it may scare the entire popu-
lation out of Hades.
D. E. Dicker. M.L.A., didn’t turn up at the
first two meetings of the Speck’s Public
Works Committee after the police-court case
against him for disloyal utterances. When
he did arrive it was tactfully suggested that
perhaps he would rather not sit in future.
But Dicker is not a sensitive person and he
lost no time in explaining that, if there was
going to be any resigning, somebody else
would have to do it. The rest of the picnic,
therefore, had to choose between carrying on
business as usual and “chucking” a job
which brings in pretty fair dividends with-
out causing anyone to sweat his collar limp.
At length someone made the interesting dis-
covery that there is nothing in the Act to
make the Committee sit at all if it doesn’t
want to, so proceedings were indefinitely
adjourned on the off-chance that Parliament
will do something at the beginning of next
session to bump Dicker from the pleasant
perch.
An obscure Heidelberg (Vic.) councillor
named May is reaching out for Frank
Anstey’s seat in the Reps. Membrey, who
represents the wit, beauty and intellect of
Northcote in the House of Peacock, was
asked to head the charge on Anstey’s en-
trenchements, but after looking round said
there was nothing doing. When an old poli-
tical warrior like Membrey shies at the pros-
pect of unseating Anstey, it is reasonable to
assume that May won’t be a merry month
for its namesake.
[Edmund Fisher, 369 Collins Street, Melbourne.]
“Edo”: Westralian Labor bosses have
stretched things so far that breaking-point
is near. This is a typical incident. The
manager of the Boulder Municipal electric-
light station with a view to economy dis-
placed a boy. Immediately two engine-
drivers stopped work because they had not
been consulted. To prevent a strike of
engine-drivers spreading all over Golden
Mile, perhaps the State, the two drivers and
the boy had to be reinstated, and a well-
intentioned manager had to eat coke—to
acknowledge, in fact, that he was not man-
ager. Nor is R. Hamilton of Great Boulder,
nor R. S. Black of the Kalgurli, nor ,T.
Darbyshire of the trans-Australian railway
line in W.A. These men and others like
them can only point out 'what work is to be
done. As to how and when and at what
price it shall be done they have no sav
worth mentioning. That is all done by the
real bosses, whose address is c/o the Trades
Hall.

“Canary Cottage,” which lasted hours
at Melbourne Majesty’s on Saturday, is a
feast of color and a frisk of folly wherein
the dressmaker reigns supreme. The musical
numbers come and go, accompanied by a
chorus, and every time the chorus comes it
brings a change of costume with it. But never
an item leaves a haunting memory behind.
The music is factory-made and the material is
much of a pleasant sameness. Some of it
will probably be cut out before these remarks
art published, and judicious reduction will
improve the effect.
* * * •
The festive proceedings hang upon a thin
line of narrative, into which some common-
place threads of human character are woven.
A young man named Jerry has a set formula
for making love, in prose or verse, to all
the girls who come within the gleam of his
front teeth, and who “never knew what Love
could do till first their lips met his.” Of
three girls in particular Dorothy Brunton
is Jerry’s very best and least unreasonable,
whereas the passion of Connie Ediss burns
like a devouring flame until her lawful hus-
band (Frith) becomes duly qualified to blow
it out. Connie’s liege lord has meanwhile
been an incidental dipso., suffering from
visible and unseen jim-jams. A number of
infant gnomes dog his footsteps in full view
or the audience, but the pink spiders and
things are visible only to his bleary mind’s
eye. Jerry’s other girl is a flirtatious flapper
who has a grim mommer (Marie Eaton) in
attendance. The chorus maids and men have
no connection with the story except as sup-
ports. Without them it would fall as flat as
a table shorn of its legs.
The brightest lines are about equally
divided between the Brunton and Ediss
ladies. Dorothy, charmingly humorous as
usual, addresses some of her most pointed
remarks to Maude Fane, who is sweet and
tuneful as the flapper. Connie Ediss makes
a broad success of the abundant matron
with the devouring fondness for Jerry, so
her lack of adequate singing power is a
minor consideration. Frith has the crudest
of comedy notions concerning the husband
who happens around in pyjamas and a joyful
smile, as though jim-jams were a joke; and
Fisher and Maguire are minor unavailing
exponents of a grotesque face and an Irish
accent respectively. William Greene is the
young man with the kiss that makes hungry
where it most doth satisfy ; but although his
style is brisk and unaffected, apd his sing-
ing is passable, lie hardly accounts for the
sustained adoration of girls who are always
finding him out. A Canary and Cat dance,
by Madge Elliott and Jack Hooker, presents
an exceptional pussy who dies of remorse
after biting the bird. Such cases are rare
iu domestic life.
