The skimpy little play that by the aid of
prolonged intervals is made to occupy the
whole of a short evening at Sydney Criterion
looks like reaching the flabby heart of the
female readers of the insipid sort of imported
fiction upon which it is founded. “Daddy
Long Legs” never strays for one instant from
the comfortable realms of stage convention.
Everything happens just as the audience
expects it to happen, except that it takes
such a long time to happen. The adopted
waif and the good, kind bachelor millionaire
with the sugary-sweet smile remind one of
the early days of the movies. Women who
haven’t more important to weep
over in these war times will enjoy the luxury
of an innocuous weep and a red nose in the
intervals between the intervals. The un-
mentioned author fails, however, to make the
title clear. Apparently Judy sees from a
window the shadow of the benevolent million-
aire so thrown upon a wall that the legs are
absurdly elongated : but all the audience sees
is a girl gazing out of a window. Surely it
would have been within the power of the
stage-manager to present this effect, import-
ant for the understanding of the story, to
the view of the audience? However, as long
as Kathlene MacDonell is on the stage noth-
ing else really matters.
When the horses had retired into the
wings and the musical elephant had played
himself to sleep on a mouth-organ tucked in
the extreme end of his trunk, the attendants
took up the matting on the floor of the ring
at Wirths’ New Hippodrome and laid a
silver trident in the centre. Then the orches-
tra away up in a box started to play “A
Hundred Fathoms Deep,’ 5 and the assembled
Sydneyites saw the great circular platform
sink, a couple of inches inside its concrete
barrier. There was a sound as if a giant
100 feet high had sucked in his breath, and
water gushed up all round the rim of the
sinking platform and met in a wave in the
centre. At the same time more water
gushed through hundreds of unguessed holes
in the sinking floor and soon there was a
great circular swimming pool, with waves
where horses had galloped and elephants
had posed. A clown fished from a hastily-
rigged spring-board and caught a live fox-
terrier. Then, of course, he fell in. From
now on all Wirths’ clowns will have to be
marine comedians. When the glistening
floor of the ring rose again and the water
drained away—all done in a couple of min-
utes—Neptune was standing on it. When he
trumpeted, eight tailless mermaids in black
TENOR PHILIP WILPON, of the N.P.W.
state Consercatorium, singing of the
weather and the birds.
neek-to-toe costumes and red caps appeared
and sank with him. Later they reappeared
in an undrowned condition and engaged in
fancy diving with “Professors” Cavill and
Scott. The latter dived twice from a plat-
form under the roof and for the first trip
Cavill told him in a loud voice that he hoped
God would be with him. Scott apparently
made the second trip unaccompanied.
* * * *
Bailey’s posing dogs and horses make an
artistic and well-lighted turn on the big stage
and the marble-white steed is excused for mov-
ing his ears. A horse must do that while his
brain works. The astonishing Riogoku troupe
of Jap acrobats, who throw one another about
with their feet, again appear; also Maximo,
the Wire Drunk, who sticks to the swaying
line as though he were a Morse message.
The seals with the educated noses did not
feel very spry on the opening night; but
Lindo’s team of young lions were fill there,
and the trainer with the whip was a figure
in a whirling ring of yellow when the lum-
bering comedy bear had made his exit and
the dangerous family did its final gallop.
The spotlight fell softly on the blushing-
elephant’s cheek and the red-coated “society
circus” turn was a great success—more par-
ticularly when one of the ladies making a
flying leap at a cantering horse landed in
the lap of a bronzed front-seater from the
bush. Some of the same front-row got well-
splashed when the engineers behind worked
their remarkable transformation and turned
the ring into a lake. A water-clown in search
of laughs kicks up the spray like a turning
liner. That sinking ring has cost the Wirths
a lot of money and trouble, but it is good
value. The only pity is that it can’t be
taken on tour.
Presently “The Pink Lady” will flash
round the corner from Sydney Majesty’s to
the Royal,, and so make way for the J.C.W.
panto. But she will be found out at Her
Majesty’s for a little while longer.
Seven weeks gone and “Very Good Eddie”
is still raking in the coin for the Taits at
Sydney Palace. The days of the cheerful
show are long in the land.
Business is brisk again at Sydney Tivoli
and the 1917 Follies are the responsible
parties. The new menu has 27 cream-puff
items on it, and the members of the co. who
are not engaged at the moment are away
changing their costumes. There are plenty
of striking and beautifully-eceentric dreams
in this year’s well-mounted show, and it is
plain that the revue girl is becoming heavily
wired. Sometimes she resembles an electric-
light installation ; at other moments she is an
imitation of an “art” lampshade. However,
they all work well and briskly together, and
are mixing more light burlesque with their
hurried songandance. Vera Pearce seems
to have lost none of her dash but a little of
her hard brilliancy. The black countenance
and quiet drawl of Walter Weems (gone
back to the States) are missed; but com-
edians Cannot, Welch and Rego manage to
get along without their old team-mate.
It's the scratch of the wolf at the box-
office that depresses the grittiest of mum-
mers ; and as the animal has not been seen
in the vicinity of the Darlinghurst house
nobody in the Smart Set is downhearted.
The Garden lias an attractiveness that is dis-
tinct ; the co.’s singing—particularly in the
concerted melodies —heals the ear after the
raucous politician has offended it, and the
fun-making is clean and not overdone. Per-
haps this week’s piogramme is the best that
the co. has offered in Sydney.

The ancient story of Ernani was narrated
with full musical embellishments by the
Gonsalez Co. at Melbourne. Princess’s on
Saturday. “Ernani” is one of Giuseppe
Verdi’s earliest works, and reveals his
juvenile enthusiasm for melodiously decorat-
ing the primitive emotions. The tenor and
baritone lovers of Elvira are two of the
sweetest products of Giuseppe’s glucose fac-
tory. Even Elvira’s malignant old uncle
delivers threats like packets of confectionery,
and the chorus hardly mentions a dagger or
a dungeon without wrapping the information
in jam. Considering the saccharinity of
the music, therefore, none of the three lovers
should be otherwise than dulcet in their testi-
monies of affection. Yet Dagradi, who sang
the name part on Saturday, passed out the
marmalade like a food dictator, and was
three inches of Fahrenheit below the
requisite warmth. In a manner of
speaking, he seemed too green to burn.
He should have listened carefully to
Scamuzzi (Don Carlos), whose utterance
was choked with beautifully-phrased endear-
ments. Elvira’s coldness to such a tender
suppliant was only permissible on the ground
that she had no ear for music. Scamuzzi
sang throughout with fine fervor and ren-
dered the solo parts of the third-act finale
in a fashion that caused an unusual dis-
turbance. Cacialli appeared as Elvira’s
ruthless uncle, Don Gomez de Siha, who has
matrimonial designs on his niece. He plots
with the chorus against his rival, Don Carlos,
in Charlemagne’s tomb, and tricks Ernani
into suicide in a most guileful manner. De
Siha’s wickedness was only faintly felt, how-
ever, for Cacialli’s comfortable bass and
casual style dissembled much of the Don’s
malevolence. Cacialli is much too large and
contented for the exposition of operatic
diabolism. His villains are mixtures of suet
and somnolence who are almost too tired to
show their teeth. Miss de Revers was fre-
quently a scream as Elvira, and ransacked
her previous impersonations, as it were, to
provide a dramatic outfit for the character.
The result was the usual convulsed spectacle
all the familiar gestures gone through like
a calisthenic exercise. The chorus formed
itself into semi-circles as before, and the
male part of it sang with great abandon. The
orchestra was also artistically responsive to "
the eloquent baton of Giovanni Gonsalez.
To-night (Thursday) “Gioconda” will be
repeated, and “Lucia” and “Carmen” are on
the programmes for to-morrow and Saturday.

The person who is led by popular talk and
press allusions to expect a flourish of
strumpets at Melbourne Royal continues to
be grievously disappointed. “Fair and
Warmer” is a farcical false-alarm wherein
all the innuendoes of feminine giddiness peter
out in harmless imbecilities. The demure
little wife of Jack Wheeler, who wants to be
compromised and consumes liquid rainbows
in a festive spirit, brings no blush to a Wes-
leyan cheek even when her deshabille is sug-
gestive of sultry weather. Likewise Mrs.
Billy Bartlett, who allows herself to be
caught in the embrace of the naughty Mr.
Evans, cheats the spectator of a prime Sas-
siety scandal. The spicy hopes that are
dashed by the two young wives are also
doomed to extinction by Tessie, the maid.
Tessie leads everyone to assume that the
head of the house is her victim ; but she only
amounts to a glad-eye display. Take it alto-
gether, “Fair and Warmer” ought to he sued
for breach of promise.
Something more than last week’s bill was
wanted at Melbourne Tivoli, and the
prayers of the audiences are already being
answered. Speaking of Saturday’s matinee,
when two of the advertised new arrivals
failed to arrive, The Bulletin would say
that the Takiness pair
of musical artists
fairly earned their
salary, whereas there
are others in the
vaudeville line of
business who ought
to be paid to go away.
Another competent re-
arrival is Clement
May, who gives
Dickens-character im-
personations and
knows how to stage
his turn. Malcolm
McEachern is vocalis-
ing with distinction
as before, and the
popular Bluett is delivering a parody-song
which partly atones for the inanity of his
other ditties. The arrival of Ada Reeve is
said to be imminent.
At Melbourne King’s the inordinate sweet-
ness of Mrs. Bascom’s nature continues to
glorify her peach preserves and purify her
human surroundings. Even Deacon .lillin-
ger, the hard-shell hypocrite of Peachville,
seems to Turn to the Right in the last act
under the prevailing influence of Mrs. Bas-
com’s goodness. The Deacon had known her
for some time before Muggs, Gilly and Joe
blew into the village, yet he remained wicked.
Apparently she was not a “carrier” of her
own virtues; but when she had imparted
reformation to the wandering crooks the
1 >eacon caught the prevailing epidemic and
became an angelic wowser, more or less. The
Taits’ farcical sentimental sermon threatens
to last over Easter. In the field of moral
endeavor no competitor can shake a stick at
"Turn to the Right.”

Pastor White continues to preside over the
harmonium at Melbourne Auditorium, and
his choir are still lifting > p their voices in
Darktown rejoicing. The Fisk Singers
change their programme once a week and
warble of many things. So far. however,
they have made no . reference to water-
melons or chickens, and that, thank de
Loard, am somethin’ to be grateful foah.

Mae Marsh and Robert Harr on, who
were the hero and heroine in the modern
Amurkan section of “Intolerance,” lately
screened at Melbourne Royal, are facing one
another in “The Wharf Rat,” a Triangle
feature at Melbourne Majestic this week.
In the Triangle story, “The Missing Links,”
Harron is associated with Norma Talmadge,
the expressive little actress who was the
Mountain Girl in the Babylonian portion of
the Griffiths extravaganza. Next Saturday
the Majestic people promise another chapter
of “Gloria’s Romance,”- a much advertised
pictorial salad in which Billie Burke sup-
plies the dressing.

“Avatar,” which Hoyt’s are unrolling in
Melbourne this week, comes from the Cines
Co., of Rome, and introduces theories about
soul-transmission in a picturesque and com-
plicated plot. The star of the film is a new
Italian screen-player, Amleto Novelli, while
a Polish heroine, Soava Gallone, is an almost
equally glittering constellation. Harold Lock-
wood and May Allison are the two big names
in the latest Metro fabrication, “PidgiD
Island.” The story deals with smuggling on
the St. Lawrence River, but the excitement
is never strenuous enough to keep Harold
and May from canoodling before the best of
the available backgrounds.

The Williamson pantomime has had a
fortnight of heavy booking at the Adelaide
Royal. Stigant and Roxbury proved a
sound laugh-getting partnership, and Full-
brook got in some irrelevant but welcome
bits of “straight” acting. The ladies are
a good team, the kiddies delightful, and the
whole show merry. Manager Myers prom-
ises three weeks of Italian opera after
Easter. Teg and the Pup are just coming
into the little King’s, reopened in honor of
their visit. Into the programme at the
Four seconds on stage with a Grand Opera Prima Donna.
Majestic, very strong on the vocal side, the
Flemmings put variety by their clean athletic
work. Carrie Moore is most attractive
when serious, least so when she proceeds
on Daisy Jerome lines. The Strollers are
at the Tivoli. As of old, Sydney James
and Madeline - Rossiter are the two really
magnetic personalities; hut Desmond and
Gwen Lewis and Connie Milne all do .good
work. On the screen Billy Burke is at
West’s (also the eternal Chaplin), Betty
Nansen at Wondergraph, Clara K. Young
at the Pav.
Perhaps the most cantankerous vessel on
the Australian coast was the collier Yamba-
coona, whose demise was inquired into
recently by N. S. Wales Marine Court. She
used to ply in Tasmanian waters until she
was bought a few vears ago for the New-
castle-Sydney trade. Apparently disgusted
with the change, the ’Coona has bucked
against things ever since. On two occasions
she collided, nearly fatally, with vessels
much larger than herself; and 12 months
ago she flung herself right on to Big Ben,
witli Nobby’s Lighthouse staring at her in
astonishment. This was at the time put
down to a German being her skipper, but
the performance appears to have been just
cussedness, for a few weeks ago she ran
full butt in broad daylight upon fne Skillion,
the biggest and boldest headland between
Port Jackson and the Equator. She sank
like a stone and the Marine Court came to
the conclusion that she was no lady and
scratched her off its visiting list for good.
Just before leaving for the A.N.A. confer-
ence in the Mallee, chief-president Albert
Ostrom finished up his year’s service with
the association by joining the A.I.F. Ostrom
is from Echuca, where he runs a boot-store
and sits in the borough council.
The bulk result of a general election is
never to be trusted as an answer to any
specific question. Here and there an elec-
torate is bound to go its own way. For
example, in Gordoij, the seat long held by
N. S. Wales Liberal leader Wade, there
were three candidates —two front-rank law-
yers, Loxton and Bavin, and a third man
of the “good local member” type. Bavin
carried the “National” endorsement. But
in Gordon the woman vote is very strong ;
and the woman vote went very solidly for
Loxton because he is an uncompromising
six-o’clocker-for-all-time —or until he can
make it 5 o’clock most days and 1 o’clock
on Saturdays. Bavin would only go this far
—he would consent to ask the people after
the war whether they wanted six o’clock
pub-closing to continue. That lifted Bavin’s
pot on. As a woman put it frankly at one
of Loxton’s last metings: “I don’t under-
stand politics, but I do understand pubs, and
the man who satisfies me with regard to the
question I understand is going to have my
vote.”

MAORILAND. LESLIE R. FREEMAN.
Off the road at last—Fred Duval, for
many years one of the best known visitors
to newspaper offices, hotels, railway stations
and wharves this side of the line. In the
long ago he rushed around most of the world
with circuses—went once with a big outfit
from N’York to Brazil and only three got
back, of whom Agratti, afterwards in Aus-
tralia with Chiarini, was one and Duval
another. Then he joined up with concert
cos. and marionettes. He first struck the
Commonwealth with the Soldene Opera Co.,
was subsequently with Dunning and the Cog-
liills, then became lessee of the Princess
Theatre at Brisbane, and spent nearly 10
years as advance man for Tom Pollard.
After the Pollards petered out he organised
an opera co. for George Stephenson, and
finally was hack with Pollard again in a
picture-show in Maoriland. His home had
been where he hung his hat since he was 10
years old. He finished at GB.
Thelma Raye, the red-haired English
actress who is sub-queen of the Tivoli Fol-
lies, married a whole jewellery business last
week. The bridegroom is Private Percy
Dawson, eldest son of a Sydney jeweller,
and when he goes to the Front presently his
wife will resume at the footlights. Hugh
Mclntosh acted as the generous father for
the occasion.
Peace has been declared in one war, at
any rate —that between the Adelaide amuse-
ment managers and the Advertiser. * It
has been waged all this year, ever since the
paper declined to join in the outcry against
an amusement tax. Some common-sense
influence at last got to work, and now the
’Tiser has'theatre ads. and the theatres no
longer screen appeals to the public to look
to the Register and Herald only.
“0.K.”: The recent conflict of picture
showmen was a short, sharp, decisive affair.
It started with a Note from the Associated
Theatres to the Co-operative Film Exchange,
threatening to withdraw their custom if the
Co-op. continued to supply films to Hoyt’s,
who, b.vjbe way, are large customers. The
idea, apparently, was to kill two birds with
one stone, for the Associated Theatres run
Film Exchanges of their own in competition
with the Co-operative Exchange, which
looks to Hoyt’s for a lot of its business.
Hoyt’s defeated the enemy at small expense.
It went at once to the Federal Crown Law
Department, explained the nature and reason
of the threatened boycott, and got an opinion
that, in attempting to prevent Hoyt’s from
paying the amusement tax instead of charg-
ing it to the public, the Associated show-
men were hampering the collection of war
taxes. Armed with this opinion Hoyt’s
manager proceeded to Sydney and gave the
enemy three hours in which to withdraw the
threat of boycott. It withdrew all right.
The voice of '£ i he Bulletin’s old friend
Eugenie Duggan is being heard again in Vic-
toria. She and brother Ned are doing a
country tour in a repertoire of old and popu-
lar plays. One of them is “Sunday,” wherein
Tilda Brune said “Hell!” with great empha-
sis in days gone by. Another is “My Mate,”
in the preparation whereof Ned is alleged to
have collaborated to the extent of supplying
some of the curses.
“C. Ockney” : I agree with “Cockaigne’s”
estimate of Tree (B. 15/8/T7) in the main ;
yet if the English mummer only shows Aus-
tralians his version of Falstaff, Malvolio.
Shyloek and Svengali, his visit will be worth
while. His Hamlet may be (as Gilbert said)
“funny without being vulgar,” but in his
true line of business his art is always worth
watching and his “production” unapproach-
able. Also he is a fine comedian .off the
stage.
“Cigarette”: During Wilkie’s 18 weeks’
season in Melbourne he staged six Shaks-
pearian plays, three classic comedies and
one hybrid (“The Bells”). This should be
a record for Australia, anyhow.
“Box Office”: On the charge against
Allan Wilkie of gagging in standard plays,
I go on the witness-stand with a 50-year-
old prompt book of Sheridan’s “School for
Scandal.” It has the identical gags used by
Wilkie in Melbourne. As for the gags put
into “She Stoops to Conquer,” I reckon
that they are the syrup served with an ex-
cellent suet pudding to make it palatable.
“Stargazer”: “Fair and Warmer” provides
a startlingly impressive lesson for producers
and players, warning them of comic inebria-
tion if they haven’t the capacity to deliver
the goods. Comic inebriation calls for very
capable acting. The man (and particularly
the woman) who starts out to be comically
intoxicated on the stage in the fierce white
limelight must have observed the humor-
some drunk frequently at close range and
with minute inspection and deep sympathy.
On top of that, he/she must possess an un-
common capacity for reproducing the quaint
effects while cautiously eliminating those
that are only provocative of moral distress.
In many years I have seen few men who
could be humorously and inoffensively drunk
on the boards, and but two women. Most of
the men were vaudeville specialists; so was
one of the women. In “Fair and Warmer”
the male ineb. has no more idea of what’s
wanted than a virtuous recluse who has
been reared by his 10 aunts; and the young
lady’s jag is hard and unseemly. Her move-
ments suggest the peregrinations of a sea-
sick spinster on the roughest pig-rooter that
ever hopped the waters, and her talk is a
spurious counterfeit that should never he
uttered. The amateurishness of the whole
business throws an effect of unripeness over
the show.
“V.P.”: The Dublin Abbey Theatre has
crashed upon a rock at last. A number of
the chief players, including Arthur Sinclair,
have struck against the management and
decided to form a co. of their own. Since
the days of the Fays, Maire O’Neill and Sara
Allgood the Abbey has had to weather some
bad storms, but this is the dead finish. Sin-
clair, who is to run the rival show, has been
the backbone of the Abbey for so long that a
performance without him will seem like a
wake without whisky. The trouble appar-
ently arose from the appointment of St.
John Ervine, the dramatist, as manager.
“Circle”: When is Australia going to
have open-air dancers? California (U.S.A.)
is quite used to them by now, and they frolic
barefooted in the East when the weather is
suitable —and sometimes when it isn’t. The
fine running Dance of the Nymphs in “The
Pink Lady” looks as if it had been inspired
by the classical cavorting of the graceful
open-air girls, who, because they dance on
the lawn or the sands, cannot indulge in any
machine-like toe-twiddlings. .The J.C.W.
dancers perform as part of a good indoor
show ; hut they could just as easily show on
a suburban lawn at a fete or on the smoothed
ocean sands. For open-air work one thing is
essential: the band musF be big enough to
make everybody hear the dance music. The
capers and attitudes of an unaccompanied
dancer are the silliest things in the world.
Remember the dancing ladies on the screen,
or the small Scotch kids leaping to the jingle
of an inaudible piano at the St. Andrew’s
Day revels?
“First Old Man”: The exclamatory play
title featuring the name of one of the princi-
pal characters is another tiling we may
thank Amurka for —if we feel like it. We
have had “So Long, Letty,” and “Very Good
Eddie,” and soon we are to sample Marie
Tempest in something equally scare-heady—
“ Good Gracious, Arabella!” There’s no
limit to this kind of cheap nomenclature,
though the Yankee playwright who chris-
tened his first piece “Believe Me, Xantippe,”
may find that effort difficult to live up to.
English authors are not doing much work
during the war, or no doubt they would be
in the movement. What’s wrong with
“What Cheer, ’Ria!” by Pinero, “Skip the
Gutter, Ermyntrude!” by Henry Arthur
.Tones; or “Great Heavens, Adolphus!” by
Barrie.

During the A.N.A. conference at Iverang
lawyer George Moir, who spends a lot of his
spare time turning his eager face to the
Liberal sun, introduced a motion favoring
“the creation of an Imperial Parliament to
deal with foreign affairs and defence.” Moir
explained, somewhat ingenuously, that the
Imperial Parliament would be “two-thirds
Motherland.” and the Australia!! representa-
tion would carry with it certain taxation
responsibilities. The mover having thus
exposed the claws of the cat. the motion
received the clammy hand, and was presently
withdrawn in favor of an amendment, duly
carried with hardly a dissentient, giving
gentle approval to the Imperial Conference
at which (alas, poor William!) Australia is
not represented.
At the beginning of this year the can-
tankerous moulders of N. S. Wales declared
peace, and went back to work after a long
strike, having gained a wage increase that
will enable each man within a few years to
make good what he lost during the disturb-
ance. But that doesn’t clear up all the
ruins. In November last the Sydney trouble
brought the Melbourne moulders out, and
they are still there. The Melbourne men
demanded an assurance from the employers
that the castings they were employed upon
were not intended for blockaded N. S.
Wales. The employers refused to give it,
and work was stopped. There is no reason
now why the Victorians should fight on or
loaf off, but they’re still at it. It is on a
matter of principle, of course. Some of these
unionists are sturdy upholders of every prin-
ciple except respect for Arbitration.

“WAR IS HELL."
“ Don’t talk to me of your bally war. I’m
full! Why, since it’s been on, do you
know there’s been times when I didn’t
know where my next motor-car was
co[?]ing from!”
“Chal” : Let me recommend this scheme to
the Nark and other States. A few months
ago the South Aus. Government announced
that soldiers’ relatives and friends resident
in Britain would be promptly advised of
casualties that interested them. The names
and addresses of S. Aus. soldiers were called
for, together with a list of Billjim’s connec-
tions in Britain. The response was imme-
diate. The working of the scheme is sim-
plicity itself. The names and addresses
received are forwarded periodically to the
Agent-General, who indexes the information,
and immediately on the issue of a casualty
list by A.I.F. Headquarters, London, com-
pares it with the index. Should No.
9999, Private Bill Croweater, 10th Bat-
talion, be wounded, his fianc€e, Nurse Bill-
jimima, of the Australian General Hospital,
and his uncle, Mr. John Croweater. of Tot-
tenham, are at once advised that Bill is in
such and such a hospital. The need for
such a scheme was suggested by the number
of cables which were dispatched from South
Aus. advising people in the old land of
casualties to fighting men.
* * * *
“Trooper Iv.”: It was during the New
Year battle with the Turks near Raffa. An
Australian Light Horse regiment was ordered
to get round the enemy position and
prepare to make a rear attack. This en-
tailed an all-night ride and crossing the
border into Palestine. Having arrived at
the position we had to await the frontal
attack by the Yeomanry. The sun was just
clear of the horizon and stray shells were be-
ginning to burst among us, but every man
was too dead tired to take much notice. To
keep awake a number of the chaps started a
two-up school. The Colonel discovered it
just as a bet was being negotiated. “Look
at those he yelled, “not two minutes
in the Holy Land, Death staring ’em in the
face, and it’s ‘heads a pound.’ ” And turn-
ing to one of his officers, he added hastily :
“And it’s a quid he does ’em too !”

“8.C.”: How their thoughts turn home-
ward ! I was marching down London Strand
when a desperate taxi-driver called me to
the kerb. Jerking his thumb at a sleeping
heap of uniform inside, “ ’Ere,” he said,
“ you’re an Orstralian, ain’t you? Well,
would you mind arsking that mate o’ yours
where ’e wants to go to? I can’t make out
th’ place.” I shook up the uniform and
called, “Here, wake up, and tell the driver
where you want to go!” One bleary eye
opened. “Flin’ers-street stash’n!” growled
the wandering Melbourne boy, and fell
asleep again.

“8.T.”: This true tale explains how the
wiles of the wicked may come to naught,
and fn what manner the deceitful are caught
in their own nets. A well-known musician,
living on the outskirts of Melbourne, a
while ago dismissed the Bloods, a married
couple who hgd been with him for several
months. The musical Bohemian had become
annoyed at length by the persistent drunken-
ness of the pair. They had presumed on his
leniency in permitting them to go to bed
drunk so far as to make
it customary to get up
drunk. On the third
night following the dis-
missal, Beethoven was
bumped from his slum-
bers by a crash in an
adjoining room. Snatch-
ing up a two-foot brass
candlestick, and rushing
to the defence of his pos-
sessions, he stumbled on
an inexplicable situation.
Lying on the floor, with
a nasty bleeding dent in
his head, was the dis-
missed male Blood. By
him was a small sack
crammed with many of
Beethoven’s most cher-
ished belongings. By the
sack was a brick, a
corner •of which pro-
vided a perfect fit for the
roomy dent in Blood’s
cranium. One of the
front windows was
smashed. Blood had been
knocked out by the brick,
hurled by an unknown
hand, while seeking to
avenge himself on Bee-
thoven by prigging the
good musician’s silver.
Bill received a long sen-
tence. No allowance
was made for his rotten
luck. For the brick which
crippled Bill was thrown
through the dining-room window by Bill’s
missus, who,'being on an independent burst,
also sought to avenge the wrongs of the
Bloods in lamentable ignorance of the fact
that her Bill was within, burgling the side-
board.
“The Pariah”: No Europeans in China
have any hankering for the services of Dr.
Peng. The “Chinese doctor” requires no quali-
fications, no study and no stock-in-trade save
a couple of needles for acupuncture. Often he
becomes a doctor because he is no good for
anything else. Those who know their busi-
ness at all have some good drugs ; but they
use them in such quantities that after a dose
the patient has a bulge on his waistline.
Moreover, Pong’s choice of a drug depends
upon a weird diagnosis; so the victim is
lucky if the overdose he is sure to get is
of the right stuff. In addition to good
drugs, Pong is very fond of administering
powdered moths, silkworms and human ex-
cretions and secretions, hartshorn shavings
and anything, almost, that is disgusting and
nauseous. About eight years ago, at San-
choinhien, I was taken to a yellow medico
unconscious, as the result of a kick. I woke
up In what-I thought was Hell, very dim
and smelly, with a yellow demon, looking
owlishly wise behind the two-inch dark-glass
lens of his spectacles, just ready to acu-
puncture me for the second time with a hot
needle. Strict legislation should protect
trusting fools and their unfortunate children
against these quacks. I protected myself
with my feet.

“Padre” : Last niglit it was cold and foggy,
and we were crouched round the stove play-

“Corio” : Two more Anzacs in the making.
When the schooner Korora finished up off
the Camden Haven Bight (Northern N.S.W.)
the crew took to the boats and tried to land
on a shelving beach within view of the light-
house at Tacking Point. But the heavy seas
off-shore continually washed them back, and
when a lifebuoy attached to lines was
lowered, an undercurrent made it useless.
Then two lads in the knickerbocker stage,
buckling on lifelines, swam out through the
growling seas to the seamen. Considering
that this feat was undertaken at night-time
during incessant rain and on an ugly coast
with an evil name, it was worth the bronze
medal the Royal Humane Society is award-
ing. The winners got their men ashore safely.
The too-sudden infant got a further set-
back lately, when the High Court decided
that Arthur Hall, of Kerang (Vic.), was
entitled to a decree nisi from his wife, for
desertion. It appeared that Mrs. Hall,
shortly after her marriage,, told Arthur that
she was about to make someone else a happy
father. She thereupon retired to her parents’
home, and, after her little adventure, Hall
desired her to return to him, but not to
bring the infant. She declined. Justice
Cussen held that this did not constitute
desertion, and that, as mother and child
should not be separated, Hall should have
taken the lady back with all or any encum-
brances. The Vic. Full Court, on appeal,
said learned Brother Cussen was right; but
five High Court Judges unanimously said
“Nonsense,” or words to that effect. On the
whole, it seems best that way. For one
thing, if a man were compelled to keep
another man’s child or lose his wife, the in-
fant would have a rough time in most cases.
From Gerard Williams, who came this
way to look at Australian mines on behalf
of London Times, and is now with the Royal
Engineers:—
So Australians turned down “Yes”! Well, I
always did maintain that a democracy was the
least democratic of all Governments; but 1 am
sorry that Australia should confirm the idea'. Yet
how could you, how could anyone, expect to get
a real vote when every fit, able-bodied coward was
allowed to vote for his own exemption, and every
woman who has an idle son or a shirking “best boy”
was allowed to vote for his continuance in
cowardice!
In any case, the odds were against you—distance,
isolation, freedom from the hell of war: what did
your people really know of the women savaged, chil-
dren raped, homes destroyed—for Kultur? England
knows more about these things; yet, believe me,
in England (and I am English), if we had been set
to the same task of getting universal service by an
appeal to the people, we should have failed.
How short our memories are! Look at France!
Schooled in ’7O, yet forgetful in ’l4. But what a
wondrously glorious France she is to-day: a France
we who had often “done” Paris, toured Touraine
and wintered in Brittany never knew —won-
derful, glorious, reborn! A few weeks ago my
division came back to a rest area. I wandered into
he knew.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Across the twilit chancel the fitful tapers gleam,
Half-veiling - , half-revealing her majesty supreme,
White star of godless ages above a world defiled,
Thrice-blessed Marv, Mother of Christ the llolv
Child.
O matres dolorosa the hot-red road.is wet
With sweat of your travailing, with tears of your
regret!
On you the outcast stigma, the curse, the gibe, the
frown—■
O mothers of the women, the women of the town!
The city flaunts her splendor across the night of
Fate,
Her heart the heart of Judas, her smile the smile of
hate ;
Bed, blood-red are her altars, your tears have told
the cost,
0 matres dolorosa, O mothers of the lost!
Like ripe grain to the threshing forth speed the boys
ye wean
Lest bayonets fail their fleshing, or lag- the war-
machine.
The valley of the shadow ye rode for them in vain,
O matres dolorosa; —O mothers of the slain!
O’er half a world's black chaos the battle bale-fires
gleam,
Half-veiling, half-revealing her agony supreme—
Not Mary, queen of mothers, who bare the Holy
Child,
But Mary, queen of martyrs—bowed, broken, world-
defiled.
VICTORIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.

“4240”: It was in the advanced dressing
station at what onr fellows call Bernafay
Wood—l don't know whether it’s on the
map like that. One hard-doer came in hor-
ribly smashed about the face. “They’ve had
a good go at me. Doc.,” he whispered when
he came round. “You’re right, lad —they
have,” said the doc. “But I beat ’em for

in me pocket!”
“Didymus” explains how we economise:—
Melbourne G.P.O. takes, its *hat off to the Depart-
ment’s latest scheme of “efficiency and economy.”
Some genius has got to work on a new system of
paying the telegraphists.
Under the old system a man signed the salary-
sheet, received a slip on which was written his name
and the amount due to him, presented the slip to
the paying officer about 2ft. distant and received his
money. This system was. quick and reduced the
liability to error to vanishing point. In branches
where various shifts are worked the paymaster and
his assistant, starting at about 10.30 a.m., had all
the morning staff paid by about 11.30 a.m. or noon,
and the afternoon and evening staffs occupied from
about 2.30 p.m. till 4. Meantime he had some
chance of attending to his ordinary duties, he being
also officer in charge of the receiving-room.
Hut that was much too simple for the “efficiency”
cranks; so now the ceremony has been • made far
more impressive. After receiving the money from
the bank, the paymaster and his assistant retire to a
secluded roost at the top of the G.P.O. There they
find, arranged in rows in a wooden frame-work, a
number of small tins, about 14 inch each way.
These tins fit tightly into the shelves. The tins are
numbered from 1 to about 450. The paymaster has the
paysheet on which are the names, the amounts due,
and the numbers of the staff. He calls out “J.
Smith, £lO, No. 15,” counts out the tenner from the
bulk supply, hands it to his assistant, who counts it
again, selects tin No. 15 and pots the dough. lie
then returns the tin to the shelf. This process takes
the two of them all the time that it formerly took
to pay the morning staff. They retire to lunch.
About 2.30 p.m. the framework —convoyed by a
guard—is taken down to the telegraph floor. Mean-
while the payee has not been idle. He has put in
time obtaining from his supervisor a slip of paper
with his name, number and amount due typed there-
on. He has to sign this slip and present it to the
paymaster. The paymaster then selects the corres-
ponding tin and levers it out with some instrument.
One of these days the paymaster will cut loose
and hammer those tins flat. Then the “efficiency”
crank will have to give us our money in fish-kettles.

For the national welfare another long-
distance jaunt was undertaken last week in
Victoria, when 250 A.N.A. delegates trav-
elled by special train clean across the State
to the Mallee. This year’s A.N.A. conference,
which began oh Tuesday, is being held at
Kerang, a hamlet in the midst of a large
irrigation area through which the Loddon
strolls down to the Murray. The quarter-
thousand gay nation-builders carried with
them a heavy load of politicians of big
calibre, plenty of smokes, eats, drinks, packs
of cards and prepared speeches, and 250
light hearts and carefree souls. No doubt
‘■bis sort of picnic is a highly beneficial busi-
ness for the Kerang pub-keepers and quick-
shavers, and a perfect godsend to the local
garage boss. But the present hardly seems a
suitable time for spending a lot of money,
either private or public, on travelling 180
miles to give the Australian Parliaments a
lead. A dozen accredited A.N.A. delegates
meeting in the cloak-room of a suburban
town-hall could probably perform a national
service of equal value.
“Montalex” : Stripped of legendary glamor
and robbed of all the glories of the days of
I laroun-al-Raschid, Dar-es-Selam (“Abode
of Peace,” as Bagdad is styled in Turkish
official documents) is to-day about the last
word in sordid and unromantic squalor. The
only water supply is what is carried from
the Tigris, and sewerage there is none ; hence
the vigor and variety of the stenches evolved
from a crowd of 180,000 badly washed
people, in a climate that gives often a mid-
day shade temperature of 120 deg. F. The
“streets” are a labyrinthine tangle of narrow
and filthy lanes, margined by a monotony of
grimy walls, unbroken save for here and
there a 'mean little doorway. Even the
bazaars, are dirty and dilapidated, though
they are kaleidoscopic with the costumes of
Turks, Persians. Armenians, Arabs, Kurds,
Afghans and Indians. The city is built of
bricks, mostly recovered from the abundant
prehistoric ruins of the neighborhood. But
the distant poets still rhyme fondly about
the old dust-heap. Having personally smelt
Bagdad (the poets haven’t), I have no sen-
timent to waste on it.
When will Labor leaders do their duty
and talk plain Australian to those fool-
followers of theirs who punish employers
for doing something more than they have to?
The latest case happened in Melbourne. Sar-
gents had been paying their waitresses more
than the standard: and when the Wages
Board raised the rates —largely because Sar-
gents had been paying 1 more —the damsels
conceived the idea that the firm ought to go
on paying extra. So there was a strike;
and the firm’s business was upended for a
few days. What sort of encouragement is
this for 'firms to pay more than an award
prescribes even when they think that the
award is less than a fair thing?

What a valuable collection of hints might
be got if every Judge was required, after 10
years of service, to write for Parliament a
report, reviewing his work in a judicial spirit,
with an eye to the removal of legal anom-
alies and injustices! Now and again a
Judge feels that he simply has to let himself
go—especially when he is a very human
Judge like Gordon, of S. Aus. He had a
young man in front of him the other day
for assault on a child of 10; and, full of
horror, he moralised on the “glaring incon-
sistency” of laws which told him that he
must not give more than two years for an
offence of this kind, yet permitted him to
inflict a life sentence for the forgery of a
cheque for a shilling.
* * * *
Britain’s moderately 7 new idea in letter-
addressing, evolved to overcome the shortage
of postal labor, looks the sort of thing that
may stay. As yet the system has been
applied only to London ; but it could easily be
extended to other large cities, and that is only
where it is needed. Briefly, the idea is the
numberinsr of districts, which loads to a sim-
plification of letter-sorting, and does away
with delays while sorters consider what a
particularly undecipherable or vague address
means. A letter directed to, say, dis-
trict “12” goes in with other letters for
district 12, and anybody can be put on to the
rough sorting of letters addressed in that
fashion. Though the system is quite new a
large proportion of London letters are
already numbered, and thus almost sort them-
selves, leaving the depleted staff more time
to handle the balance that are addressed in
the old fashion.

In a commonplace Melbourne divorce case,
which' the Win-the-War press reported as
though it were a matter of grave national
importance, the husband stated that his
wife’s parents desired her to get a divorce
because, “under existing conditions, they could
never take her to Government House.” Of
course, that settled it. When the bonds of
matrimony restrain a lady from getting to
Government House they have plainly ceased
to be holy, and ought to be severed.
“A..T.8.” : The Chinese herbalist who tried
to cure a dreadfully-burned child with honey
and liquorice powder (B. 18//3/17) belongs
to a very privileged class. The Boards of
Health in the various States are emphatic
that even inspectors of nuisances should be
certificated. Yet they allow the herbalists to
practise and dispense medicine without any
qualification. Provided they keep clear of
the drugs prescribed under the Poisons Acts
they can go till Doomsday. Some of them
have passed the chemists’ • examination,
which entitles them to dispense any drug,
but they don’t bother to trade as ordinary
pill-pounders —that would loosen their hold
on the ignorant and morbid who love the
mystery that clings to the word “herbalist.”
I can’t believe that such dreadful ignorance
prevails in regard to anything else. For
example, what bricklayer, seeking for jobs
at bricklaying, would find it profitable to
hide the fact that he had “served his time”
learning the trade? Yet actually there are
people who will only hire a doctor provided
he can convince them that he isn’t qualified.
“Vemi Dominie”: Far be it from me to
ferment a yeasty argument on the merits of
the “religious instruction” imparted to
State School children by duly authorised
persons; but I think the privilege should be
reserved for persons of some sympathy and
intelligence. I am sitting in my school siz-
zling in silent protest at the stuff that is
being served out to my youngsters at this
moment. They are the overflow of a mining
town, and as primordial as chipped flint.
Their soul-needs require special understand-
ing. But he (or It) is a large oleaginous
mass, who ignores the essential thing and
fails to grasp all the welter of values in the
situation. He begins heavily like a beaten
tom-tom: “Next Sunday is Septuagesima
Sunday.” The kids look politely uninter-
ested. “I shall read the Collect for that
Sunday.” When he has finished they sigh
soullessly. He proceeds: “The lesson for
to-day is taken from the umptieth chapter of
Deuteronomy, beginning at the nth verse.”
Then follows a dusky discourse, and the
young miner listens with his tongue in both
cheeks—or mostly doesn’t listen. Outside,
the earth is newly washed, the sky is deli-
cate with clean greys and blues, the sun sifts
through the majestic blaze of a bougain-
villaea, and the air is heavy with the perfume
and the passionate life of everything. And
all the gods that are are Alive and Articulate
and Insistent. But this man, with his
gabble, makes of God a dead thing. And so
lie works out his half-hour, copfuses their
young souls with his jargon, and signs the
visitors’ book. I hereby place it to his
Debit Account.
THE FIRST TALKING-MACHINE.
“Wriggs”: Blame it on to our school-
masters who beat “The Village Blacksmith”
into us when we were young. Naturally, when
we were marched to the ancient village of
Figheldean, on Salisbury Plain (Eng.), and
saw the famous old spreading chestnut tree
leafless before us, we decided to knife a slip
of bark for the old folks at home. But so
many of us started that to-day wire-netting
surrounds it, while a notice remarks that
anyone further damaging the tree will be
prosecuted. Feeling sick and discouraged, we
trooped over the way to the church where
the Village Blacksmith heard his daughter
sing. I suppose we took our penknives with
us. What do vou think they did then?
Threatened to close the church to us! In
England they hate peonle who want to show
how much they appreciate poetry.

NEUTRALISES STOMACH ACID, OR
MONEY BACIv.
Recently a well-known - authority on the
treatment of stomach troubles, who has
claimed that practically all stomach trouble
is due to acidity, decided to put his theory
to the test. Every sufferer from stomach
trouble was told to take half a teaspoonful
of hixiirated magnesia in a little water
immediately after eating or whenever pain
was felt. The test completely vindicated his
theory, for relief was indicated in each of
the first hundred reports received, showing
conclusively that the trouble had been due to
stomach acid which, as is well known by
physicians, is instantly neutralised by bisu-
rated magnesia. Since making known this
test, the well-known chemists who have
specialised in preparing bis urn ted magnesia
exclusively for stomach use have arranged to
place in every package of genuine bisurated
magnesia a binding guarantee contract of
satisfaction or # money back —proof positive
of their belief' that nearly every so-called
dyspeptic is really suffering from acidity—
and their confidence that genuine bisurated
magnesia will instantly neutralise stomach
arid and stop food fermentation. Always
look for the name “Bisurated” on the label
and the guarantee contract inside the pack
nge.—Aim’.

State Commandant (Vic.).
The first officer to join up with the new
Australian Army Reserve, constituted as “a
national insurance for the future peace of
Australia,” was I.ieut. Arthur Ilenty. He
is a grandson of the late James Ilenty, who
founded the ancient Melbourne firm which
still bears his name. .Tames was one of the
Portland brotherhood which, a long neck in
front of Batman and Fawkner, first began
to populate what later on became the State
of Victoria.
Unless Edden can recover on a second
ballot there will not be left one survivor of
the first Labor brigade in the N. S. Wales
Parliament. McGowen’s personal popularity
couldn’t save him in Redfern; canny Gann,
of Broken Hill, saw the writing on the wall
and crept into a Railway Commissionership
a few months ago; big-hearted, rugged Alf
Edden, of Kahibah, finds himself at last
second on the poll; and so does J. B. Nichol-
son, .of Wollongong. All these got into
Parliament on the ’9l wave, and had been
there ever since—over 100 years of it among
the four of them.

WHAT DTD SHE EXPECT?
“How is Mr. Dauber getting on with your
portrait, dear ?”
"Oh, I’m not going to sit for him again.”
"Why? Is he too slow?"
"Slow! I should think he was. He wants to
just go on painting all the time."
A youth named Thomas Day, of Mount
Gambier (S.A.), anxious to enlist and hav-
ing a preference for a Melbourne depot,
started to walk there. lie did about 50
miles on foot, then struck a job as a drover.
After travelling with the sheen for three
days he had earned enough to pay for a
second-class ticket by rail from Serviceton.
Arrived in Melbourne, he made a beeline
for the Town Hall, and offered himself as a
recruit. The story ought to end with a
V.C. But the plot went wrong, ard the
gritty youngster failed to pass the doctor.
It wasn’t his heart that was wrong, though.
This has been a bad year for the Craw-
fords. There were two brothers, both of
them with brains and little else to start off
with. They both worked' hard. One got
into Parliament in Queensland, read law.
and was in practice in Clermont when the
recent flood arrived. He was drowned. The
other, taking to the Church, got his M.A. :
then he, too, was bitten by the political
insect, left the. Church, joined the Labor
party, got a seat in the N.SAV. Parliament,
and was presently admitted as a barrister.
On Saturday he was beaten at Marrickville :
and though there were four candidates the
P.L.L. man had such a lend that Crawford
hasn’t even the satisfaction of a second
ballot.
. An interesting sidelight is thrown on the
late Chief Justice Way, of S. Aus., by., the
settlement of a local four-year-old lawsuit.
The trouble was between some quarry people
and the nearest house-owner. Both sides
had money, there were complicated points of
Nuisance and Trespass, and the old Chief,
with the caution which became almost an
obsession in his latest days, “reserved” his
judgment. He had never, in all his long
career, had one upset on appeal. Appeal was
prettv certain in this case, whichever way lie
gave it; and he was tortured by the thought
that something might happen to spoil his
record. Hence he reserved judgment, and
went on reserving ■it month after month.
Judgment was still “reserved” when he died,
so the litigants found themselves faced with
the necessity of going through the costly
trial all over again. After thinking about
it for a year, one of them sensibly sold out
to the other.
Lawyer Davine, of Warragul (Vic.), beat
Vice-President Senator Albert Blakey in
running for the 1917 chief-presidency of the
A.N.A. As a rule, this office is filled in an
accepted rotation, and Blakey was the office-
bearer all ready to move up into the shoes
of the retiring president, Private Ostrom.
But Blakey’s views on Conscription made
him miss the step.
Adelaide has lost it£ veteran of military
veterans, William Mills. He claimed to be
94, and his fighting was over before most
grandfathers now living were born. It was
all against the Sikhs, at Sobraon, Chillian-
wallah, and so on, in the ’4o’s —so long ago
that his medals attracted special attention
from Kitchener when in Australia. Mills
lost an eye in that long-ago campaign, but he
told no thrilling yarns about it, explaining
modestly that it was done in a tent by the
spur of “a comrade of mine who was making
his bed.” The old warrior was given a
stirring military funeral'by the Veterans’’
Corps in Adelaide —founded 30 years ago by
actor Wybert Reeve.
Hr. Foley, the new R.C. Bishop of Bal-
larat, is a cousin of Dr. Mannix, the
Coadjutor-Archbishop of Melbourne, and they
.both coine from “the beautiful city called
Cork.” The Ballarat bishop does not seem
to have' anything like the warlike spirit of
his cousin. Archbishop Carr, of Melbourne,
told a deputation that waited on him a few
days ago to ask him to restrain his helpmate
that he couldn’t interfere with what Mannix
said in public. But things may alter a
bit. Dr. McCarthy, Dr. Carr’s Vicar-General,
has been made Bishop of Sandhurst (Bendi-
go), and Archbishop Mannix has been made
V.-G. in his place. Now, therefore, Dr.
Mannix will have a lot of “departmental”
work to do under his chief, and the chief
will have more direct and immediate control
over what he does and says.
P. .T. Minahan, the Labor M.L.A. who was
pushed out in the Belmore (Sydney) selection
ballot, and who preferred a martyr’s death
at Cootamundra at the hands of I-lolman
rather than an inglorious count out, was the
first and last man since the days of old Ed-
ward Terry to give away the whole of his
Parliamentary screw. This proved a marked
source of weakness to him ; in Parliament
because of its impossible example, in the
electorate because of the amount of sheer
mendicancy it induced, which grew on what
it fed and remained always hungry. If a
member spent £50.0 per annum judiciously
he should at least get a serviceable advt. out
of it. but let him not mention the amount of
his birdlime, otherwise in every district
tlierc_ will be about 9500 people and 49
associations asking why their dividend
wasn’t paid.

hibition, as he frequently did. A few years
ago he put it this way :
Must the millions of honest, stalwart men be
thrust aside all for the problematical benefit of a
relatively small number of fools or weaklings with a
morbid streak in their blood and brains? We shall
never cure intemperance by drastic or compulsory
legislation so long as thousands and tens of thousands
are cold and hungry, can scarce find work and are
ill-paid when they get if. and live in dirt and foul

Dead recently at Hay (N.S.W.) John
Bent, brother of the late Thomas, Premier,
etc., of Victoria. He was a native of the
Nepean-Penrith district; his father and
Henry Parkes were laborers on John Jami-
son’s Regent Ville estate contemporaneously.
Passed out at the Coast Hospital (N.S.AY.)
last week, Mrs. Clara Phillips, only surviv-
ing daughter of the last representative of
royalty in the Shoalhaven abo. tribe. Her
native name was Gunyeh. She went to the
La Perouse Mission Station when she was
about 21 and lived there for 20 years.
“Try Fluke” : Charles C. Dacre, the old-
time sport who died at Auckland the other
day, belonged to a family whose Maoriland
story goes back to within a few years of a
century. Charles C.’s father was an ex-
Royal Navy captain who had a Sydney-
registered schooner, the Royal Mail, with
which in 1824 or thereabouts he went hunt-
ing for kauri spars in God’s Own Country.
He ran M.L.’s first greenstone cargo. This
was in 1842. Early that year Dacre was in
Manila, when some Filipino agents for Chow
firms got sight of a beautiful greenstone
m6rd he had obtained in 1830, and fell over
one another in their excited inquiries as to
where the rest of the stuff was. The skipper
sold liis mere at a very big price, returned
to Sydney, fitted out the Royal Mail with
blasting powder and quarrying appliances
and sent her off to Milford Sound and other
M.L. bays to pick up a load of the jade. The
man he sent, in charge was blinded by a gun-
powder explosion, and when, with a lot of
trouble, two tons of the stone was shipped
off to Manila,/ it fetched scarcely anything.
It turned out to be the wrong kind—full of
black specks—and the Shanghai Chows for
whom it was wanted yelled that it was all
one-piecee no good.
With Josiah Thomas fighting shy of the
Reps.- seat for the Barrier, the last of the
men who led the big Broken Ilill strike of
24 years ago quitted politics. Sleuth and
Ferguson, the ablest of the lot. dropped out
long ago. Cann. who was the miners’ man
in the House at the time, took a. Railway
Commiss'onership a few mouths agb.
Thomas, who xvas the strikers’ delegate to
Sydney and Queensland, has had a comfort-
able job ever since—first in the Assembly
for the safest Labor seat in N. S. Wales, and
then in the Reps, for the easiest Labor seat
in that House. Now he has to go out into
a cruel world and battle for a Senate seat
with the Liberals; and the spectacle of
’Siah, the uncompromising Socialist, asking
for the confidence of the Win-the-War Fat-
man, and the Fatman pretending to give it
to him, is going to be one of the choicest
sights of the next few months.

war. llis own uncle won the Cross at Luck-
now in Mutiny days. Farquharson’s two
boys have got into khaki in the hope of add-

(A Hymn of Hate.)
LFon The Hulmutn.]
What is meant by active service
’Ere where Hell is leakin’ 100-e,
’N’ the oldest ’and's as nervis
As a dog-bedevilled goose,
Has bin writ be every poet
What can rhyme it worth a dam,
But the 'orror as we know it
Is jist jam, jam, JAM!
Oh, (he ’ymn of ’ate we owe it—
Stodgy, splodgv, seepy, soaky, sanguinary jam!
There’s (he “fearful roar iv battle,”
What gets underneath ver ’at,
Mooin’ like a million cattle
Each as big as Ararat;
There’s the red field green ’n’ slippy
(And I'm cleaner where 1 am),
But the thing that’s got me nippy
It is jam, jam, JAM !
Druv us sour it has, ’n' dippy,
Sticky, sicky, slimy, sloppy, stummick-strafm’ jam l
Of the mud that’s in the trenches
Writers make a solemn fuss;
For the vermin ’n’ the stenches
kittle ladies pity us ;
But the yearn that’s honest dinkum,
’N’ the prayer what ain’t a sham
Is that Fritz may bust ’n' sink em
Ships of jam, jam, JAM!
For we bolt 'em, chew ’em, drink ’em,
Million billion bar’ls of beastly, cloyin’, clammy jam 1
We are sorry-sick of peaches,
’N’ we’re full right up of plum,
’N’ our innards fairly screeches
When the tins of apple come.
Back of Blighty piled in cases,
Jist as close as they can cram,
Fillin' all the open spaces,
F the jam. jam, JAM!
Oh, the woe the soldiers face is,
Monday, Sunday, ruddy, muddy, boundless bogs of
jam.

The British Tobacco Co. (Australia),
Ltd.
, Dividends. N
6 p.c. To Re- Re-
£ £ £ £ £
Jan., Profits. Pref. Ordinary. serves, serves.
1915.. 532,824 89,084 12 p.c.= 438,618 5122 24,498
1916.. 538,532 89,084 12 „ ~443,495 5953 30,451
1917.. 536,974 89,084 12 ~ ~447,175 715 31,166
In 1903 the British Australasian Tobacco
Co. took over the Dixson and Cameron busi-
nesses ; and they, again, were amalgama-
tions. Subsequently other concerns were
brought in; and now there are apparently
three cos.—the British Australasian, the
States Tobacco and W. I). & H. O. Wills
(Aus.) —whose shares are held wholly by
this co. These subsidiary cos. have each an
independent existence; and, the. directors
say, the published figures of this co. “do
not include the co.’s interest in the undivided
profits of the subsidiary cos.” Nor, of course,
do the reserves of this co. include the
reserves of the subsidiary cos. Pre-
sumably. however, the profits of the subsi-
diaries don’t very greatly exceed their divi-
dends ; at any rate, there is no obvious rea-
son why any large amount of profit should
be laid by. The capital is hoav £5,219,256,
of which £1.484,727 is in 6 p.c. cumulative
pref. shares of £1 each and £3,734,529 in
fully paid £1 ordinaries. Of this £5,219,256
no less than £4,769,470 is represented by
“shares in subsidiary cos. (book value).”
As the balance-sheets of the latter cos. are
not published it is impossible to say how
much of the capital is represented by good-
will and other hypothetical assets. Certainly
there must be a large difference between the
book value of the shares and the value of
the premises, plant, stock and other tangible
assets. In a case of this kind, therefore, the
investor has to disregard the tangible secu-
rity for his principal, and consider only what
the chances are of the business being left
alone to make profits. The man who wants to
see gold for his money, or something that can
be converted into gold if the business stops,
must pass British Tobacco shares. The
market takes a sanguine view of the future,
and in normal time’s the ordinaries are
worth about £2. In other words the market
adds, say, £3,500,000 to the par value of the
shares; and it pays that mostly for goodwill,
in addition to whatever of goodwill may be
included in the book value of the shares in
the subsidiary cos.
Twelve months ago there were 3,702,244
ordinaries, and since then 32.285 have been
issued—to the trustees of the Employees’
Trust. During the/ past three years the
additions to capital in this way have been:
1914 .. .. £33,536
1915 .. .. 38,708
1916 .. .. 32,285
Total. . . . £104,529
The plan of co-partnership is worth some
attention ; for the British Tobacco Co. carries
profit-sharing further probably than any other
Australian co. The shares are allotted to
employees, male or female, who have been a
certain period with this or a subsidiary
co.—two years qualify in most cases. Salary
or wages are the basis of allotment; but for
the purposes of the allotment no salary of
over £250 a year is taken into account.
There are six trustees —four appointed by
the directors and two by the employees—
and they hold the shares in trust for the
employee to whom they have been allotted.
After the trustees have hqld them for five
years the employee is entitled to have them
sold, if he wants to sell them; but as he
gets the 12 p.c. dividend on them he mostly
doesn’t want to, especially as at the end of
eight years the trustee hands them over and
the employee goes on the register as an
ordinary shareholder, with full rights.
Assuming that all the 104,529 shares allotted
since the scheme was introduced are still
held on behalf of employees, the latter now
get an addition of £12,543 to their wages or
salary in a twelve-month. And they don’t
get the profit only; the principal is theirs
too. The co. does not, of course, bind itself
to go on distributing shares; that must de-
pend upon whether the co-partnership idea
gives practical results. In case the employee
dies within the five-year period the shares
are sold for the benefit of his dependants.
It is a fine scheme, not only for inducing the
employees to take an interest in their work
and for ridding the cos. of those wretched
little strikes that are so harassing and ruin-
ous, but also for minimising the risks of
political action against the trade.
Incidentally, and as part of the same
scheme, the co. has an insurance department,
embodying house-purchase and annuities. On
the house-purchase side the employee may,
by himself putting up 5 p.c. of the amount
he requires for building, borrow the balance
from the co. at the astonishingly low rate of
3 p.c.: and repayments may be spread over
a very long period—up to 29 years. The
life insurance premium is payable half by
the co. and half by tiie employee; it is one
of the A.M.P.’s ordinary endowment policies,
maturing at 60 or 65, according to the age of
the employee at entrance, and the benefits
are payable in either a lump sum of by w r ay
of annuity. If the employee cannot pass the
medical test, then the co. does the next best
thing to encourage the employee to be
thrifty— it will pay into the savings bank
the amount which it would otherwise pay
into the life office. There are other minor
benefits offered by the co.; and if. all to-
gether, they don’t get for the co. and its sub-
sidiaries a superior crowd of workpeople
who value their jobs, the directors have
every reason to feel hurt about it.

Revenue was £48,784; and working and
other expenses were £25,629. That looks
like a decent profit. But the trouble is
that the co. owes £172,000 on debentures and
also merely leases part of its line from the
Emu Bay and Mount Bischoff Co.; wherefore
last year interest called for £10,083 and rent
£10,370. Usually, when a trifle is knocked
off for depreciation, there isn’t anything
left. In fact, there has often been less
than nothing; so that the co. owes £5OOO
on account of deferred rent and £6579 on
account of deferred interest. Also on
£lOO,OOO of pref. capital 4 p.c. dividend is
cumulative; and there is a dead horse of
£12,000 to be got rid of on that account.
Some years ago this particular dead horse
became so eloquent that the directors threw
out all but the bones. They “funded” the
debt, the understanding being that it would
be shovelled out at the rate of £5OO a year.
llow it is being shovelled out may be judged
from the fact that at the end of 1914 funded
dividends and interest (they were of course
to carry interest) amounted to £14,526. The
total is now £16,218. There are 260,000
ordinary shares which have been written
down from 20s. to 15s. To write them
down ss. seems to have been the directors’
little joke. The debentures are repayable
in 1924 ; or the co. can buy them up before
then at 110. That is another little joke.
The truth is that, without paying dividends
even on the pref. capital, the co. is not quite
holding its own. Here is a three-year com-
parison :
1913. 1916.
More or less liquid assets.. . . £15,572 £21,334
Liabilities (exclusive of debentures) . . 21,648 36,012
Excess liabilities .. £6,076 £14,678
If ordinaries, in those circumstances, take
a cheerful view of the future, they deserve
first prize for optimism.
* * * *
Stanmore Preserving Co., Ltd., Sydney,
jam manufacturers, fruit preservers, etc.
The co. began in 1914 with a deficit of
£9605, made up of goodwill, £8956, and pre-
liminary expenses, £649 :
—,
Pref. Ord. To Re-
Dec., Profit. 8 p.c. 8 p.c. serves. Deficit.
£9605
1914.. £3693 £794 £l6OO £1299 -8306
1915.. 4571 1587 1600 1384 6922
1916.. 5390 1600 1600 2190 4732
The first Stanmore Preserving Co. was a
little private concern that was formed in
1906; and a few years later it had a capital
of £6OOO. Herbert Priestley looked after
the pounds; and a couple of Tates, practical
fruit-boilers, looked after the coppers. Early
in 1914 11. E. Pratten, who used to be con-
nected with Taylor Bros., became inter-
ested ; and a new co. was formed to build
something better than the little tin shed
which thus far had been the factory. To
provide the funds 20,000 8 p.c. pref. shares
were issued ; and 20,000 ordinaries were paid
for the business and net assets.
The first year was spent in building and
shifting camp, and the co. was just nicely
ready for the war ord,ers which then started
to pour in. Before the end of 1915 it had
“placed” 3,000,000 tins of jam; and the
directors now say: “The Stanmore jams
have given entire satisfaction to the Imperial
Government, and further large contracts
have been secured, upon which the co. is
operating.” This paper takes the directors’
word for it that the Imperial Government
likes their jam. It doesn’t know any
“Imperial” Government itself. But. it
does know that Billjim’s letters —especially
from Egypt—have made dreadful remarks
about “Imperial” jam, along with remarks
of quite another sort about the too-rare Stan-
more article.
The co. really wanted more than £20,000
of new capital to start with ; and by the end
of the first year it was £I4,SS3 iu debt.
Then the heavy war-orders fell in ; and. to
further strain the finances, tinplate and other
materials, including sugar, became rapidly
dearer. That meant much more money
sunk in the necessary stocks. By the end
of the second year the co. owed £31,003; and
this had moved up to £37,247 by the end
of last year. Several times the directors
have considered the issue of further capital,
but they have held off. It is the stock that
runs into the money: 1914. £12,655; 1915,
£28,025; 1916, £40,561. In ordinary cir-
cumstances you might wonder whether the
directors weren’t losing their heads ; but here
you have two reasonable explanations: (1)
high prices and (2) the fact that it is a long
way between the factory and the biggest
market. Altogether, then, recent figures are
quite abnormal; profit figures, perhaps, as
well as the others. The co. ought to do well; for
the market is as big almost as the world.
But don’t judge by what has been done
under the entirely unusual conditions —some
favorable, some unfavorable —of the past
couple of years.

This municipal trust got to business about
the middle of 1910. At that time only the
municipalities of Prahran and Malvern (both
“cities,” if you please) were interested.
Now cars also run in St. Kilda, Caulfield,
Kew, Hawthorn and city of Melbourne.
During 1914 the councils of the suburbs men-
tioned came to an agreement by which all
the lines operated by the Trust on behalf of
the councils were taken over as Trust lines,
subject to an indemnity against loss until
Sept. 30, 1916, in respect of the Caulfield
Extension lines. In the past two years under
that agreement Caulfield has paid out £5249
and St. Kilda £736. In the table the profits
are shown exclusive of these payments by
councils; and the payments to councils are
shown net.
At the beginning the Trust was financed
by the Melbourne Tramway Co., which lent
it £lOO,OOO at 4 p.c., on debentures issued at
par, and then £21,500 issued at 10s. p.c.
premium. This loan expired early in 1914.
The Commonwealth Bank subsequently came
into the business and is a large holder of
debentures. The Trust’s total liability is
now as follows :
Amount. Interest. Due.
£70,000 4J p.c. 1933
500,000 4| p.c. 1934
35,000 44 p.c. 1942
70,000 44 p.c. 1943
(Due to municipalities.)
20,185 4 p.c. 1941
28,591 4-J p.c. 1944
All except the first two are payable by instal-
ments, and the dates mentioned are the due-
dates of the last instalments. There is no
general sinking fund ; but in connection with
the big £500,000 loan floated early in 1914,
and known as Loan “D,” a special sinking
fund has been established. Subsequently,
when the Commonwealth Bank came in, it
required that 2 p.c. of the amount advanced
should be paid into the bank annually. This,
accumulated at compound interest, will ex-
tinguish the debt in 26| years. Apparently,
however, it is not incumbent upon the Trust
to pay this 2 p.c. out of profits. The bank’s
concern, of course, is. to get its money;
where it comes from is the Trust’s concern.
Perhaps 2 p.c. p.a. is a bit stiff, especially in
the early days of a line; but there ought to
be some sinking fund, anyway—the Trust
ought always to be doing something towards
making the lines its own, instead of really
Cohen’s. Down to the last couple of years
nothing whatever was done in this way; the
accumulations were practically all for
renewals. And because there were no
renewals worth mentioning the reserves grew
very fast. In one later year, however, the
fund was bitten into; and special war
expenditure (mostly payments to men on ser-
vice) affect the profits, and thus steady the
reserves.
Another way of putting the history of the
Trust would be as follows :

In addition councils had to part up £2172;
but that won’t be a permanent source of
revenue, even if the “Extension” doesn’t
pay. The undertaking to date has cost
just under £700,000, exclusive of stock in
hand ; and £15,000 a year is nothing out of
the way to put by for future renewals, without
taking the sinking fund into account at all.
It means only about £2 7s. Od. p;c. on the
capital actually borrowed. Nothing seems
plainer than that the Trust can’t afford to
work on a narrower margin. It is the
trouble with nearly all publicly-owned con-
cerns —there *is always the pressure to com-
pel them to give too much for the money.

Reserves.

Or, if you like to include the building altera-
tion account and the leasehold account at
valuation, the reserves are £14,748. It is a
tale of remarkable success. The capital is
£70,000; and though £28,034 of that was
represented by goodwill, leasehold (at valu-
ation) and building additions on leasehold,
the co. has in six years put so much profit
back into the business that to-day it has in
book-debts, stock and cash no less than
£75,857, after providing for all liabilities.
And then it has its plant and fixtures to the
good. The items have moved like this: —
1912. 1915. 1916. 1917.
Sundry creditors . . £7,310 £3,062 £4,754 £5,410
Goodwill items . . 29,459 24,565 23,605 22,645
Sundry debtors . . 3,257 3,499 3,620 4,472
Stock . . . . 31,237 41,500 52,885 66,248
Plant and fittings.. 5,624 5,806 5,432 4,910
Cash, etc. .. ... 14,728 16,513 14,805 12,547
While the very small liabilities are even
smaller than they were five years ago, the
stock has been more than doubled, and is
now valued to within a few pounds of the
capital. Meantime a dividend averaging 0
p.c. has been paid. Three factors appear to
be responsible for the extraordinary profit.
(1) Cash trading. (2) The co. has no capital
(apart from what it has spent on the lease-
hold) locked up in real property. When a
co. has half or more of its capital invested
in city premises, it can’t expect to show a
big return. The security may no doubt be
better; but this isn’t a co. formed to invest
in city property —it is a trading concern.
(3) Management. Besides having a man
who knows the retail business up and down
as managing-director, it has one of the solid-
est accountants in the city as chairman and
a man who knows the wholesale rag trade
as his colleague. The maintenance of pro-
fits depends very largely upon the continu-
ance of such management. Apd, fortunately
or unfortunately, good managers are not
immortal. No doubt there are good man-
agers yet unborn. But the other fellow may
get them. That is the risk you must take
with a trading concern.

to get their “fortunes” from beats me. No, there
is no champagne in cocoanuts, only plain, bro\Vn
beer.
People are dazzled with London copra prices. For
the two pre-war years copra averaged £lB per ton
in the Solomons. For the last two j'ears it has
averaged £l4 here. At moment of writing copra is
£4O in London and £l9 locally. What is the
remedy ? Co-operation, perhaps, but it can t come
yet.
We have been buoyed up in the past with the
belief that bigger prices would rule, owing to the
world washing itself more and eating margarine.
But the only prices that have risen so far have been
those for labor. Recruits cost £8 per head in
January, 1913: to-day they cost £l6, with a threat
of an early jump to £lB per head. All these
calamities do not make the big cos. so uncomfortable
as they do the smaller men. Levers, the soap people,
are in a position to pick up at the other end what
they lose at this. The Solomon Island Development
Co. is in the business to make cash for itself, but it
was projected in the first place to provide its
parent (8., P. and Co.) with certain freights for its
steamers, in view of possible future competition from
other shipping lines.
There are threats of a copra boom after the war.
Let investors study Solomon Island floats with their
eyes glued on the following points: —
Labor and copra prices. —Unless outside labor is
allowed to supplement the local supply, increased
competition will only mean that the enhanced cost ot
labor will balance any increase in copra prices.
Capitalisation. —Heavy capitalisation should be
shied at unless outside labor is allowed to come in.
As things are at present, cos. simply cannot get the
requisite labor to plant the area necessary to ensure
decent dividends on "big capital. Put it this way.
Granted the net return per acre is satisfactory, the
labor is not forthcoming to provide the turnover
required to pay the rosy divs. pictured in pro-
spectuses. ,
Length of waif.— Pictures are circulated showing
palms bearing hundreds of nuts at 44 years. True
enough—sometimes. But don’t build on that. 1 he
average palm takes from five to seven years to come
into bearing. Appreciable returns cannot be looked
for until the seventh or eighth year. Optimists
who blither about dividends within five years should
be chased.
These conclusions have been arrived at whilst
watching a block of scrub turn into a cocoa nut
plantation, and slowly climb towards maturity. I
have been told that I am “on a 1 of a good
thing.” I have offset the toil and sweat involved
against the figures produced, and fail to go fanti
with joy. People who discuss cocoanuts should put
ice on their heads. Rubber is looked upon as an
also-ran down here, yet on the face of it 1 would
swap a cocoanut estate for a rubber ditto any day ,
or, best of all, become a copra buyer in Sydney.
In dissecting the accounts of the Stan-
dard Fire and Marine (8/3/’l7) the fact
was overlooked that tin interim dividend
had been paid during the past year. There-
fore the profit was shown as £8026 and the
dividend as £6250, whereas the true figures
are £11,776 and £lO,OOO respectively. It
was a bad mistake, and there is no defence.
All hands responsible are fined 40s. or three
days.
* * *
It will surprise a good many people to
learn that the Commonwealth Oil Corpora-
tion property in the Wolgan i alley
(N.S.W.) is now again paying out at the
rate of £60,000 a year in wages and £->O,OOO
a year for stores, etc. The history of the
old C.O.C. is one long record of extrava-
gance, miscalculation and muddle. Nearly
a million and a half of money was spent;
but most of it was spent in doing the wrong
thing or doing the right thing in the wrong
place. When no more money could be
raised the co. found itself with retorts un-
suitable for handling the very rich shales
on the property, and a huge tunnel only
half completed into the Capertee Valley.
David Fell and Co. were then appointed
receivers in Sydney. They found they
could do practically nothing with the assets;
the only way out seemed to be to make an
attempt to provide suitable retorts. Mean-
time, in London, a scheme of reconstruction
was sanctioned involving the writing-off of
over a million of the capital.
John Fell, of a Sydney firm of oil-mer-
chants, then came into the business, and he
undertook “to build retorts which would
eventually re-establish the industry and place
it upon an economic business basis.” The
retorts were provided, and for some time
past large quantities of oil have been pro-
duced under an agreement by which Mr.
Fell undertook to work the concern for three
years, putting up most of the necessary capi-
tal himself. It has been and continues to
be a pretty tough job, however, partly on
account of keen foreign competition, partly
because the Inter-State Commission’s recom-
mendation of a bonus was never given effect
to, and partly because labor and other costs
have become more and more burdensome.
The development of the industry is therefore
still uncertain, and will remain so until
present conditions change.
Occidental. Cobar (N.S.W.), had its usual
half-yearly meeting lately. For the six months
it treated 6933 tons for 29880 z. gold, valued at
£12.669; hut as it cost about £14,000 to get,
there isn’t much turkey and champagne on
the shareholders’ table yet. A machinery
breakdown and the coal-strike were respon-
sible for a loss of two months of the time.
A new manager, A. S." Lilburn, was ap-
pointed last November, and in making a
special report to the shareholders on- Nov.
28 he said the usual nice things of the future
and found fault with practically everything
at present —except the mine. In a summary
he stated: “I consider the co. has a good
mine with an ample supply of ore, of payable
value, and it is only necessary to maintain
a constant output, increase the tonnage, im-
prove the extraction on lines mentioned, and
the directors can feel assured that profitable
results will follow.” But the same sweet
things have been written so many times
before that shareholders nowadays scarcely
wake up to listen. The reserves are esti-
mated at 65,000 tons worth from 40s. to 48s.
per ton, or two years’ crushing at 15,000 tons
a half-year. Lilburn thinks he can handle
that much.
