Central Illawarra Coke and Coal Co.
(N.S.W.) has struck a 7ft seam with only
one clayband in a borehole south of Mount
Ivembla. The bore was put down three
miles from the outcrop, and the discovery of
such an extent of coking coal to the south is
highly important. The Hoskins firm, of
Lithgow and Sydney, is developing a large
coal area in the same locality.
T. J. THOMPSON.
Died last Saturday, aged 87, T. J. Thomp-
son, one of the fathers of the Sydney Stock
Exchange. The kindly old man with the
blue flower in his button-hole had been giv-
ing fatherly advice to investors for nearly
50 years. He had been 84 years in Aus-
tralia ; his father was a draper in business
in Pitt-street, and the son's first job was be-
hind the counter. His sons remain in the
sharebroking business.
Since it started its new plant on suppos-
edly lib. ground Y Water (N.S.W.) has
given its staff something to worry over.
Three runs have been recorded, but not one
has gone over fib. of tin oxide, so profits
aren’t being earned. Certainly it is given
out that the new plant is doing a bettor
yardage than was expected; but good yard-
age isn’t good tin. That, presumably, is the
reason why Moline ran up to do some
scratching on the tailings dump in order to
find out where the other ilb. is. A co. can’t
afford to waste quarter-pounds with tin at
£213. And it can still less afford to lose
quarter-pounds w T hen tin drops to £163. as
it may—though it doesn’t look as though it
will for a while.

“Ore reserves are estimated at approxi-
mately 22,000 short tons, at a grade recover-
able by amalgamation of from 30s. to 325.
per short ton.” Thus N. E. Giblin, 8.E., in
liis first report on the Edna May Central.
Rough out the estimate this way; 22,000
tons at 325. = £35,200 gross return. Take the
tails as worth 9s. per ton. as per assay
results for the past six months, and £lO,OOO
can be added, making the total value of the
gold in sight £45,000. Deduct 225. per ton
for milling and mining, and put cyanide
charges and loss at ss. per ton. Thus £30,000
is mopped up. This leaves £15,000 profit in
sight for 60,000 shares of 10s. each. That
profit means ss. per share. As the market
price of scrip is 20s. the difference (155.) is
represented by faith in the next levels or by
a belief that the Consolidated bulge of stone
will meander into the Central at less than
450 ft. True, the Board has also £6OOO cash
in hand ; but the new cyanide plant and air-
compressor will mop that up and cause a bit
of the nest-egg supposedly hidden in the accu-
mulated dumps to disanpear.
Or work out the Edna Central position on
the records of the past. The co. put £7500
to the credit of working account, sold 9000
reserve 10s. shares at a premium of (sayl
£2500, and crushed 68.352 tons for gold
worth £83.717. The capital and the pro-
ceeds of the gold have gone—£ll,Boo of it on
plant, £950 on the main shaft, £9500 on
development, and all the. balance in wages,
stores, etc., except the cash in hand and 9s.
paid in divs. But even that watery record
does not deter the optimists on the Board from
declaring that appearances in the claim,
coupled with developments in the adjoining
mines, “justify great confidence in the
permanency and profitable prospects of the
company.” Does the man who got out this
screed claim that the Edna Deeps develop-
ments shows where or when the main Edna
shoot will make across the boundary into the
Central? Or is there Gd. worth of evidence
that the Consolidated find is more than a
local bulge in an otherwise low-grade reef?
Fortunately N. E. Giblin does not let
“bull” or any other market influence into his
mine reports. So he doesn’t give the mine a
lOoz. sample anywhere, and he puts the New
make of ore as worth 475. 6d. for 137 ft. to
the south and to ttie east 245. 6d. for 102 ft.
Beyond that the average is about 11s. per
ton, which won’t pay. All the same, there
must be something in the wind, or the crowd
that does it wouldn’t prop the stock so
readily whenever the 18s. mark is touched.
Melbourne-owned Mary Mac (W.A.) is in
need of money. The plant cost more than
had been estimated on account of the war,
and the prices of supplies and material have
advanced considerably. Owing to an intru-
sion the “25,000 tons of payable ore” about
which official reports had so much to say
have disappeared, and only a very limited
quantity can be shown for the heavy outlay
incurred in development. Had an expert
with a knowledge of the district furnished a
report the Board would not have fallen into
the error of imagining that 25,000 tons, or
anything like that quantity, would be avail-
able. An estimated 39,000 tons of slimes
stacked on the property are valued at
£20,000. but treatment costs will take a big
bite out of that.

New Boddingtons (W.A.) have slumped.
Even the official announcement of the dis-
covery of a large reef in the bottom has not
influenced the market, and the trustful per-
sons who bought shares on the strength of
what was said in the prospectus are now
sorrowing. Knowing something about the
small, treacherous reefs of the locality. The
Bulletin urged caution at the time the
armful of rich specimens on. which the
flotation was engineered were exhibited < in
Kalgoorlie. Seeing the way things are going
the Golden Butterfly has hurriedly aban-
doned three adjacent blocks on which it
acquired an option while the boom-fires were
burning.
Some' Melbourne optimists have been in-
duced to take an interest in the Harbor
Lights, Gwalia (W.A.). Several option-
holders have had a go at this show in the
last 10 years, but all have retired hurt. The
Associated Northern did some drilling and
then left, and others who followed found
that the grade of ore and conditions of work-
ing did not promise success. The present
holders will probably pay for the same in-
formation.
After frittering away a good deal of cash
Golden Point (W.A.) has come to the con-
clusion, forecasted by The Bulletin, that
its Comet Yale option is valueless. When
the show was acquired it was said to have a
continuation of the Sand Queen shoot, but it
is alleged that when the 12 acres of ground
lying between it and the payable portion of
the Sand Queen reef were offered, the Board
lapsed into silence. Do shareholders know
anything about this?
General-manager Corbould, of Mt. Elliott
(Q.). which threatens to have its output of
copper stopped for want of coke, told the
Queensland Minister for Mines the other
day that he understood the shortage was
owing to exports. In any case, whether this
raw product of one of our most important
industries is being largely shipped away
or not, it seems certain that a new con-
sumer of considerable importance has come
into the local market. This is Sydney Per-
ries. Ltd. On account of the increase in
the price of coal this co. renewed its experi-
ments with the use of coke. They were so
successful, it is understood, that the output
of certain local gasworks has been secured.
This gas-coke is not suitable for smelting;
but it used to go to people who now have
to turn in other directions for their require-
ments.
* * * *
A recent message to The Bulletin about
the New Mount Lyell Consols was mangled.
This puts the facts as far as this paper can
ascertain them:—
The New Lyell Consols replaced the Lyell Consols,
which had worked out its Tasmanian mine. There
are in the New co. 200,000 paids and 300,000 con-
tribs. ; of the latter 150,000 have been underwritten
and about £7OOO is still available, lea vine; 150,000
shares in reserve. These carry a liability of 25., and
when issued will give £15,000 more working capi-
tal, less 3d. per share underwriting commission.
The co.’s property is in S. Aus., adjoining the
Wallaroo. Two lodes were located by thd drill, and
it was while crosscutting for these at the 250 ft.
level that the co. came on a body missed by the
drill. This body. Captain Hancock, of the Moonta
and Wallaroo, and Inspector of Mines Jones think is
the Wallaroo’s “Blue” lode. The two bodies cut by
the drill have yet to be reached. The management,
when it saw the end coming in Tasmania,, looked
round Australia for a promising show that wanted
capital for prospecting and development. It could
find nothing that looked, as good as this; and it is
satisfied now that it didn’t make a mistake.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, March 26, 1917.
My Dear Arini, —
This time the date is a certainty. The
Stricklands pack up the pram, and the kin-
dergarten in May. After all, they’re going
by the Canadian route, and will see the snow
melting on the. Rockies.
Another departure is also imminent—and
Premier Holman's suit-case is being dusted
for the trip. He goes to England in May,
and will spend about four months whisper-
ing soft promises into the ear of Cohen.
One of the first batch of V.A.D.’s to be
sent to London mentions in a letter to a
girl-friend that she has been to our old
friend Lord Beauchamp’s place, Madresfield
Court, to see the new baby, who was born
last Christmas Day. This is Beauchamp’s
third son. He lias also four daughters. Not
a bad record for the noble pair who were
married as recently as 1902.
The same letter-writer mentions the
announcements of the London winter
fashions. “Dainty chemises for street wear,”
announces one journal. Reminds one of the
awful dreams most of us have experienced
of being in a public place at midday clad
in a bangle. Then there’s the “Conciliation”
boot. Leather being almost forbidden save
for military needs, the Conciliation has only
a tiny toecap of patent leather. The rest of
(lie boot (except, of course, a sole) is of
cloth.
Veteran Sam McCaughey’s infant great-
nephew had to bear a douche of cold water
in the Fog in bleak January. Mamma is
Airs. Cave-Clark, with a hubby at the Front.
Godpa Lieut. Frank Chesney, of Rosalie
station, Darling Downs, was attending to his
business at Salonika. So Major Cunning-
ham saw that the name Gordon Chesney was
duly mentioned during the incantations. A
lunch at Hyde Park Hotel, the tables gar-
nished with white blooms, settled the busi-
ness.
Last week’s private cables from France
t<dd of the death of Butler Wood, of Kirri-
billi, who went away with the sth Artillery
Brigade as a lieutenant, but later on won pro-
motion in a howitzer battery. “But.,” the
wealthy son of a squatting father, lately
dead, was a line horseman and a cheerful
young officer much favored by his men. He
bad been on leave in London, and was killed
within a week or so of his return to the mud.
Killed at the Somme, Owen Tuck, who left
las job as senior classical-master at The
King’s School, Parramatta, to wear a
private’s uniform last year. Tuck, who had
a first-class Oxford degree, left Australia as
sergeant, but had climbed up to a lieutenancy
in France.
Frank Leverrier, K.C., found it impossible
to tell the Thrift-ladies the exact relation be-
tween Science and Industry this week. So
his name melted from the programme. But
H. Y. Braddon, H. E. Pratten, Prof. Irvine
(who hands out stiff doses of Economics in
our _ ’Varsity), Ruth Beale, and Meredith
Atkinson are on the stump. March 30 is
the last date on which you can get a hint
that may reduce your indignant family to
tears and dripping.
Another Military Cross. This one goes
to Captain W. S. Bone, of our Army, who
was sworn into khaki on the first day of
war, and had a hand in the Rabaul fuss be-
fore stepping over to' France to see what
was doing. Mrs. Bone, who lives at Longue-
ville, is accepting congratulations in the
proper spirit.
Artist Rupert Bunny, one of Australia’s
greatest brush-men, writes, with frozen
hands, from Paris. “In rooms not heated
by the scanty handful of coal that ordinary
citizens can beg or borrow, the temperature
is_ seven degrees below zero. Outside it is
15 deg. below. We have had an Arctic winter,
and, so far as I know, the best chance of
getting almost warm is in the schools, so
all the artists are working there. Goodness
knows where they get thet coal, or how, but
they have it.” Hypersensitive and apt to
brood over spiteful criticism (the obscure
are never maligned, if he could but remem-
ber it!). Bunny writes bitterly of having
been called a deserter from Australia. “Yet
in Paris I am known everywhere as ‘Aus-
tralian Bunny.’ From France and Rome I
have had all the warmest encouragement—
very little in London since Millais and
Leighton passed out. Poynter, the present
R.A. President, has refused some of my
work which was not only hung in the famous
International Exhibition in Rome a few
years ago, but was singled out for praise
by the greatest art critics in Europe. So I
cannot find a place to-day in London, where
Australian artists abound and are not called
deserters.”
After five months’ incessant hard work for
tbe American Red Cross in Paris, the deli-
cate Bunny broke down. He refused to be-
come a naturalised French citizen, the only
bar to certain national commissions. Yet the
sensitive skin quivers still at the word
"deserter.” On March 15, by request, he
gave a one-man exhibition in the Georges
Petit Gallery. Gustave Geoffery, one of the
men that count in Europe’s Art circles,
offered to write the preface to the catalogue.
Perhaps, later, we shall get a copy in Aus-
tralia.
The Roumanian Miss Egresitz. who was
here last winter, is now teaching foreign
tongues in a Methodist Girls’ School in the
Malay States. It is a big academy with 200
pupils, partly supported by the Government,
otherwise by the American Mission. “We
prepare candidates for the Cambridge
(Eng.) University,” she says. “Though
three of the teachers belong to the Mission,
the others are, like myself, only common
mortals.” In Kuala-Lumpur, 600 feet above
the sea, architecture in the Byzantine style
faces streets that look as if housemaids had
just dusted them. “Malay bullock-carts and
a passing show of gorgeous Chinese and
Malay glad-rags, with Europeans bumping
past in rickshaws, make a vivid picture. Also
there is rain! This is the season for it. So
much of the railway line has been washed
away. The richest man in the Malay States,”
concludes the lady from Bucharest, “died
yesterday and left 27,000,000 dollars. Of
this 1| million was invested in the British
War Loan. It sounds like fiction, but this
Crcesus was a Chinese who began life as a
coolie.”
When the 2nd D.A.C. committee met to
consider final details of its tent at the
Show, hon. sec. Mrs. Ballantine made some
remarks about thieves. There were bat-
talions that lost (I think) eight dozen spoons
last year. So a night-watchman is one of
the exes, this year.
Australian authorities in Egypt snatched
thousands of pairs of socks from the burn-
ing. Don’t start, Arini. Sister Susie sent
too many to the heated desert. The surplus
reached 81,000; and when distracted O.C.’s
in France were trying to keep their men in
reasonably dry footgear, that A.I.F. sur-
plus was handed over. In Egypt men were
actually burning these comforts; as they
couldn’t stow extras away. “Send your
tinned stuff, fruit, vegetables, boiled lollies
and cigarettes to your own brigade or bat-
talion, and label it so that the boys know
you did send it,” said Mr. Budden to the
D.A.C. dames. “But don’t try to conserve
your socks and warm clothing.” It was upon
the D.A.C. that the War Chest committee
urged more concerted action ; but there’s no
reason why other more exclusive comforts-
dames shouldn’t listen.
The Vagabonds’ Club, having done so
much for the patriotic hat, might now do a
little for itself by putting a polish on its
productions. It needs the help of a good-
natured professional to give its stage business
a lift and cast an eye over the grease-paint
department before the show. With £lOOO
already earned for local war-funds, it started
for its second thou, with “What would a
Gentleman Do?” Ruby Coxhead. Hugh D.
Jennings and Peter Egan were in the lead
at Saturday’s performance. The cash goes
to the Fire Brigade’s Red Cross fund.
Dorothy Davenport’Hill, a Neutral Bay
charmer, accepted the usual token from Syd-
ney F. Harvey the other evening. Excuse
me if I mention the grand-daddy of the
penny stamn, Rowland Hill; but the girl is
his great-grand-niece, or something of the
kind. Dorothy was in white shimmer, with
clouds of tulle billowing over the train.
Bridesmaidens Violet Graham and Dorothy
Walford, in blush pink, had the latest notion
—silver lace caps—on their heads. Refresh-
ments at the Australia, and then Jenolan
Caves for two.
Yvonne Cliarvin, the slender lady who
teaches the right way with a piano at the
Conserve torium, is back from a long holiday.
She meant to have a pleasant outing and
return from Noumea with her husband, Cap-
tain Cliarvin, of the Pacifique. But he,
desolate sailor, had unexpectedly to spend
six weeks of grass-widowhood in Sydney
while the Pacifique had an overhaul in dock.
Stuck fast in Noumea for lack of a craft to
bring her home, the French-Australian did
the usual thing—organised a concert for the
benefit of their men at the Front.
The Trench Mortar folk have joined the
Comforts Funds struggle. Can’t you hear
your last threepence fall in the till? The
McMillan dame opened their show in St.
James’s. In a hall crowded to suffocation,
the übiquitous Budden talked socks and the
Cheero girls took the stage for song and
dance. Mrs. E. Mitchell was hon. organiser,
and hon. sec. Mrs. Mackay mentioned in a
soft whisper that the Trench Mortar Com-
forts baby was eight weeks old.
The Palace Theatre was crowded to the
keyhole for the' matinee to help the Went-
wo'rth Free Kindergarten. This has a
building debt that is going to melt when
£3UO (or not far off} passes into the
treasury from the Palace show. Mrs. Hugh
Mclntosh had such hefty workers as
Mrs. Ferrari Passmore and Mrs. Connolly
to push the show through. Mrs. Wilkinson,
another strenuous lady help, was one of the
bidders for a huge rose-bowl, given by little
Mrs. Shimizu, who beamed in a box when
the china brought nearly £4O. All the
bidders, including John Mackay, handed it
back to the auctioneer, Barry Lupino.
Before putting it up again, Barry expressed
his joy by turning a Catherine-wheel over a
grand piano. Minnie Love gurgled sweetly
about a Broken Doll, and Ethel Cadman
flashed a fine set of teeth when her turn
came. All the theatres “lent” somebody, and
the show was a topnotcher.
The Curnow-Earp wedding occurs this
week. The bride is a fair-haired daughter of
George Earp, M.L.C., of a Newcastle firm.
The ’groom has done his bit at the Front.
Vera Cree and her shepherdess, Ada Gee,
reached London without a war-time dip in
the sea. The girl is studying under Leonard
lies at the Royal Academy. He’s the man
who rounded Ella Caspers’s velvet voice.
Miss Gee is working for her A'.R.C.M. under
Hubert Parry, chief of the Royal College of
Music.
Leslie Platherly, a lieut. in the R.N.IL, a
son of W. Hatherly, of Newcastle (N.S.W.),
stepped out in London lately to the tune of’
the Wedding March. The bride is an Eng-
lish bud, Muriel Yardley, of Birkenhead, the
place where they count the wharves by the
mile.
Here is one of the enthusiasts of the N. S.
Wales Women’s Horticultural Guild —Miss
Marcia Brace. An Englishwoman of inde-
pendent means, she likes Australia so much
that with the exception of occasional jaunts
to her native drizzle she has lived on this
side for the last eight years or more. Talk
to her about chickens, cauliflowers or any
other kind of blooms, and she’ll answer back
in suitable terms. Gardening and the out-
door life have called her since she was a
youngster. In Eng-
land she took a
certificate which
gives her profes-
sional status
amongst bulbs and
seedlings. Chick-
ens are a later
hobby. If you
mention Rhode
Island Reds—she
owns a poultry-
farm in the Gran-
ville hinterland—
she’ll describe their
uttermost feather.
Miss Brace lec-
tures in this Thrift
week by special re-
quest. But she’s
not prepared to
draw any hard and fast lines 05 say where
Thrift ends and Mean Habits begin.
The Brian Pockley memorial window
was unveiled by Anglican Archbishop
Wright on Thursday. The young doctor was
the first Australian commissioned officer
killed in this war, being shot while attend-
ing to the wounded in New Guinea at the
landing there in 1914. Artist Norman
Carter has deftly worked the modern battle-
field incident into glass for the chapel of
the Church of England Grammar School.
St. George is shown in one light and the
other _ has St. Luke, as “the beloved
physician,” attending to a wounded soldier.
It is a complete all-Australian job, done
with the skilled co-operation of glass-painter
F. Tarrant.
The Returned Soldiers’ Society, started by
Svdney Maorilanders, continues to flourish.
At the recent annual meeting Owen Cox
was invited up to the chair; and the promise
of a suite qf furniture for the forthcoming
M.L. Club was the first sign of his active
interest. The Gould-Smitli lady and Miss
Parker are again secretaries. ' Lately the
wind has been raised by concerts held on the
steamers coming from and going to Ward’s
land. Judging by the results it has been
almost a gale.
Mosman Public School is giving a lead.
The first three recruits obtained by the
school (old boys preferred), over 21 in years,
are to be insured for £2OO and the pre-
miums paid up till six months after the war
ends. In addition, the youngsters of the
school guarantee to keep these special
knights well supplied in all the etceteras a
soldier looks for on the other side. Any
offers ?
A Sydney woman writer, who .prefers to
be anonymous, has broken out in a booklet
of verse. Proceeds go to the Y.M.C.A. War
Fund. “Rhymes at Random” are worth the
bob.
Ren6 Maxwell, a young Sydney song-bird,
got quite a large helping of butter from the
usually chilly critic in the Morning Post
(London). The affair was a Sunday con-
cert in the Royal Albert' Hall.
"Daddy hong-legs - ’ Waldron, his family
and suite are inhabiting Esclaroy, the Wil-
liam Sandford home ou Bellevue Hill, during
their sojourn at this port. The same roof
sheltered John McCormack and his train
when they were here a while ago.
A Duntroon boy writes me that the
Soudan donkey, mentioned a week or two
ago, died of exposure in July, 1914, through
straying from her stable on a cold, frosty
night. Mrs. Bridges (now the General's
widow) found the body next day, and it was
buried near the riding-school at the college.
“By the way,” he adds, “it is not generally
known that Jenny had a foal, born on the
voyage to Australia. The youngster
worked in the Sydney Botanic Gardens pull-
ing a lawn-mower for some time, but came
to an untimely end.”
The George Perrins gave a tea-party at
the Wentworth to say good-bye to daughter
Amy, monologue artist and nice girl. She
is off to the Malay States, where T. M. Phil-
son will be waiting on the shore. There was
music and a song or two —Eileen Mullen
warbled of red roses —with subdued feminine
purrs over Amy’s diamond ring.
From one of our Billjim brothers: -
It isn’t only our beautiful nursing sisters that
show our youngsters a fine tenderness. Next to me
there is a kiddie who enlisted at 17 and isn’t much
more now —a nit e, clear-eygd, clean-faced boy. The old
barber came his rounds yesterday, and the boy had
one of his first shaves. A few sweeps of the razor and it
was done. The weary lad put his hand under his
pillow and held up his little hoard of silver. “Help
yourself,” he said. “May I?” replied the kindly
old chap. “Then I’ll take this.” And he stooped
over our boy and kissed him on the lips.
Members of the Cavell Memorial Fund
Committee are going to take turns behind
the counter at 259 George-street. It is the
latest venture in the cake and jam trade.
On April 10 St. Vincent’s Hospital starts
a week’s campaign for its lean treasury.
Among other forms of attack there’s to be a
matinge at one of the J.C.W. theatres.
Carleon, at Bellevue Hill, for many
years the home of the C. B. Fairfaxes, is
now inhabited by the T, PI. Kellys, who have
just finished a three years’ rustication at
Bowral.
“Rosna’s” latest London tale of the Senti-
mental Bloke: —
On January 27 Dorothy, the eldest damsel of one
of the Melbourne Lempriere families, was married
to Frank Debenham, who is a majah in the Oxford
and Bucks Light Infantry. Day before that George
Irving, of Westralia, a captain in the Rifle Brigade,
married a nice doctor’s daughter (or a nice daughter,
anyway) named Olive Andrews, at Elstree, with the
bride’s bishop-uncle to see that all was shipshape.
On February 3 the Bishop of London firmly tied the
knot for Marjorie .Lawford, of the Bathurst elan ; she
married the Rev. H. R. B. Morgan, vicar of Windmill
Hill, Bristol, formerly chaplain to Mr. All;ins in
France. On February 6 Miss Yuille of Melbourne,
who has been war-nursing in Egypt, said all the
necessary things at Brompton Church, in the pre-
sence of D. Hume Turnbull, who is a son of the late
J. J. Turnbull, of Toorak. Also on January 31 Lydia
King, of Orange, became Mrs. H. G. Carter, her
benedict being a son of the 11. J. Carters, of Wah-
roonga.
Maoriland also had a word with Hymen. In Glas-
gow Eric Aubrey Hawkes Whitcomb, of the Christ-
church family, now a lieut. in the R.F.A., married
Nancy Templeton Young, of East Kilbride. At Hen-
don meantime John Rutherford Patterson, of Dunedin,
was shackled to Lucie Spencer; and at Brompton
Oratory, Mona Levin, one-time of Wellington, be-
came Mrs. J. D. C. Hawkins, bridegroom being a
captain in the R.F.A. and son of a brigadier-general.
Small, precipitous millinery is holding its
own with the wide flat hats of the new
autumn. The diminutive headgear turns
sharply off the face, in imitation of North
Head, and there’s a small veil to break the
fall to the nose. Pancake, crowns —-very flat
—with trimming gummed on, have come
along with a rush. You’ve got to look out
that they don’t go off in the same way, fur
the wide brims catch every squall.
Now that quite incompetent young house
maids can get from ISs. to £1 a week a sub-
urban dame who pleaded for a help at His.
asked why the steady increase in Diary
Jane’s demands. “Well, it’s this way,” said
that mistress of the situation. “I’m getting
my little sister taught the piano, and my
boots alone cost 275. 6d. a pair-—it’s hard
times for me!” “And for me,” sighed Mrs.
Subbubs, who wears shoe leather —half-soled
and frequently heeled—at about half the
money. Yet, I don’t know. Perhans merely
Mary earns more than her mistress. And if
she does, why shouldn’t she get it?
Beatrice Harraden and Elizabeth Robins,
the English novelists, are running a library
and ivaste-paper depot in a military hospital
entirely staffed by women. Our own Red
Cross can afford to brag about waste-paper.
It has taken £5BO cash for “waste” and
calendars during the past ll.months.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, March 26, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Prahran Red Cross Carnival started
with beat of drum on Saturday afternoon.
State Governor Stanley had swallowed a nice
speech beforehand, and when the time came
to orate he went up like a balloon in praise
of the work that is being done by the
Prahranese. Lady Stanley sat alongside his
Ex. on the platform and looked as pretty as
a flower in black with fine muslin petals
turned back from her chin and a large pearl
in either ear. Mrs. Young wore a pale face
and a gold bird in her black hat. Mayor
Embling was loaded with every available
bit of the local fur, chain and lace. His chest-
nut-haired wife supported a flat pale-lemon
hat; and a fearsome company of neighbor-
ing mayors and their womenfolk dressed the
stage and said a line or so apiece. The
grey-haired Miss Abrahams, whose name is
always on hospital-ward donations, had a
seat in the background. Several nurses in
white brightened the stage. Genthelp Conant
looked most lugubrious as he listened to the
speeches; the hall was a sea of pretty girls
and he was “skied” and silenced amongst
the talking mayors.
All the stall-helping girls were uniformly
dressed in cunningly-draped gowns of Dutch
blue, dull red and orange, and their hair
was bound in handkerchiefs of Dutch blue
and white. Even the small girls, who were
told off with sweets-baskets, were colored
miniatures of the bigger girls. The riot will
rage for a week.
Baronet Rupert Clarke is steaming home
with his medical discharge in his kitbag,
after more than a year’s work at the Front.
The good sport was not too fit and hardly
young enough for the job; but he scorned
the idea of a light Red Cross billet and did
his bit as pluckily as any 30-year-old. Fever
knocked him out of action at last.
All the Ross Munro clan (they squat
heavily in Bananaland) streamed down to
Melbourne to welcome home their son and
brother, who struck trouble while flying. He
looks none the worse for his active service
except for an honorable scar in the middle
of his forehead. The family put up at the
Oriental and held high jinks to welcome
him and his little English bride. Her first
name is Betty.
When the boys of Melbourne Grammar
School rolled into assembly last Thursday to
take the oath of allegiance to Acting-Chief
Girdlestone, Herring, the school captain,
climbed on to the platform laden with fare-
well trifles for the departing Franklin, who
is leaving to cane the Huns out of France.
The boys’ parting gifts were a special brand
of wrist watch, a pair of field-glasses, a
pocket camera and a siLer cigarette-case
just the size of a tunic pocket. The grati-
fied Head, finding he had no words and no
voice, got young Herring to thank the boys.
And didn’t they lift the roof with cheers!
Maude Harvie, the enthusiast who gave
the Purple Cross movement its first push in
Melbourne, engineered a crowded tea-party
for horse-lovers the other afternoon. The
administrators of the Purple Cross funds
claim a record of cut-to-the-bone economy,
and they are now asking, at the point of
their balance-sheet, for £20,000. They want
to start a horse-hospital in France, where
the native Australian gee-gee will get all the
home comforts he deserves.
Before the Australian Wireless Corps sailed
away, some 12 months ago, Sergeant George
Thompson had some valuable books stolen.
He was in the habit of giving lectures on
wireless theory to the boys of the unit, and
was trying to purchase more text-books in
Melbourne when he came across one of his
own in Cole’s secondhand department. That
set him inquiring, and he found they had
been purchased from a man.in uniform. Old
King Cole himself was shortly on the scene and
gave the sergeant not only his own treasured
books, but the run of the whole “wireless”
stock. The unit has done good work in
Mesopotamia, and that’s how the old book-
man did his bit towards it.
Major Hurst has been made a lieut.-
colonel. He is a clever and enterprising
Australian who was sent to Aldershot before
the war to study heavy gunnery. Then he
came back to Australia and took a lot of
heavy ironmongery away with him. The
news of the promotion comes from France.
Mrs. Mackworth (late Leonie Peterson)
is having—well, not too dull a time in
Egypt. She is one of the officers’ wives
who have been there since the earliest fight-
ing and has endured two Egyptian summers
to be within coo-ee of her husband. Mack-
worth now has short furlough and they have
gone wandering into Abyssinia or some-
where for a holiday. Leonie is all right
in Cairo because the latest demigod sent to
Egypt by Great Britain is a cousin to her
tall soldier.
From “Rosna,” in London: —
Dr. Francis Mueeke, Ada Crossley’s S. Aus.-born
husband, has been over on leave after strenuous
work with the R.A.M.G. He has been out at the
sundry Fronts since the early days of the war, and
now wears a lieut.-col.’s crown and star. Ada
meanwhile sings to wounded Anzacs and Bilzacs
in hospital, and when they are convalescent has
them to tea and introduces them to the gum-trees in
her garden.
The Anzac Fair at Selfridge’s on Australia Day
captured about £4OO for the useful Anzac Buffet.
Considering that the thing was rushed together in a
few weeks, the result is Al. Among various
useful and decorative articles offered for sale was
a Zeppelin engine, quite a good one as white ele-
phants go, but not to be recommended as a family
pet. As the Buffet entertains and feeds over 5000
of the boys weekly, all gratis, it needs more cash
than it has got. At the A.N.A. Corroboree at the
Connaught rooms on the same festival night, the
girls of the youngest army, or as many of them
as could get away from hospitals and troopships,
were the guests of the Australian Colony in N’Yark.
A musical engagement, just announced, is that
of Olive Bassnett, who used to lift her fine voice
at the Adelaide Conservatorium, to George Pearce,
pianist, of the same city, now with the A.I.F. He’s
the son of a parson at Brighton (S.A.). The lady
an’d her mother and her sisters have been for some
years in London and are now looking for a chance
to slip home, the singer girl having captured a
licentiateship and other honors at the Royal Academy.
At moment of writing a younger sister, Dorothy, is
being married to another South Aus. warrior, Alan
Reid. Dorothy is in the War Office (where the
she-minions are divided into Temporary Ladies and
Permanent Women), and she is going to continue
her job while she is needed and her Benedict is at
the Front. The Bassnetts’ Anzac son has a commis-
sion in the 8.E.F., and is big-game hunting in
Mesopotamia.
Norman Brookes’s proposed purchase of
the South Yarra Bodycombs’ humpy fell
through, so the tennis king has bo’ ght up
the Alfred Leveys’ domicile. Its reglorifica-
tion is, however, adjourned for the present,
as it is leased for a paying guesterie. Mean-
time the Emmertons have lent daughter
Mabel a more modest abode in Walsh-street
until husband Norman gets back from his
Red Crossing.

Mrs. Glenloth Peter gave an Australia
party before bolting for her country home.
Mrs. Stapely and Mrs. Sternberg and a jolly
group of Bananalanders, including the widow
Clifton, were among the guests.
A local fashioneer notes
There is much less in skirts than there has been
recently, and if war frugality continues we may look
for continuous diminution.
Just as well. After 30 we did wander away
from the Milo ideal.
Several women are making a strenuous
effort to raise funds for the Foundling Home
in Berry-street, East Melbourne. Mrs. Fan
Dango Mackay intended to have a fete at
her home, but hubby Sam Mackay hurriedly
offered a donation of £lOO to be rid of the
disturbance. So Mrs. Geoffrey Syme has
offered her house and garden for the show,
and Lady Stanley will probably go out and
say the opening incantations in the third
week in April.
Besides conducting miserable strays to
the death chamber, the Lost Dogs’ Home
receives paying guests. This part of the
business occasionally leads to trouble. Some
Melbourne people hired a kennel flat for
their adored mastiff while they went holi-
day-motoring. Before they departed. there
was a tearful discussion about food. “Dear
Lion” was used to this and that titbit and
must get it or he would die of grief and
stomach-ache. The chief attendant listened
with some impatience. “All are fed alike!”
he said—“the very best of horseflesh and
the best dog-biscuits.” No entreaties could
persuade him to promise more for the
fatted pet, and the motorists left, dazed
with grief. During their absence Lion
became acquainted (through a fence) with
a bulldog lodger, and as the fence wasn’t
300 ft. high, Bully sailed over and knocked a
lot of stuffing out of the star boarder, whose
owners returned to find their darling with
punctures in his nose and one tooth loose.
A woman dentist-vet. was called in and
repaired the family idol; but his worshippers
are still threatening to make trouble.
The William Baillieus’ Toorak place
buzzed with a flapper frivol last Friday
night, when son Tom, who is off to the
Front, was frantically farewelled by youth-
ful fox-trotters, including a soldierly group
of old schoolmates. Di Gilio’s matchless
fiddle squeaked out the final two-step at the
chill hour of two.
Dora, the Reggie Weigalls’ attractive
second daughter, has had her turn with
appendicitis, and is sending news of com-
plete recovery to her medico fiance, Hilary
Carlisle. He has just finished his term
of hospital after being wounded in the
trenches.
The Willie Grey Smiths’ only son, Lieut.-
Commander Jack, was washed overboard and
drowned last week. He had a delightful
personality and was the idol of his parents
and sisters.
Leslie Walford and his bride, pretty Dora
Alexander, have arrived in Melbourne from
Sydney. Dora is eagerly displaying a most
expensive trousseau; but the bridegroom
wears his usual nonchalance, as if marriage
were an everyday occurrence. *And when
you come to think of it, so it is.
A young Victorian took his mother by sur-
prise a few days ago and came into this
sunny world behind an impromptu screen of
coats and vests put up by some kindly sewer-
age men who were working on the road close
by. I regret to say that the sudden emer-
gency stampeded all the members of my own
s ? x . within call. Do you remember that
plaintive old song with the line, “I was born
on a Friday when my mother wasn’t at
home”?
Sister Adelaide’s assertions :
A Chamber of Commerce Conference and a Thrift
Campaign thrust themselves upon our attention
during- the week. The Thrift Campaign, organised
by the League of Honor, is a special appeal to
women, and was emphasised at two public meetings
at the Colton Hall on Thursday. Lady Galway pre-
sided at one and Mrs. Nutter Thomas (the Anglican
Bishop’s wife) at the other. The Governor at the
first roll-up led a discussion on abstruse commercial
and financial problems, which were beyond my com-
prehension, but I gathered that they meant war to
the bowie on German goods. On Monday evening the
Mayor and Mrs. Isaacs held a reception in the Town
Hall, at which the visiting delegates to the Commerce
Conference were chief guests.
Pte. Donald Kerr, a lawyer who won the Military
Medal at Pozieres, is home again. He was decorated
for leading 13 parties of stretcher-bearers to some
wounded machine-gunners who were actually in the
same trenches as the Germans. That was on a
Tuesday, and the injured were not all got to then-
own lines till the following Saturday. The South
Australian was recommended for honors by practically
every officer of the battalion who was in the trenches
at any time between August 12 and 15.
Mrs. Barton, who was married to the doctor of
the Osterley just before that ship left Melbourne, and
was stopped at the West by the embargo on women
and children travellers, has passed through on her
way back to Sydney. She intends to make for
America unless an easier way to England is opened.
An aviation accident has resulted in the death of
Dennys Fowler, son of the' late James Fowler.
Another R.F.C. man, Captain R. G. Hopwood, has
been reported missing since August last. He was
A.D.C. here when war broke out, and left promptly
to rejoin his regiment, subsequently getting a trans-
fer to the Flying Corps.
Dr. Gertrude Halley, who came here from Tas-
mania some time ago as medical inspector of schools,
has resigned, after doing some uncommonly good work
for the State.
Officially this year’s Easter is to be extended, and
Tuesday has been gazetted a bank holiday, so that
overworked clerks may get some of the rest they
need after the strain of the last couple of years.
Josiah Symon, K.C., has been made a vice-president
of the Royal Colonial Institute; he is the only S.
Australian to achieve the position. Samuel Griffith,
George Reid and ex-Prime Minister Deakin are the
other Australians on the V.P. list.
Died at Athelney, on Friday, Alfred Hughes, for-
merly manager of Kars station, in the Broken Hill
district, and brother of the late 11. W. Hughes, of
Booyoolie station.
How is this for the weaker vessel ? Merle Diener,
aged 16 and daughter of the captain of the Merle,
which recently sank in Lake Alexandrina, took the
wheel from her father while the other members of the
family were being transferred to a barge, and stuck
to it all through the storm and while the steamer
was actually sinking. Indeed, it was only when she
was practically washed out of the wheel-house that
she let go.
Artist Follen Bishop, R.8.A., has added another to
his list of successful pictures of this village and its
surroundings. This time it is “Our City Far Away,”
a view from the hills, over Adelaide and the plains, to
the sea.
C. W. Fowles, who died the other day, was a son
of the man who was in charge of the Cape Jervis
lighthouse when the Sorata went ashore, with the
late Duke of Manchester aboard. All the passengers,
including the ship’s cat, which was Persian, were
brought ashore and housed at the beacon. Subse-
quently the cat took up its residence in the bush,
and it is stated that felines with a dash of Persian
in them are now part of the fauna of the Cape.
Ruby Wilkie, a Toorak girl who can sport
a natural blush, is engaged to Edgar Lind-
say Rich, a gold-braided Englishman with
Australian relatives. They must have fixed
it up by wireless, because he is now on active
service in the Australian navy. But he was
here occasionally in the days when the navy
was a sociable, dancing, stay-at-home insti-
tution.
The Brighton Clauscens have given their
blessing to the engagement of grand-daughter
Beattie Forbes to Ellis Richards, a clever
young scientist whose dad was a well-known
Bananaland mining man. The fiance is a
Bendigonian now.
Mrs. Charles Fairbairn arrived in London
in time to give away her handsome tawny-
thatched daughter Esther in marriage to
Colonel Wheatley, D.S.O. The wedding took
place in London last week, but the breakfast
was clouded by the news from France that
the second son, Lieut. J. Fairbairn, of the
Flying Corps, was missing. All friends wish
her good news of the lad.
Prospective grandmother Melba has sent
from ’Frisco some of the most exquisite
baby-clothes ever seen in Australia. Other
infant garments of costly, misty quality are
being made by the Ladies’ Work Association
of Melbourne. Which reminds me that the
clever organiser of this workshop, Mrs.
McDevitt, was nearly killed in a motor
accident lately. She lias gone to Queens-
land to recuperate.
Irma Caron is going to Java for the sea-
son. A Dutch entrepreneur came here
searching for talent to entertain race visi-
tors, heard Irma and was conquered. Now
the young person is packing up for the
concert job. She gives a musical farewell
to Melbourne at the Masonic Hall on Thurs-
day,. March 29, with Biddy English (accom-
panist), Jessie Masson and flautist Amadio
helping.
Jennie Brenan came home from Colombo
in fear and trembling. The boat preceding
the one she travelled by was sunk when only
a few hours out, and her ship slipped away to
sea in a haze of rumors and fears. All the
passengers slept in their clothes, with life-
belts clutched in their hands, for several
nights. Hostess Rosie Musgrove saw her
off and did not return at once to the tea-
estate, in case of a prompt, shipwrecked
return. But, after all, they had a quite
uneventful trip.
_ Manager “Greg,” who is producing the
big film “Intolerance,” is a brother of Grif-
fiths, the maker of the monster. The
screen-name was evidently taken to avoid a
clash of Griffins —I mean Griffiths!

“T.F.M.” : “Leo Lear” (B. 15/2/’l7) is
right in saying that the Austins of Barwon Park
were not actually the first to bring rabbits into Vic-
toria ; but he may dismiss the notion that the credit
of doing so belongs to the man and woman who, as
he says, landed at Geelong- in 1839. According- to
the award of a primitive Arbitration Court, which
sat at Melbourne in May, 1836, to settle certain dis-
putes between the rival houses of Batman and Fawk-
ner (the Montagus and Capulets of the infant
settlement), it would appear that bunny first arrived
in Victoria in 1835 under the auspices of Johnny
Fawkner. The judgment of the Court was to this
effect
We award in the dispute between Mr. Ilenrv
Batman and Mr. John Pascal Fawkener on the
hist claim, thirty shillings; on the second claim,
nothing, although a strong presumption is in our
minds that some hasty expressions of Mr. Batman
may have led Bullet [a Sydney aborigine] to destrov
the rabbits; on the third claim, damage five shillings
and a fine of twenty shillings; and in the fourth
claim, nothing, as it does not appear that Mr. Bat-
man set the dogs on the calf. We cannot omit re-
marknig that There has been a degree of forbearance
on ,t' le P al t Mr. hawkener highly gratifying to us,
and, if generally practised, very conducive to the
general good.— A. Thomson, John Aitkkv j* V es
Simpson. '
“Wancli” : As a matter of fact Bunny
came out with the First Fleet, and with careful
nursing by the authorities established himself on the
Parramatta. lie was included in stock returns every
year, each grazier being required to state, among
other live-stock, approximately how many rabbits lie
possessed. The happiest man on the river was the
one who had most cottontails on his estate. For a
long while it was feared that the creature wouldn’t
take to Australia, and it was only by unremitting-
care that the pioneers succeeded in establishing
warrens in their paddocks. Up to about 1880 bunny
was kept as a pet by farmers and pastoralists. Then
of a sudden he was anathema. He had become
established beyond eradication, and was a rapidly-
advancing- plague. Soon it was compulsory to pursue
and kill him continuously. He was a disaster, an
abomination, a nightmare, a national calamity. But
he is still going strong, and by the time the asinine
methods of destroying him have been put a stop to all
the insectirorous birds will have been wiped out and
the country will be swarming with flics and cater-
pillars.
* * * »
The Rook” : The largest congregation of
ibis I ever came across was about 18 years ago on
the flats near the Murray, 25 miles above the South
A us. border. The rookery covered an area of about
naif a square mile, and during the incubating season
theie weie thousands of brown, white and any other
species that existed, of all ages and sizes. The
nests were made among the grass tussocks and rushes,
and were so plentiful that the old people were bump-
ing each other for space like politicians for limelight.
Goodness only knows how each pair knew their own
little plantation, but I suppose they had some dis-
tinguishing mark.

teamsters with their waggons and 30 horses were
camped for dinner at “The Weatherboard,” now
Wentworth Falls, Bine Mountains (N.S.W.). In
those days there ’were no railways beyond Parra-
matta. Seeing a storm approaching the carriers
commenced to cover up. their loads, when a flash of
lightning struck the camp. livery man and horse

A very fair arrowroot can be
obtained from the nuts (orange-red when ripe) of
the burrawang, common in many coastal districts of
N. S. Wales. The aboriginals’ method was to break
up the iipe nuts and expose them in openwork
baskets to the flow of a stream. Result, a jelly-like
mass resembling cooked arrowroot. The flour from
the ground-up fruit was used by the blacks, and it is
still employed occasionally by bushmen. Bush rats,
too, appreciate the nuts, and" drag them into their
holes > while our aggressive friend the bulldog ant
often decorates his mound with them. The rotted
butts of burrawangs make first-rate humus for
gardens. 'those who find the burrawang nut disagree
"ith them are advised to try the wooden pear or
the gummy geebung, luxuries commonly available in
burrawang country. If neither of these prove satis-
factory, you'll just have to put up with steak and
onions.
“Bad Australian”: “Mitchell Grass” (B.
14/0/’l6) has the right grip on the lariat when he
observes that the best that can be said for its general
use is that it is cheap. Even that concedes too much.
The lariat was invented for use in open country
where yards could not be built for lack of proper and
sufficiently-cheap materials. The lasso in the hands
of those who swing it most is not cheap owing to
the large number of animals killed or maimed by its
use. Even the most careful vaquero or gaucho
cannot help “cruelling” a wild range animal when
getting in his work in the open where the animal
has room to make trouble; and as for the careless
and brutal operators, I have seen hundreds of
animals—horses, mules and cattle—killed or crip-
pled by that kind, and that kind is the rule rather
than the exception. There is no place to safely
handle wild animals like a good yard and crush. If
these had been readily available the lariat would
never have been divorced from its legitimate func-
tion—jerking the bandido and liorSe-thief to King-
dom Come. As a jerker to Kingdom Come the
lariat is unparalleled.

“S.S.”: The man stood at his back
door and whistled twice, whereupon a powerful, dark-
complexioned tyke, half-retriever, half-collie, dashed
out of the grateful shade of the deserted pig pen,
jumped at the latch, sprung it dexterously, shoul-
dered the big gate wide open, and scampered off up
the paddock. Presently he came into view again
behind a herd of co\to. Meanwhile the boss had
seated himself on the grass with his back to a post,
and was slowly churning a pipe of tobacco. The
dog brought the cows up to the gate, weeded out two
or three, and headed off half a dozen heifers. “How
the deuce can he tell which are not wanted ?” I
asked. ‘Dunno,” replied the man on the grass.
The dog drove the cows through into the milking
yard, closed and relatched the gate, and smai'tly
schooled seven cows into seven bails. Then he
trotted down and barked at the tired man on the
grass. “Jimmy Gee! But that’s a clever dog,” 1
said. The man on the grass shook his head wearily.
Dunno,” he replied in hopeless accents, “the swine
couldn’t milk one of ’em to save his life. I suppose
I gotter get up.”

I ick” : Talking of animal longevity, can
anyone tell what limit of age may keep the under-
taker from laying his hold upon a goose? At Thor-
wood, near Bungonia (N.S.AV.’), the other day I
attended the funeral of an old bird that had been
the property of three generations of the one family.
He was known to have been a' least 62 years old
and was related to the goose that raised trouble in
our boarding-house. The star boarder wiped the
perspiration from his brow and laid down the
carvers. “May I ask where you got this bird,
Madame?” he said to the landlady. “Oh, yes,”
gushed Mrs. Hashalott, “my son shot it.” “Ah,”
say s his jills, “he must have had a very powerful
pom-pom.

STEVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He lies beneath the Syrian sand,
Away from all the tracks he knew ;
And now there's grass on the Paroo,
And heavy rains make fat the land.
'lhree miles across in roaring- flood
The Warrego comes rolling down,
And word goes forth from town to town
Of cattle bogged in seas of mud.
The black duck throng the brimming dams,
White pelicans come soaring east
And crows in legions make a feast
On floating carcases of lambs.
On plains that once were powdered dust
Knee-deep in lakes the mailman rides
With water pouring down bis sides,
. And takes the swollen fords on trust.
This year a great unrest will fall
On all who’ve known the Road’s decrees
Or felt a horse between their knees.
I wonder will he hear the call!
He was a man: lean-built, well-knit,
No braver rode on camp or track,
An artist on a horse's back,
A comrade when the fire was lit.
*
Whatever change the years may bring
They’ll never see his like again
At riding races on the plain
Or shooting wood-duck on the wing.
1 hough men may come and men may go
They’ll never find a man to beat
For pluck and horsemanship his feat
That morning on the old plateau.
In scrubs too thick to canter through
Some mavericks had their hiding-place,
Led by a lordly beast whose pace
Would beat a flying kangaroo.
It: swept through timber like a bird,
And on some quiet, moonlit night
The men would see a streak of white,
And fear would seize the sleeping herd.
This phantom beast had long defied
The hardiest dogs and horsemen tough,
And no one could get near enough
To send a bullet through its hide.
To Steve at last the challenge came.
He laughed and turned their jests aside,
But grinning faces stung his pride
And forped him to the reckless game.
At break of day the tracks he found,
And racing like a man possessed
He cut the leader from the rest,
And forced it to the open ground.
Where miulga stakes are spears of death
And emu-bush grew thick and rank
He stuck like plaster to its flank
Until it reeled with sobbing breath,
Red blood upon its glossy coat;
Then when its strength was nearly spent,
Across his saddle-bows he leant
And tied his scarf around its throat,
And sent it swinging back alone.
A scarf, a silken scarf was all
He flourished at Life’s carnival—
The oidy brand he called his own.
He lies beneath the Syrian sand,
At here from the sky a brassy sun
Blinks down unmoved on battles won:
I wonder can ho understand
How green his country looks to-day,
How sweet have grown the ways he knew,
With grass waist-high on the Paroo
And flowers along the Castlereagh!
I wonder will he wake and stir
A\ hen two by two the drovers pass
Through leagues of golden barley-grass,
With rattling bit and jingling spur.
Or hoar on quiet Syrian nights
The night-horse on its ceaseless tramp
Around the sleeping cattle-camp,
While faintly sounds a far-off bell.
He gave his life ungrudgingly,
But if Death makes his spirit free
I think it must come back to dwell
Amid the ways it loved so well.
VICTORIA.
VANCE PALMER.

"Werrimee”: The hornet loves to build
its mud cells in the corners of drawers and open
cupboards, under shelves and behind curtains, over
doors and windows and in the top corners of rooms.
As. each cell is completed the builder deposits eggs
therein, and crams the cavity with spiders or a
miscellany of grubs, each of which it paralyses by
Planting its sting - in a particular spot. If they were
killed outright they would be useless for food by
the time the young hornet was ready for breakfast,
then the cell is carefully capped, arid the next one
proceeded with. A skilful worker is the hornet.
Each bit of mud is well moulded and rolled into
little pellets before being carried home. The insect
is his own bric-kmaker, bricklayer, hodcarrier, mortar-
mixer and architect, and he shows himself a finished
and painstaking tradesman in all departments.
“Billy T.’/: Lamb’s house was at the
foot of Winter Hill, and about 50 yards from his
front door was a tunnel which a hopeful and ener-
getic prospecting party had put in many years
ago. This tunnel was Lamb’s sanctuary. Three
of us were working a patch of surface dirt at the
creek below, and in five weeks we arrived at a
thorough understanding of the course of events in
the Lamb household. When we heard a shrill out-
burst of voices, and the crack of a plate smashing
against the chimney bricks, we looked to see Henry
Lamb burst from the front door and dart for the
hill at his most vigorous hobble, with Mary Lamb
in hot pursuit brandishing a broom handle, an iron
ladle, a stewpan or any weapon she might have
snatched at in the heat of debate. If luck was-
with Lamb, he dived into the tunnel and was lost
to view in the subterranean gloom. Sometimes
Mrs. Lamb followed in a few paces, but it was as
dark as the illimitable pit 30 yards in; and there
were tributary, drives, it was useless to pursue
Henry further. As a rule Mary contented herself
with hurling a few stones into the void, accom-
panied by many bitter and unladylike reflections
on Henry and Henry’s mother and all Henry’s
blood relations. An hour or so later Lamb would
reappear, very cautiously, and return by slow
stages to his duties as a tiller of the soil. If
Henry missed sanctuary by a hair’s breadth, which,
as he was rheumaticky, happened twice, it was
Heaven help him, and Heaven didn’t. “An angry
woman is hell on a horse,” runs the only Australian
proverb. She is worse on a husband. : This
domestic heresy hunt was the only amusement we
had in eight months at Winter,Hill; yet we were
sorry for the Lamb that Mary had. One day there
was the usual pursuit, but this time Mary followed
in and did not reappear. From the tunnel came
reports of a fearful belaboring, and the yells of
a strong man in his agony. We chased up, and fol-
lowed on. In the gloomy interior, about 15 yards
along, Mary was walking round her Lamb with an
enormous Kaffir mealie spoon that Henry had been
so unfortunate as to secure at the Boer war. She
was basting the life out of the hapless man. “Run,

L Fou’ : An insect I met among young
messmate timber in Gippsland (Vic.) must be near
the limit in protective coloring, shape and motion.
Known as “stick beetle,” it is built something on
the lines of the praying mantis, but is lankier.
My largest specimen was 18in. long. Of the exact
brown of the gum stems it lives among, its cylindri-
cal body is like a straightish twig or curled bit of
bark, with twiglet leg's branching off. It has a
casual ■ habit of standing round with its legs in
odd, angular attitudes, so that it seems no more
symmetrical than any other bit of wood. It has
no head or tail to speak of. When it walks it pro-
ceeds slowly and at the same time sways its body
fiom side to side like something blowing in the
wind, so that you hardly notice it is going forward.
I never heard it make a noise, but if it did, on the
strength of its other performances, I bet it would
make a rustle like a gumleaf. The next-hardest thing
to see is an insect pest that comes on mint. About
t\vice as big as rose aphis, and the exact purple of
mintstalk, it folds its wings square like a mint-
stalk and, sending- out a good mint smell, lies low
on the stalk and says nothing- while you peer and
wonder what’s become of the leaves that ought to
hate waited for lamb and geeon peas.
Nick Bottom”: None of the ban-ackers
fot Australian tannin-bearing substances seem to have
anything to say for ironbark. Yet this hide is specially
valuable for tanning fishing-nets and the like. Tweed
River (N.S.W.) fishermen use it in preference to
mangrove bark because it makes the net practically
pioof against salt-water rot. The method of 'pre-
pai ing the tan is simple. The bark is chopped into
small pieces and boiled in a tan-pot for about three
hours. 'Then the bark is taken out of the pot, the
Are extinguished and the dry net immersed in the
boiling liquid. It is allowed to remain in tan over
night and then taken out and dried. I have also
seen sails and tent-flies treated by this process; but
the resulting color, a dark brown, is reckoned a
drawback. Most people prefer the golden brown of
the she-oak, or the slightly darker mangrove tan,
ignoring the greater w-eather-resisting properties of
ironbark in their desire for a more effective color
scheme.
“Truthful James”: “Eryander,” “E.S.S.”
and other sages who have a weakness for the investi-
gation of the life, length, habits, etc., of the centi-
pede, should visit Ardlethan (N.S.W.). Armies of
these crawlers inhabit the drains and sand-dumps
near the batteries. Ten-ineh specimens are not
uncommon. An inquisitive pup came in conflict with
a big one the other day, and we had a defunct mon-
grel 20min. after hostilities ceased. I dissected a
9in. brute, and found in his carcase 137 grains of
sand and one grain of tin. I reckon these beasts
have devoured the enormous tin deposits that were
discovered at Ardlethan about four years ago.
“D.F.” : If an yone wants to try a cheap
and all-Australian cure for indigestion let him chew
the bark of the white wattle (Acacia dealbata). There
are bushmen in the southern part of Tasmania who
swear by this remedy, and in particular a most vera-
cious citizen of Dunalley claims that his long-stand-
ing and obstinate case of the disease has given way
in a marvellous manner since ho acquired the chewing
habit a few months ago.
“Wart” : Allow me to introduce my
family of aerial acrobats and loop-the-loop artists,
they are a family of fantail fly-catchers known
locally as cranky fans. The family consists of four
young birds and the two old ones. (This is the
second brood the old birds have reared this season).
Their nest is in a gumtree just at the end of my
verandah, and is one of the neatest pieces of bird
architecture I know’ of. It is shaped likb a wine-
glass broken off, the outside covered with spider-web
and the inside carefully lined with horsehair. The
eggs were a light buff with dark brown blotches. The
young birds have been taking their first flying lesson
the last day or two. They sit on the end of a limb
while the old birds beat round the leaves to start an
insect, and then we have an aerial coursing match.
Ihe four set out in chase of the fly: one moment
they go full speed ahead with tail straight out
behind and closed like one feather ; the next moment
the tail is opened out as a fan and used as a brake
or rudder, and the owner is performing spirals,
dives, circles, loop-the-loops and many other tricks
impossible to most birds. When the insect is cap-
tured, which is every time, they all come back to
the limb, and if, as is usually the case, it has been
caught by an old bird, the youngsters all vibrate
their wings and cheep until it is handed over. And
then the whole performance begins again.
\\ illie \Y: One of my best friends is
the big black garden spider whose job in fine
weather is to spin a lovely gossamer at night across
the garden path from an apricot tree on one side
to a rose bush on the other. From cricket-chirp
till the moon is well up lie weaves his trap, and
there is no guesswork about his pattern. Yet I don’t
think he takes in any of the standard knitting
periodicals. If the day promises to be warm ho
gets to work like smoke, and has the thread coiled
into his innards in a marvellously short time. But
if the sky is cloudy he loafs on the job, and seems
to be wirelessing messages to me as to what he
would like for the evening gorge. After the inner
pattern of the lace work has been wound in, the
spinner glides up to the topmost branch of th.e
apricot lookout, and in a twinkling the longer foun-
dation threads are brought in, and the garden pathway
is clear to friend and foe alike. When I get to
know him better I’m going to ask him why he takes
in his web every night instead of letting it brave
the breeze like other master builders of his race.
“Fast” : A fox-terrier belonging to a
Benalla (Vic.) resident very strenuously disappeared
"hen chasing a rabbit. It was a real vanishing
trick, for although diligent search was made for «
couple of days no trace of the animal could be dis-
covered. The other morning a neighbor of the owner
saw the hindquarters of what looked to be a deg
protruding from a log. Assuring himself that the
poor beast was alive, he liberated it with the help of
an axe. Sure enough, it was the lost terrier. It had
been a prisoner for 11 days without food or drink.
Though a bit lean, next day it was as well as ever.
“Ivuku Kuku" : The old Papuan resident
settled “down South’’ for good, away from * coral
•eefs and palms, remembers New Guinea with liking,
hut strangely seldom calls his house after anything
in the Territory. I have heard of an “Ela (Port
Moresby) and “Hanuabada” (Port Moresby’s native
village), but of no others, and none in any case from
the eastern end of the country. Here are a few
suggestions:—
Yegunuma—My house. Marana —Sunshine.
Lorona—-A good thing. Siai—Bird of Paradise.
Xumaloro—Fine house, Roni—Peace.
good house. Sana la—A storehouse.
Kaioni—Greeting. Ealasi—North-west wind.
They have a language for every district in Papua.
My cooky boy, who is nothing special in the way of
a linguist, speaks five fluently, and I know of a
Government interpreter (native) who speaks 18. But
Suau, from which the above are taken, is the cur-
rency over a very large area of the country.
“A.S.H.” : There’s a mistaken idea that
once a dingo has chewed a young beast nothing will
save it. For a time 1 believed the yarn, and let
several calves die, but recently when a poddy was
badly mauled I washed out the wounds (one of which
was a hole as big as one’s fist) with a solution of
permanganate of potash, and inserted some fine linen
soaked in carbolic oil. After a few days I repeated
the treatment and persisted for the next three weeks,
adding an occasional syringing with a solution of
phenyl, then applying Stockholm tar to discourage
flies. Result, calf is busy growing into good beef.
* * * #
“Haka”: Maorilaud’s champion all-round
cocky hails from the Mount Peel district. Some
time ago he acquired a small grazing run and 1800
jumbucks for the purpose of growing wool. Labor
being scarce, he acted as bis own cook, offsidfer and
musterer. He undressed the mob himself, 80 being
the lowest tally put through the porthole in one day,
did his own classing, pickin’-up, rolling and baling
and handed his war profits over to the patriotic
funds. If anyone is desirous of lending him a hand
his present address. is somewhere in front of the
German lines.
-X- * * # j
“Salex”: At Sackville (Ilawkesbury
River) there is a cave upon the walls of which
are hand-prints, ranging in size from a baby’s np to
a man’s. These prints are black and are embedded
in the rock face. As to liow they originated there
is much doubt, (Incidentally, there is much doubt
about the origin of most things, including mer-
maids.) On the top of the cliff near the. cave
referred to, there is a flat rock once carved in
squares similar to a. chessboard, and the late James
Holmes informed me that black brother used to
play a game there, somewhat similar to draughts.
There is no trace of the board now, the sandstone
having weathered away.

Test of Motor Tyres
HEN Balmain Bros. (Bega) Ltd., of N.S.W.,
instituted the Cooma-Bega Motor Mail Service,
they decided to test out the various makes
of Tyres, irrespective of nationality and price
—with a view to the equipment of their fleet.
THE AUSTRALIAN WHITE TREAD
DUNLOP
demonstrated its superiority by test against the leading im-
ported makes. Result—Dunlops were selected and fitted to
the fleet of heavy 35 h.p. Mail Cars, which besides passengers
invariably carry upwards of 2 tons of postal matter daily
over some of the most mountainous country in N.S.W. It’s
a route that soon demonstrates the merits of a tyre, its
strength and its dependability under the severest stresses.
In the first test “Dunlops” won —that’s 12 months ago. How
they lived up to that test is told in the following letter
The Manager, Dan lop Rubber Co. Ltd.
Dear Sir,—As we have now been shoeing our cars
almost exclusively with Dunlop Grooved White Tread
Covers for a period of 12 months we consider that
We are due to place on record our appreciation of
the good honest service which these tyres are
giving us. We know the mileage which each tyre
on each car has completed, owing to the fact
that we have embodied in our business system a
daily statistical record completed from drivers ’
daily reports, which must tally with the finan *
aal records of our business . In short, we can
talk facts about the Dunlop White Tread
Cover. On our Cooma-Bega mail cars, equip-
ped with 880 x 1.20 Dunlop Grooved White
Tread pneumatic covers (twin on rear
wheels), we exceed the average of 3,000
miles even on these heavy cars. Some of
the covers attain between 4,000 and 5,000
miles. Exceptional service when one realises
that we run a daily mail service up and down
Brown Mountain, rising more than 3,500 ft.
in a distance of 11 miles, climbing the whole
distance. The balance of our rolling stock con-
sists of heavy cars to carry passengers ( 8).
These are shod with standard sized covers, and we
have at present one tyre with its tread still perfect
after having completed a distance of 6,214 miles
as follows — 5,158 on rear wheel; 1,056 on front
wheel. Our services cover a distance of 160,000
miles per annum , and it can be readily understood
that we can only use the tyre which proves itself to be
the “ best miles for money” proposition, regardless of
the original cost price of the tyre. Fortunately for
ourselves we have found that the Dunlop is the only
possessor of virtues which go to make an economical
proposition, as its first cost is cheaper than most other
tyres on the market, and its mileage record equals the more expensive tyres now competing with it. You
are at liberty to publish any of the information supplied herein, as any excerpt from this letiei can be
authenticated by an examination of our office records. Yours faithfully,
BALMAIN BROS. (BEGA) LTD .
& & & W. H. BALM AIN, Managing Director.
All Motorists can have just the same satisfactory service, and at a running
cost per mile that no imported tyre can approach. There’s no need to send
your money out of the country for tyres —“Dunlops” will do everything
that is promised for imported tyres, and do it cheaper!
