Business, Bobbery, etc
“Wattle Bark”: “Barkers” earn every
penny of the 30s. or 355. a ton they get tor
stripping the black wattle of its overcoat;
for it is about the toughest of the many
tough jobs in the bush. The barker’s tools
are the stripping bar, a bit of round iron
about 35 inches long with a knob on one
end and a point on the other —a cross
between a chisel.and a spoon. It looks easy
to gouge the bark off the butt of the tree
with the bflr, but it is not as easy as it.
looks, and mostly you have to haul it off
with your hands. Stripping the limbs is
worse "work, with the addition that you have
to climb like a monkey and hang on with
your feet while you work with the bar and
your hands. You rip your clothes and your
skin getting up and down the trees, and it
there are any weak spots or cuts on your
hands the rough edges of the bark soon find
them out. Some barkers cut the trees down
to save climbing, but it doesn’t finish the job
any sooner, and the wattle-tops make such
an unholy mess on the ground that many
run-holders discourage the plan. As black-
wattle bark is worth £6 or £6 10s. per ton
there is good money in the business just
now. I have seen a good tree give well
over a cwt. of bark. If the trees are prime
and the bark is running well a good man
can get off over two tons a week. But at
best it isn't easy money.
* * * *
“0.K.”: Melbourne’s gas monopoly has
been safeguarded by the local politicians for
nearly 40 years.- As far back as 1870 a
select committee advocated a transfer of the
business to municipal control, but Private
Enterprise has managed to keep the reform
at a distance ever since. Five years ago a
Board fixed the standard price of gas at
4s. and the standard dividend at 10 per
cent.; but thus far the Peacock Government
has let this: household matter lip. The some-
what alarmed co. reduced its price to 4s. 4id.
when a larger reduction was threatened ; but
when the danger cloud had blown over it
raised the price to 4s. 7d. One of the Mel-
bourne suburban councils has formally pro-
tested against this further imposition, and
if the public is not top tired to combine for
its own protection the co. can be made to
sit up. It is fallaciously supposed that a
gas co. can do as it likes with its customers;
but if all the householders, with one accord,
met their next gas account with a refusal
to pay more than the standard price fixed
by the Board, it is certain that the co. would
not proceed to stop its business by cutting
everybody off at the meter.
Probably Justice a’Beckett (Vic.) meant
what he said the other day. but the sharks in
court merely grinned. His Honor was re-
proving a husband and wife who appealed
against a magistrate’s order against them
jointly for goods supplied. “Never mind
Lord Halsbury’s decisions,” he said, “they
are good friends of the lawyers. The sensi-
ble course would have been for the husband
to pay tin* money.” Then ’s Honor recol-
lected' that he was a lawyer and not a
sensible man. and upheld the point of
law taken. Also he “certified for costs in the
application.” That was why the sharks
grinned.
Shackleton’s ship, the Aurora, has been
sold to English buyers for a little over
£lO,OOO. Gear sold at auction in Welling-
ton (M L.) brought in another £9OO. The
old ship was a whaler before she was com-
mandeered for Polar work, and it is likely
that she will soon be away again nosing for
white water.
“Mnrkah”: “Bustler” (B. S/3/’l7) casts
aspersions on my knowledge of accumulators
in connection with the electric lighting of
trains. His first objection, re the evil effects
of possible over-charging, is easily re-
moved, for the Edis n cell which he men-
tions cannot be over-charged. Therefore
excessive train speed would not lead to the
over-charging of batteries. But conceding
“Rustler’s” point, it wouldn’t require “elab-
orate arrangements” to meet the trouble.
Auto, cut-out switches and over-load releases
are very simple affairs. “Rustler” ascribes
injurious effects to jolting. The accumula-
tors carried at sea in connection with wire-
less take no harm from years of battering.
Neither do those used in electric delivery-
cars, and they experience more jolting in a
_ week than would come their way in a year
on passenger trains. The fact that the
electric delivery-cars which are becoming so
common in our cities carry, in addition to
their load, accumulators that supply suffici-
ent energy to not only light them but also
drive them makes “Rustler’s” argument
that the weight of the apparatus is too great
look silly.
“Lee-oh”: The Norwegian barque Blen-
heim. just sunk by the Huns —without warn-
ing. as per Hun custom —was one of the very
last of the old-timers in the London-M.L.
trade. Forty years ago, when she made her
first run across the Roaring ’Forties to Auck-
land, she was a. full-rigged ship and her
name was the Wanganui. She was one of
the fleet of Sunderland-built iron ships
turned out for the N.Z. Shipping Co., and
when she got a decent quartering breeze
there were few windbags in this trade that
had the legs of her. When the Shaw-Savill
people bought her they changed her name to
Blenheim, and when the Scowegians got hold
of her and the old red ensign was hauled
down a Frederikstad firm set her a-lumber-
ing. There can’t be many of the old fleet
afloat now. One of the handful is the Agda,
c.v Waitangi, in her day a beautiful main-
skysail-yarder, now hauling piles and pit
props somewhere in the North Seas. Another
is the Euterpe, though they don’t call her
that now. She was one of Shaw-Savill s
clipper passenger-carriers, and Captain Tom
Bowling, now retired at Christchurch
(M.L.). was one of the skippers that trod
her poop. The splendid old liner has been
razee'd to barque rig. her classic name has
been altered to Star of India, and a Cap’n
Christiansen or a Svenson works her up and
down between Alaska and ’Frisco in the
salmon-packing biz.
The shuffling of the P. and O. arrange-
ments throughout Australasia has caused a
shudder to run down the spines of the local
stall's. The British India Co., for which
Macdonald. Hamilton and Co. act as general
agents, will take control. The live P. and O.
offices will be scrapped, in Melbourne the
A.U.S.X. Co. will be the local agents, both
companies being directed from one office.
The top dogs, such as Gordon Wesche and
Plummer, will have ail interest in the com-
bine. The frigid P. and O. manner has been
kept alive by beautiful imported young men;
and there will be shrill yelps of agony when
they have to sell their first tickets for a
Bay trip.
* - * * *
“D.F.” : Some of the timber used in the
Electrolytic Zinc Co.’s works at Risdon
(Tas.) is another object-lesson in the devel-
opment and conservation of our great
natural resources. A lot of the beams and
uprights are oregon, and manager Gepp says
they are used, not because the U.S.A. timber
is preferred to Australian hardwood, but
simply because decently-seasoned- hardwood
could not be got in time for the job. As a
matter of fact, the oregon is .a heap dearer
than the local wood and not as strong, but
the Tasmanian sawmillers are just like the
State Government —they live from hand to
mouth.
* * * #
“McSalt”: “McNorth” (B. 8/2/T7)
doesn’t know everything about the Inver
line. I sailed on the Inveresk and the Inver-
clyde, also on the ill-fated luverlochy that
piled herself up on the Victorian coast in
3902, and 1 can make an affidavit that so
far from being graceful vessels, built with
a special eye to speed, they were mostly
“Russell’s Coffins” built by the mile on the
Clyde and cut off in lengths to suit pur-
chasers, with a special eye to the economical
carriage of coal. After being in the old
Macquarie, which could and did travel, to
say nothing of the speedy Harbinger, the
Inverclyde was something like a canal barge.
At earning dividends for her Aberdeen
owners, the Inverclyde, which was originally
a stump-topgallant-mast barque and any-
thing but graceful, could no doubt give
points to the Macquarie; but when it came
to travelling, the Macquarie could sail rings
round her. As for “McNorth s” yarn about
the steamer that the . Inveresk ran a dead-
heat with, he doesn’t tell us what sort of
a steamer it was, and he should have men-
tinned that the barque was in ballast and on
the particular bit of this world’s snfface
that suits a windjammer better than any
other. If a packet can’t travel in ballast
running the Easting down, she’ll never get
a move on at all. The one good word I can
say for the Inver boats is that they didn t
feed you too badly, though they made up for
it by under-manning and extracting more
work per shellback than anything afloat, bar
a Not a Scotiainan.
* * *
Just before the war Sydney Harbor Trust
constructed a new wharf at Miller s 1 oint
to accommodate vessels like the Ceramic
and Niagara, and even bigger things. A
week or so ago the costly berth was accom-
modating a four-masted Yankee lumber
schooner. That and nothing more. If any-
one had asked old R. R. P. Hickson, ex-
president of the Trust, how long it would
be before his new wharf would berth a
Yankee lumber schooner he would have
leaped four feet and called for a strait
jacket.
* * *
“4217 Gunner”: Australia makes and
takes the biscuit. One day the bread ration
was missing and we got an issue of Aus-
tralian biscuits instead. They were so good
that the whole ammunition column hopped
out of its dugouts and kangarooed down the
hill to the quarter-master’s home to see it
there were any more. We preferred our own
country’s biscuits to the bread, and the
bread was good.
THE LANDMARK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the mark of our history,
And token of our progression;
Pyramid of our mystery,
And warrant of our possession ;
Here in the ranges, a sentinel sign,
Poppet-heads of a ruined mine.
It rose in adventure’s hope and fear,
An Argo-ship for a quest of gold,
Handiwork of the pioneer—
Footprint left by the men of old;
There in the sunlight, romance's sign,
Poppet-heads of an olden mine. •
Relic of courage and industry—
This is the badge of our nationhood,
Parchment-stamp of our liberty,
Rickety fabric oi crumbling wood :
Worn of the sun and frost and wind,
Cracked and splintered and rusty-pinned.
Blazon it strong on our standard high:
Sound we its fame with loud insistence:
“This is the sign we have conquered by,
This is the reason of our existence!”
Mystic structure, heroic fame —
Poppet-heads of an ancient claim.
Over the ridges stern and grim,
Stands it starkly against the sky,
Shadowy -seeming as day grows dim,
Fading ghostly from sight of eye;
Gaunt and lonely, a spectral sign—
Poppetheads of a perished mine!
N.S.W.
JAS. P. McENROE.
THE WAY TO TOWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Up Woolcott-street to Darlinghurst
The noisy trams swing past,
From early morning when tlie “first”
Links ciosely with the “last.”
Now' past a home of other days
With dust-grimed skirts of green;
Here soulless eyes of tall flats gaze,
There terrace-houses lean.
Past all the shops of William-street,
Now open sky and park,
Good green things for the eye to meet,
And memory hears the lark
That sang and soared for you and me
One sweet boronia time;
A single hand-touch then was fee
To set the earth a-chime.
Grey, mother opposite, I know
Your brave eyes, stricken numb,
Vision the babe of long ago,
Now buried by the Somme.
The actor closes up his score,
And for a moment lives
The triumphs that he knew of yore.
To all the green space gives
Some message but to her who sits
Demure with wind-blown hair,
With rounded cheek where lurks and flits
A dimple here and there.
Of stiff park trees what need has she
To steal a wistful glance,
While youth and beauty make her free
The green fields of romance?
Up Woolcott-street the loud trams beat
With never pause or wait;
Through Darlinghurst, down William-street
They bear their human freight;
And some are sad while some are gay,
But all are thrall to town. ''
Grey William-street where night and day,
The trains ply up and down!
N.S.W.
JOHN EGAN.

The World’s Viewpoint on the Hupmobile
BECAUSE motor-car buyers have learned keenly to
judge values, the world-wide approval of the Hupmobile
is both interesting and important.
The car that wins everywhere the high favor accorded to the
Hupmobile must be the best in its class and the Hupmobile
is that.
It must be comfortable to the point of luxury—and the
Hupmobile is that also.
It must afford the maximum in roominess, convenience and
easy riding—as the Hupmobile does.
IT must be fast in the getaway, flexible and easily handled,
powerful for speed and for the hills and heavy roads,
silent and smooth-running.
And the Hupmobile is all these to an unusual degree.
Last, it must be economical to run and to keep—and Hup-
mobile reputation on that score is flawless.
Any Hupmobile dealer is ready to submit the car to any
reasonable demonstration you may require to convince you
—as thousands already know—that it outpoints any car in
its class.

POLITICAL
POINST
The Win-the-War party is merely trying
to bluff the peoples that it is a party. The
men who are running it know better. That
of course is why Hughes and Cook each
fought for a majority of members in the
Ministry. And that is also why Hughes has
been struggling and scheming for direct
representation on the Senate ticket. Neither
section trusts the other; each is fighting for
its own hand. Hughes wants to be able to
hold the .whip over the other lot —wants to
be able to say: “All right! You throw us
over, and so many men will follow me.
Then where’s your majority?”
The S. M. Herald makes a perfectly true
statement (March 24), which may be worth
remembering:—•
The P.L.L. is making a great mistake if it imag-
ines that that vote [on Conscription] represented any
desire to make an end of the war before complete
victory is assured. Conscriptionists and Anti-Con-
scriptionists alike, taking them by and large, have
no illusions as to what defeat in this war would
mean for us in Australia.
In other words, the S. M. Herald admits
that the Anti-Conscriptionist bus no more
illusions about what defeat would mean for
Australia than the Conscriptionist has. and
he is, in the main, just as desirous of con-
tinuing the war till complete victory is
assured. Which is a plain confession that
nine-ffenths of the claims made on behalf of
the Win-the-War Government are dishonest
electioneering claptrap.
“PHEWr
FATTY: “Gripes! Somebody's blankets are
turnin’!”
HIS MATE : “That ain’t blankets. That’s
Bluey, V.C., smoking the cigars ’is tart
sent ’im.”
If the Censorship were not controlled by
party politicians who are fighting an elec-
tion, it would drop like a thousand elephants
upon the people who, like “Thomas K.
Brown,” write such things as this in the S.
M. Herald:—
Think of this, you fathers and mothers and rela-
tives of the boys at the front. Their lives depend on
reinforcements to relieve them from too long a stretch
in the trenches. Yet these men would withhold that
succor, and penalise those who would send it. Yet
they dare ask the relatives of these brave lads to
vote for them to place them in a position where it
is possible to do them even a greater injury. There
can only be one answer to this.
The simple fact is that the Federal Labor
party while in office (that is to say, from
the deposition of Joe Cook to the retirement
of Tudor, Higgs and Gardiner, Assistant
Minister for Defence) enlisted over four-
fifths- —over eighty out of every huudred—of
the men who have got into khaki. Suppose
it were announced that the Prime Minister,
the Minister of Defence, Sir Dougins Haig,
and General Birdwood were opposed to any
more men being enlisted in Australia, would
that be “prejudicial to recruiting”? Would
that be passed? If it wouldn’t, why should
the other monstrous lie?
RETIRED BEATEN.
PRIVATE (sadly) : “Excuse me, sir, could I
have a couple of hours off —to go and
see my dying father?”
OFFICER (sternly): “Dying father? Why, I
left him not ten minutes ago!”
PRIVATE (hastily) : “Ten minutes ago! Sir,
you take the ban — lie’s been dead seven
years.”
Up to the end of last year Vic. had dis-
charged 0117 Billjims, and 112 of them —one
in every 55 or thereabouts—had been -planted
on the land. Perhaps, after all, it is just as
good a proportion as “the land,” under pre-
sent conditions, deserves. But no State
which hopes to make a success of itself can
afford to leave it at that. If present condi-
tions on the land are so unattractive, either
they will have to be altered or the State will
go broke—nothing is surer.
Listen to Prime Minister Hughes assuring
his brother-legislators that the Government
will do nothing rash in restricting the im-
portation of luxuries:—
It is well known that thousands of people are
employed in this country in the manufacture of silk
garments. If the Government prohibited the import-
ation of silk, and these people were thrown out of
work, the matter would be even more serious.
How like a Freetrader! It suggests that
scores of thousands of women would go
gownless if they couldn’t rustle around in
cheap silks, and that the very best females,
not to mention the very worst, must have
either silk undies or none at all. The ‘“thou-
sands of workers” couldn’t possibly be em-
ployed in making up materials other than
silk —Australian tweed dress stuffs, or local
woollens, for instance. No, a thousand
times, No!
They cabled to McGrath, Labor M.11.R.
of Ballarat, asking him whether he wouldn’t
join the Win-the-War party. He replied
from the trenches that he wouldn’t care to
be seen dead with that crowd. Whereupon
the Win-the-War party decided to oppose
McGrath of Ballarat. They’ll teach him to
go oft' fighting! Suppose he won the war,
what does he think they’d do for dust to
blind archbishops and other dull electors
with?
From a report in the Sydney Telegraph :
The Papal Delegate struck the table with his
stick. The clamor went on. The Archbishop raised
his hand to restore order. He was taken no notice
of.
It knocks holes through the bottom of the
story which the dailies have been encourag-
ing others to tell, without having the pluck
to tell themselves. Michael, poor fellow,
was held in the hollow of the praste’s hand;
and the praste lmd sold him to the Labor
party for promises received. That was the
story. Now the crowd says: “Here’s a
politician—and we don’t like him!” And
when Papal Delegate and Archbishop inter-
vene, the crowd brushes them aside, with:
“None of your business this, if you please.
Keep to your place, like any other citizen!”
Slain at the Assembly polls and yelping
ever since, Richard Denis Meagher, Mayor
of Sydney, ex-Speaker and former president
of the P.L.L. Richard, who used to imagine
he held the Phillip Division of Sydney “in
his pocket,” stood this time as (1) an in-
dependent Laborite, or (2) a "sympathetic
Nationalist,” according to the company he
was in. But it mattered nothing, anyway.
The inhabitants of Phillip flung him and his
cigar and his "great services to the Democ-
racy” ifito the dustbin and banged down the
lid with the weight of a 2 to 1 majority. St.
Denis fits the bin much better than he did
the Speaker’s chair.
Disgusted with the Jokook crowd for
fusing with Hughes, Givens, W. G. Spence,
Josh Thomas and others whom the Argus
lias for years taught them to hate and mis-
trust, several Liberal leagues in Victoria
contemplate running candidates of their
own. instead of accepting anybody the Win-
the-Spoils party likes to foist upon them.
When the Argus —sturdy advocate of free-
dom of thought and action—heard of this it
remarked:—•
The vote-splitter must not be treated with gentle-
ness. lie forfeits all claim to consideration by reason
of his faithlessness. No heresy-hunting will be coun-
tenanced by Liberals, neither will they attempt to
punish men for acting according to conscience; but
the vote-splitter must not blame anyone but himself
if, as the penalty of his own perfidy, he is for ever
shunned as an enemy by the organised forces opposed
to the Caucus.
Of course! The Liberal who won't allow
that Josh Thomas, for instance, is a proper
Liberal candidate when the machine says lie
is, is a scab whose life must be made a hell
for him. And all the time a little man will
go around talking about the intolerable
tyranny of the Labor “junta,” and begging
for votes as a protest against it! And the
Archbishop of Sydney pollutes his cathedral
by giving his blessing in it to a crowd as
dirty as that.
The Liberal attitude, which bears a strong
family resemblance to the Labor attitude,
has been plainly stated by the Argus after
consideration of the situation at Ilenty,
Fawkner and Echuca, where there are three
or more candidates in the field :
There is no room to-day for the independent candi-
date. A candidate is either for the Win-the-War
Government or against it; and so are all the electors.
Independence is down and out, apparently,
with Labor sitting on its chest and Lib-
Labism patriotically squeezing its weasaud
lest the miserable creature get off some silly
shriek about Freedom.
What is there to be said in defence of a
Senate elected on the block-vote system,
whereby it is possible for 1,000,000 voters to
be "deprived of all representation in the “dem-
ocratic” House of Review by 1,000,000?
Phil Belirend, a good Australian with a
name that doesn’t matter, is up agaiust
Willy Watt, at Balaclava (Vic.). Jack Cur-
tain, now in charge of the Westbalian
Worker, failed to oust Willy last time.
Belirend is an electrician, and was sent to
Cockatoo Dock, Sydney, last year to do some
special work on the cruiser Brisbane and
the hospital ship Ivaroola. He unsuccess-
fully opposed Norman Bailleau for the
cream-puff dfcronghold of Toorak at last
State elections.
DANTE UP TO DATE.
“I found me in a gloomy wood, astray, gone from the path direct.” —The Inferno.
Everything or thereabouts that is ever
done by any Government of which Mr.
Hughes is Prime Minister is done by Mr.
Hughes. For instance, Mr. Hughes bought
the Commonwealth wheat-steamers in Eng-
land ; nobody else had apparently any say
in the matter. Now, however, the Auditor-
General’s report discloses that Ministers
considered the matter this end, and that it
was W. G. Higgs, then Treasurer, who
authorised the Commonwealth Bank to hold
at the disposal of the Prime Minister in
-f i pn kaa aaa /» «-» * ■ t> oc?
London f 0 ,500,000 for the purchase of — O
steamers for the Government.
Auditor-General Israel has dug out another
instance of the Defence Department’s busi-
ness methods. A soldier deserted —in Aus-
tralia—in October, 11)14, but this little detail
jvas overlooked, and the allotment money
was paid out regularly for 13 months. Pro-
bably that is why—but, no; we mustn’t men-
tion it. It might prejudice recruiting—the
recruiting of supporters for the new Con-
fusion Ministry.
QUITE SAFE.
MISS STRIPEY : “You sailors must run ter-
rible risks in war time!”
JACK: “Oh, no. We don't get so much shore
leave now, y’know.”
llow they do fear the people! No sooner
hud the first cable arrived regarding the
boil-over in Russia than a Confusion sup-
porter in the Reps. was put up to ask if in
view of the situation in Petrograd the Gov-
ernment would "reconsider its determination
to send the Parliament to the country at
such a serious juncture.” But it couldn't
be done; and the House was shut before
the Government had time to consider
whether, in view of a really serious dog-
fight in Collarendebri, it wasn’t an act of
madness and disloyalty to plunge the coun-
try into the turmoil of a general election.
The Lutherans of South Aus., a strong
religious body founded by the German
settlers of 70 years ago, are to have all then-
schools closed, under last year’s Act, before
the end of 1017. A deputation to the Pre-
mier, who is also Minister of Education, has
asked him to stay his hand till Parlia-
ment can have a chance to reconsider. They
accept (they said) the mandate that all
teaching must be in English; but for a Gov-
ernment to insist on more would be
“bigotry.” In other words, they put it that
it is a religious question, not a national one.
But Vaughan wasn’t taking any of that.
“No,” he syid, “Australia wants citizens
who don’t segregate themselves into factions,
but are members of a full and free democ-
racy.” And then he threw at them, to knock
them off the “religious” perch, the example
of the Methodists, who don’t ask for separate
schools. Which, of course, settles it; for
anything the Methodists do in S. Aus. is
right. ' __
THE VITAL ISSUES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Mr. George Fairliairn the people of Victoria
have the choice of a candidate for the Senate who
combines the qualities essential to a legislator in
these serious times. Mr. Fairbairn is one who has
always bec-n recognised, in athletics as a ‘‘good
sport.” Both tennis and golf have been played by
him, and every amateur sport has held his whole-
hearted support.—Aitovs, 22/3/’l7.
Look on that picture and on this,
Then say which you would choose.
Of tariffs and economy
This Labor person prates, but lie
Does not set forth his views
On “goff,” or say what he will do.
To help the tennis season through.
A quaint, misguided energy
Into the fight he flings,
And talks, with tongue that will not flag,
Of senators who’re “in the hag,”
And other smellful things.
But not a word ho speaks of subs
For any of our sporting clubs!
‘‘Do you desire to win the war ?”
“What have you done for sport?”
Ask of each candidate these two
Plain questions, and you’ll soon know who
Will best hold freedom's fort.
I mean*to vote, though critics scoff,
For Fairbairn, Liberty and—“ Goff” 1

Your car, as well as your Tyres, will last
longer when Tyres are Full-Sized.
WHEN Tyres are Under-sized they must be Over-
inflated- —in order to properly carry the Load.
Over-inflated Tyres “ride roughly,” trans-
mitting excessive Vibration from every pebble
and rut in the road.
The continuous strain of high Air-pressure on the
Fabric of such Tyres, while not in evidence when the
Tyres are new, in the end seek out the weakest snots,
developed through the heat of Friction, and through con-
tinual flexing of Tyre Walls in much Mileage.
Then, we have the Early “Burst” or the Premature
Puncture—with which all Motorists are familiar.
The needless expense of these could, in large
measure, be avoided by the logical use of Tyres which are
FULL-SIZED- —large enough to carry the Load without
need for excessive Inflation.
IT may here interest the Header to know that practi-
cally ALL Millimeter Tyres made (except Goodrich
Tyres) are much UNDER-SIZED.
If he doubts this he has only to measure the cross-
section Diameters of the Tyres, when new, on his Car,
and see.
He will find, for instance, that the so-called
“SI 5 x 105 m/m” Tyres measure only 00, 97, 98. 99 or
103 millimeters in diameter when new (according to
make) instead of the 105 m/m they are branded.
Since the Walls and Treads of these Under-sized
Tyres are just as thick as if they bad been made FULL-
SIZED (or up to their specified diameters) it will be
evident that the shortage in Tyre cross-section Diameter
is entirely a Shortage of Air-capacity.
And, since Air-capacity, in proportion to weight of
Car, is what determines RESILIENCE, it will be
apparent that any deficit in Tyre cross-section Diameter
:
results in such excessive vibration as to seriously tax the
longevity of the CAR, and the Comfort of its Occupants
—out of all proportion to the amount saved in making
Tyres UNDER-SIZED instead of FULL-SIZED.
THE Manufacturers of Goodrich Tyres now break
through tradition, and build all their rubber-tread
Goodrich Tyres FULL-SIZED.
This regardless of Trade Custom, or the greater cost
of supplying full-sized Tyres to the Public at prices that
compare favorably, for similar quality, with prices which
all other Makers charge for regular Millimeter (or
Under-sized) Tyres.
Such a marked difference exists between the PER-
FORMANCE of Full-sized Tyres and thrt of Under-
sized Tyres, on the same Cars, that the Public are
expected to promptly realise it, when Full-sized Tyres are
once offered by a reliable Maker.
Volume of Sales may then be expected to compensate
for the lesser profit, per Tyre.
Goodrich Tyres will therefore be found to measure
precisely the number of millimeters, in cross section
Diameter, that their “Size-classification” pretends to
have.
They will measure this while new, and unstretched.
CAR-OWNERS are invited to use, without charge,
the Goodrich Compensating Tyre Scale (to be
found at every Goodrich Dealers), and thereby
■find out whether or not THEIR Tyres ARE
UNDER-SIZED.
A revelation in Comfort and in Economy on Mileage-
Cost then awaits them, through use of Goodrich FULL-
SIZED Tyres.
Why accept less Resilience and Longevity than would
be delivered by the Air-capacity you PAY for?
GOODRICH

TOTALISAT OR.
Racing Clubs are advised that the Turnbull
and Jones, Ltd., of New Zealand, Totali-
sator can now be seen at W. G. Watson
and Co., Ltd., 279 Clarence Street, Sydney.
Turnbull and Jones, Ltd., have supplied
Machines to all the principal clubs in New
Zealand, and their experience extends over
a period of 20 years. Mr. Seager, their
representative, will be pleased to make
appointments and supply full information.
SPOSTING
NOTIONS
Rosehill Racing Club’s Rawson Stakes
(9 furlongs, w.f.a.) run on Saturday was mainly
remarkable for the good performance registered by
Giru. There was a good field (Bombata, Lingle,
Quinologist, Harriet Graham and others ran) and
most of the money was for Cetigne, who was backed
down to 7 to 4. ' Giru was at 4’s, Melii King and
Lingle at 6’s and 7’s. Giru (9st. 21b.) beat Cetigne
(9st.) fair and square, and may perhaps have had a
little in hand. Lingle (9st.) came fast from the
lmckblocks over the last couple of furlongs and
finished third. Why the public rushed Cetigne was
not clear in view of his performance on the previous
Saturday and that he looked a trifle dull. Giru
was perhaps never better than he is just now, and
if this run cannot be taken as giving him a big
chance in tbe Doncaster Handicap with Bst. 131 b.
then form and condition go for nothing. A couple
of weeks, though, plight to put Cetigne well forward.
Quinologist ran on well for a mile. He, too, will
be a better-conditioned animal at Randwick than he
was last week..
* * * *
Of tbe horses engaged in big events
at Randwick two performed exceptionally well at
Jlosphill on Saturday—Spurn and Chantemerle,
winners of the Railway Stakes (7 furlongs) and the
Club Handicap (1 mile and 3 furlongs). Both
were backed at 3’s, a circumstance which made the
discerning individuals who mopped up 100’s to 4
from the double merchants when the early doors
opened feel happy and glad. Spurn after disquali-
fication last year was sent to the harem, but missed.
Puti into work again it seemed doubtful if she was
going to recover form, but Saturday’s gallop knocked
that idea clean over the ropes. She carried 9st. 21b.
and beat First Principle (9st. 31b.) by half a length.
Both mav be having something to say in the Don-
caster, and if First Principle is not arguing the point
somewhere near the end this page is going to bn
mildly surprised. Bonnie Plume was doing well
for six furiongs and then seemed to be satisfied that
the trip was far enough.
Chantemerle won with Bst. 131 b. in record time
for the course (2min. 201 sec.). The week had im-
proved him a lot. lie will be still brighter at Rand-
wick if all goes well. Whether he can appropriate
the -Cup is another matter. That he is a brilliant
horse admits of no doubt, but 9st. 51b. is a thump-
ing big weight to carry over two miles, and Chante-
merle had done nothing to deserve it.
* * * *
On the first day of the Rosehill meeting
the • stewards read the Riot Act to the mannikin
placed in charge of Dear Laddie and told him that
if he rode as bad a race again his name would be
Mud. On Saturday another youth was lifted aboard
this nag in the Parramatta Mile and persuaded
Dear Laddie to win from end to end. He carried
Bst. 811). and ran the distance in “411,” which was
a good enough spin to draw attention to the fact
that the horse has 7st. 71b. in the Doncaster. Dear
Laddie is well enough bred to do anything, being
by Ayr Laddie-Bright Alice, but seems to have been
annoyed that 1800 guineas was paid for him as a
yearling. He fell at Randwick a year ago and a
vet. cut away most of his lower jaw to make him
better.
* • * *
A moke that may be heard of again is the
two-year-old Prince Yiridis, the property of “Baron”
Brown, who bred him. Earlier in the year Prince
Viridis ran a good second to Lady Comely at Rand-
wick. On Saturday he landed the bacon at Rosehill
in the second Juvenile Handicap in a style which
suggested something later on. The unlucky King o’
Malt was second, and as the Prince gave him. 101 b.
tbe win was worth taking notice of. Incidentally
people prior to this race threw away their boots in
the rush to invest on Fight Ever, a recent winner
up Newcastle way. They didn’t see their coin any
more. Prince Yiridis is by the Baron’s old-time
champion Prince Foote from the imported mare La
Crosse.
* * * *
Until the Rosehill meeting finished, Ike
found business on the Doncaster and Sydney Cup as ,
lively as a drowned kitten. But there was some-
thing doing at the bargain-counter during the week,
and from this it transpired that Spurn, Giru, White-
field and Cetigne were looked on with greatest
favor by punters. There was also money for
Balarang, First Principle, King’s Bounty and Polv-
crates. Woorak has been going to a longer price,
and the stock of Bonny Plume and CPstello found ■
buyers in limited quantity. These, positions may
change a little before tbe day of the thud. Court
Jester has had most backing for the Cup. Harriet
Graham and Lingle come next.

The A..T.C. notifies that conditions and
added cash for the Spring handicaps of 1917 and
the classic events up to 1919 will remain
as heretofore, with the exception that the
Sires’ Produce Stakes nominator gets a rise in
pocket-money from £lOO to £250. The embargo (in
operation since 1913) keeping geldings out of the
Derby is lifted. Next year, therefore, colts and
geldings will struggle under the same weight (Bst.
121 b.) for the £5OOO.
. - * *
“Trojus” : How is this for a series of coin-
cidences? On a recent Saturday at Caulfield, in the
South Yarra Handicap, the AVclkin horse Three was
No. 3 on the card, drew position No. 3, and though
quoted 7-2 and 4-1 in the papers, the best price the
writer heard at the start was 3-1. Finally the nag
finished third.
* * *
“Trot”: Take the absurd two or three
hundred pounds put through the machine at Kembla
Grange and Gosford (N.S.W.) and compare with the
figures for meetings in Maoriland if you want to
have a laugh. At meetings of similar size over
vonder £BOOO would be a small trunover. There
were races at Napier Park (M.L.) the other day, and
on the first afternoon £16,385 went through the tote.
Napier Park is not rushed by millions, either. There
would be about as many people at the meeting as
attend Rosehill, and not nearly so keen a betting
crowd, as a rule.' Now let the tote be operated
at Rosehill under the existing regulations,
which make the opposition of MacShortodds compul-
sory if there is to be any racing at all. Can any-
one imagine that £16,000 would be tinkled up? I
don’t want to bet, but would be ready to lay a
dollar to 2'd. that a fourth of that sum would not be
dealt with. But there are no Ikes at Napier Paik,
and the Government doesn’t kill the machine before
it starts to do business.
If the Australian horse has a bad time in
competition with the English nag, the animal born
hereabouts does well enough in Maoriland. Biplane
and Bimeter, acquired in Australia by G. D. Green-
wood, have this season won £4500 m stakes, and
may do more when the Spring comes. Achilles, one
of the best middle-distance horses ever raced in the
Windy Isles (G. G. Stead reckoned him the best),
was bred in N. S. Wales, and not only did he win
races but'he kept on doing it for a long time.
A weird yarn was published the other day
about a parsonical politician in Maoriland who,
wanting to give racing an uppercut, told a story of
a friend desiring to get a cavalry-charger for use in
the war, and being “told by an expert not to buy a
racehorse.” Therefore this simple soul believed he
had evidence that “racers are no good in time of
war.” Obviously the man who wanted to rush off to
the wars on Desert Gold or Sasanoff, or go prancing
away astride of Patrobas, ought to be provided with
a keeper. Racehorses are not chargers, and no one
ever thought they were. But they are thoroughbreds
—the foundation' of the remount in all countries on
earth “The better bred a horse is the better it is
for work of any kind. Than the thoroughbred horse
there is no gamer or more enduring animal. The
mongrel is—well, just a mongrel. Racing or no
racing, it is certain that without the thoroughbred
strain the horse goes back to a mere weed.

Watch out for the M.V.R.C. The old-
established Moonee Valley (Vic.) racecourse, run
hitherto as a proprietary concern, has decided to
become a properly-constituted club.
* * * *
“Mulruana”: During a race at the Mel-
bourne Trotting Club’s meeting at Richmond one
sulky came into contact with another and capsized.
Two others also turned turtle. One of the fallen
nags quickly extricated himself from half a sulky
and galloped off attached to the remainder in the
opposite direction to that taken by what was left of
the field. Things looked in apple-pie order for a
head-on collision till an official, Bert McCabe,
dashed at the runaway, caught the reins, and steered
the animal clear of the oncoming competitors. It
was a courageous thing to do, and swiftly done.
* * * *
Albert Lloyd is a hefty youth whom the
Sydney Stadium first took notice of as a middle-
weight. Owing to the young man’s habit of tying
himself up he got hurt and adjourned. He is back
now, bigger and wiser, and contending for the heavy-
weight championship. On Saturday lie had a dispute
with Colin Bell and within ssec. Colin found himself
contending with what seemed like 14 locomotives.
Lloyd hit him hard and often, and, powerful man
though Bell is, the other was the stronger m clinches.
The first round ended without a decisive smite. In
the second Llovd leaped at Bell like a hungry bear
and handed out such an assortment of bash that
Colin’s bald head was brought in sorrow to the
mat. Lloyd meets Dave Smith next, on which occa-
sion both parties will have to be on the alert for
contingencies. , #
Billy Monty, who had been helping to
biff Abdul, was severely biffed by Bobby Gfaham at
Brisbane on Saturday. It was a battle of giants,
one turning the scale at 7st. 111 b. and the otb.er at
Bst. Bobby knew too much about the fine points
of the business and -plastered William with straight
lefts wdiich turned the youth’s dial into something
like a sunset. There was always the chance that one
of Billjim’s swings would cause a sudden adjourn-
ment, but hopes of its arrival evaporated in the
19tli round when his hand turned dog on him. It
probably wouldn’t have made any difference,
So, according to a cable message received
the other day, it has come to this: “Darcy
is considering a match with George Chip, at Mil-
waukee.” Having knocked Chip cold in Sydney
the deed must be repeated on the other sicl.e of the
world. Darcy stock seems to be doing a slide.
* * * *
“Carfax” : Will nobody throw a banana or
something at these people who are always moaning
about the iniquity of “sport” while a war is on r
In the name of Heaven how will it help the war to
come our way if people, young' and old, start accumula-
ting fat and learning to play the guitar? Sport (of
course no one includes all these ridiculous little race
meetings which are to be objected to on grounds
altogether apart from war) is one of the nation s
greatest assets in relation to defence, and so far
from being decried ought to be encouraged more than
ever. If the war has shown anything at all it is
that from among footballers, swimmers, runners,
tennis players and so on —that great body of healthy,
vigorous manhood which keeps itself fit and eager——
the very finest military material has been secured.
Snort in Australia keeps thousands of young men in
good health lit an age when the value of this cannot
be estimated 'in terms of cash or anything else. Why
throw cold water on the kids who are coming to
maturity? Shut down on athletics and they will
take to pastimes they would be 100 per cent, better
for knowing nothing about. #
The annual report of the Metropolitan
Rugby Union (N.S.W.) is a mournful document.
Qn one page is a memorial table of .those affiliated
players who have died on active service. There are
101 names on the list. Over the line at last 1^
Lisle Lane, killed in France, came from
Orange (N.S.W.), where he was a crack footballer.
“Paddy” Lane, now deceased, one of the best-known
players for the Wallaroos in Sydney years ago, was
a brother. Young Lane joined the A.I.F. as private,
and worked up to two stars. He was in the Land-
ing, and was wounded next day. Fever sent him
back to hospital after he had rejoined his mates ; and
when he got over that the battalion had gone to
France. A bullet gave him a third spell in hospi-
tal; but be was back again in the Somme trenches
before the Last Post sounded. Age, 24.
* * * *
“Haka”: Passed up at Oafnaru (M.L.),
Chummy Noel, a once widely-known Maoriland toe-
baller. In the days of such famous players as
Taiaroa, Lvnch and Kehoe, Noel played wing three-
quarter for Otago Province, and was about the
speediest thing that ever wriggled into a jersey. One
of his matches was against the English team in ’BB.
•In more recent times Chummy converted himself
into a fielder, hut when the Act was passed nullify-
ing the legalisation of the bookmaking industry he
paid out and reached for his hat. He was 53 when
the whistle sounded.

A . 13-year old youngster named Viney,
touring with Sydney schoolboys recently, bagged 22
wickets for 32 runs in two matches at Bathurst.
» * * *
When Sydney, playing against Western
Suburbs, closed their premiership innings on Satur-
day at 403 for nine wickets, Jimmy Taylor seized a
bat and proceeded to smite the Sydney bowling. The
Suburbs crumbled away at 277, but James was going
strong to the last, finishing with 126 not out. This
was his second century in the season, and brought his
average for 10 innings to 58. Last year he made 622
runs, averaging 41.66 per innings.
■* * * *
Richard Teece, who has just surrendered
the job of running the A.M.P. Society, was secre-
tary of the N. S. Wales Cricket Association half a
century ago. Joseph Clayton was in the chair before
Teece.' Both did much to get the Ass. up on its.
legs. Teece also had a hand in founding the Sydney
’Varsity oval, and was behind an oar in the first
inter-’Varsity boatrace in the early ’7o’s.
* * * *
E. S. Collins, Bananaland’s breast-stroke
champion for 1917, last week won the 100yds. in
lmin. 21 4-ssec. (2 2-ssec. outside the State record)
and the 220yds. in 3niin. 25 2-ssec. He was a com-
plete stranger to championship efforts before he
defeated Brett (last year’s champion), Springfield
and Thurlow ; but he seems to be a hustler who may
lie hard to separate from his title.
“Surf Bather”: Swimming is a most
democratic sport; yet if you start hunting amongst
the champions or near-champions for people named
Smith, or Brown, or Jones, or Robinson, you can t
find one of them. In England the great swimmers
have been E. C. Daniels, Nuttall, Tyers, Jarvis, 11.
and J. P. Taylor, Davenport, Billington and Bat-
tersby. The Jones family appears twice on the lists
in the remote past, when the sport was much less
democratic than it is now, W. Blew-Jones winning
the 100yds. in 1883, and T. Jones the 220yds. in
1889. On Australia’s roll of great swimmers are the
names of Cavil], Kieran, Beaurepaire, Hardwick,
Lane, Springfield, Wickham, Healey, H. Baker and
Solomon ; but no member of the numerous family of
Smith has put in an appearance. It is the same in
America. There are dozens of Smith boxers and
footballers, but the family seems to shy at the wet.
* * * *
Alone amongst ladies’ swimming clubs in
Victoria, N. S. Wales and Queensland, that of
Brighton (Vic.) bars men as spectators. Conse-
quently its “open” fixture the other day was not
open even to reporters. When the new Victorian
Ladies’ Amateur Swimming Association approved of
the trousered brigade attending, all meetings, the
Brighton club gathered its skirts about its lower
limbs and withdrew, fleciding henceforth to float “on
its own.” As a result, no Vic. female champions or
other leading swimmers competed at the recent
Brighton carnival. Nevertheless, according to the
treasurer, it was every way the most successful since
the club made its first splash. #
Successful lady bowlers are not generally
what the fashion reporter would call modish. But
a refreshing exception has just turned up under the
hat of Mrs. Arthur Paterson, of lvew (Vic.), who has
downed all other competitors for the coveted lady
“champion of champions” trophy in Melbourne. The
victorious young matron is plump, pretty and charm-
ing, and seems an absurd waste of good looks in a
bowling tournament. She would be an enormous
success selling tuppenny trifles for a shilling at a
charity carnival.
Ashfield beat City for the Sydney bowls
pennant by the quite unexpected margin of 38, the
suburbanites winning in every rink. A margin of
38 in a first-class bowls contest is equal to a 10
wickets’ victory at cricket, and the downfall of the
City cracks is still a cause of wonderment where
they live and rejoicing where the winners take
refreshment.

Down at Blackman’s Bay (Tas.) the
fishermen don’t, as a rule, bother about barracouta ;
there’s more money in crayfish and trumpeter. But
lately they started to catch the ’couta. “You see,”
one of ’em explained, “the blamed things come into
the bay so thick they blocked us from, getting out,
and we had to fish lanes through.”
* * * *
“,T.G.”: Blackfish, although apparently a
different variety to that of the Monaro streams men-
tioned by “Wattle” (B. 15/3/’l7), are indigenous
to most Vic. creeks and rivers running south of the
Dividing Range. Like that of its N. S. Wales
cousin, the flesh is excellent eating. Unfortunately
it is being cleared out of its home by imported
(English and Scottish) trout. The fool Vic. Fish-
eries Department has introduced the energetic enemy
to nearly every stream in the State, all in the in-
terests of ’aughty tourists who arrive in crowds of
two or three a month.
* * * *
“Rodds”: What are these pikers talking
about with their tremendous struggles with 51b.
trout caught with “any old bait” and their jewfish
turning the scale at 71b. or lewt., or something?
Why, they don’t know what fish are, and the man
who says that the rainbow rises to any old grub
tossed in after him ought to be fined for perjury. The
rainbow or any other trout is as particular about his
meals as a lady’s poodle. I know, because I’ve
pursued the trout where he is a fish, not a smelt, and
that is iti Maoriland. There you can annex the rain-
bow running up to 161 b., 181 b. and 201 b. ; and on
these occasions he is worth looking at. I once took
an Australian fishing near Tokaanu, and when he had
caught two brown trout each as long as his leg he
knocked off, rushed away with his fish in pursuit of
a photographer, and had himself reduced to a picture
holding one in each hand. Then he spent the rest
of the day writing home about what had happened to
him. As for jewfish—well, nobody would go for jewy
who ever went after kingfish off Cape Brett.
* * * *
“T.F.” : An old-man tuna tackled a bather
in a Central Otago pool the other day and very
nearly got him under. The eel, explained the badly-
scared swimmer, secured a fighting-hold on the back
of the left ankle and shook the owner like a cat
worrying a mouse. The cannibal was kicked and
shaken off with difficulty. Maybe this explains some
mysterious drownings in the M.L. backblocks. A
4ft. eel is a powerful brute, with something of the
python in his build and strength. But this tuna
was small-fry compared with Jimmy Carroll’s cham-
pion of the Waikaremoana country. Timi’s yarn,
which no doubt he took with him to the Cold Coun-
try, to the envy of all pakeha anglers, is that it took
a hundred Maori braves, bare to the buff, to haul
this specimen into shallow water and make the line
fast to the scenery till the morning, and that when
daylight came the fish or devil had disappeared,
taking with him a quarter of a mile of the four-wire
fence with big straining-post attached. Some people
doubt the word of the ex-Minister for Native Affairs ;
but, then, some people would doubt the Miracles.
“Teacher” : Who can account for a cod
without eyes? He weighed 121 b. when I landed him.
but how he took the bait beats me. Have n-!i a
sense of smell ? *

“Izaak’ f : Schnapper or snapper? Dampier
wrote of catching “snappers” in 1699, and took
mentioned capture of “the snapper kind in 1< iU.
Professor Morris, of Melbourne, in his Austral-English
dictionary, writes that “snapper” was the original
form derived from snapping- Fishermen, perhaps ol
Dutch origin, bagged “schnapper” (from schnapps ).
It was first printed thus, according to Morns, m
Tenison Wood’s “Fish of N. S. Males’ (188 i). A
search in old N. S. Wales Government Gazette shows
only “snapper.’ ,
“H.V.E.”: The rarest wild duck is, I
think, the freckled duck, who is but a casual
visitor to N. S. Wales from Westralia. I have twice
shot this bird on Monaro in wet seasons. It new in
company with black duck, and occurred but singli.
Color, dark brown, freckled minutely with grey, in
size it is about equal to a well-bred black duck.
The blue-winged shoveller and pink-ear are other
chance visitors to the tablelands. One dry season
out West I marked down a vast flock of shovel-bill-
in a Monaro waterhole. and with an ancient muzzle-
loading duck gun slew nine in one shot. Ihe
charge, four drams diamond-grain powder and 2oz.
shot, blew the nipple out of the somewhat rust-eaten
slow-flrer, and deposited the gunner on Ins back among
thistles; but there was a fine load of duck-meat to
supply the next meal or two. #
“Eryander” : Those “misguided gun-entlm-
siasts” * who told “Foolar” to aim in front of the
hurrying target (B. 22/3/’l7) were absolutely right
in principle, though either they were wrong in their
deductions or “Foolar” erred in the application ol
their advice. With 45 grains of smokeless powder
No, 6 shot travels 40 yards in l-20th of a second,
and in that time our “black-cheek falcon coveung,
at top-speed, 120 ft. in a second—would have moved
Oft The figures speak for themselves, and failuie
comes from the fact that few men can .judge cross-
distance as accurately as distance in the line of sight,
A try for this Oft. would in most cases mean an ami
of 12, 15 or even 20 feet ahead of the bird. Its the
eve that errs, not the mathematics. #

11. J. Parsons’s attempt to fracture the
Sydnev-Melbourne motor-cycle record last, week was
a tussle between bad roads and the machine—and the
bad roads won. Parsons whizzed off from Sydney
GP O with a time schedule arranged to wipe out
.Tim’ Bolger’s 23hr. 41min., and seriously threaten
Boyd Edkins’s car record of'lGhr. 55min. But after
half a dozen smashes and the discovery of a broken
chain at Avenel (62 miles from Melbourne) the time
schedule fell in a heap. So did Parsons; and lie had
a few days in hospital to ruminate on whether it
was all worth while. „

“M PA”- Clarence Weber gave a show of
strength 'and agility at Melbourne Town Hall the
other night and astonished the house, with In-
fests. Never since his entry into the strong-man
business has this remarkable athlete looked more im-
pressive. He emptied his bellows down to 364 in.
and blew ’em up to slHn. ; incidentally he worked
bis lungs independently, breathing with one-and hold-
ing the other quite still—a feat in the business. To
illustrate strength and balance he spun a bar, with
a small boy clinging to each end, on the ball of his
foot while reclining oil his back. His high-kicking
reached 9ft., and other samples of nimbleness took
the form of leaping, a good-sized kitchen-table at a
standing jump, and hopping through the curve of Ins
]e | >’ while holding the toe of tlvc foot. This capri
was done backwards and forwards. Webei lias gbou
up the shifting of vast quantities of pig-iron—a
dull game at which he excelled some years ago. IDs
muscle-control is wonderful, and the muscles them-
selves are as soft and pliable as a child s. Clarence,
who was born at Brighton (Vic.), started as an
amateur foot runner and won many good pots. He
next tackled cycling, and won several championships,
besides riding a. mile in 2min., off scratch, in n
handicap. In weight-lifting he made some records,
and he collared the cateh-as-catch-can championship
at the same time. He is equal to better than. 20ft.
at a broad jump, besides winning-events at hammer-
throwing, shot-putting, high-jumping, vaulting, hurd-
ling, walking, sheaf-tossing and other minor caper-.
Given a good track he can lump his 14st. 41b. ovei a
hundred in five to six yards worse than evens.
Writer has watched this big Australian grow from
the first moment lie lifted a weight in public, and the
things he can do to-dav make’ some of the imported
athletes in vaudeville look cheap. Incidentally, Weber
is a good citizen with a family of six, very muon
after his own looks.

Grey Hair Unnecessary.
A simple, old-fashioned home-made lotion that
will restore the color of youth.
One need not resort to the very question-
able expedient of hair dye in order not to
have grey hair. The grey hair can easily
he changed back to a natural color in a
few days’ time merely by the application
of a simple, old-fashioned and perfectly
harmless home-made lotion. Procure from
your chemist two ounces of tamtnalite con-
centrate and mix it with three ounces of bay
rum. Apply this to the hair a few times
with a small sponge and you will soon have
the pleasure of seeing your grey hair gradu-
ally darkening to the desired shade. The
lotion is pleasant, not sticky "or greasy, and
does not injure the hair in any way. [1

Co-operative Film Exchange, Ltd., head
office in Melbourne and branches in other
States, which began with a deficit of £5489.
comprising preliminary expenses £439, and
flotation loading £5OOO :
To
Feb., Profit. Dividends. Reserves Deficit.
£5439

1915.. 3128 9 p.c. = £930. 219 S 1737

Oec., Reserves.
1916.. 632 632 503
Four of the big picture-show people having'
formed a combine to import films and show
them, some of the other people in the busi-
ness launched this little concern. The pro-
moter got 10,000 shares (£5000) and the
money put up was £0645 ; so this co. is over-
loaded, too. And under-capitalised—so
under-capitalised that it owed £17,28.1 on
December 31. As against this debt and the
co.’s own funds there is. in addition to a
few etceteras, stock-in-trade £9059, hire of
films paid in advance £6257; and “estimated
value of unexpired lease of hired films,”
£5184. These are all precarious assets. A
film is a gamble, just the same as a play;
and no self-respecting pawnbroker would
advance very much on a bushel of them. The
security for the principal being thus
wretchedly poor, nothing less than a 15 p.c.
dividend is a fair thing. This has never
been in sight yet, owing to the co.’s need of
trading capital: and for the past year the
directors say: “In spite of the continued
expansion of the business, owing to the in-
creased cost of supplies, coupled with the
heavy duty, it is becoming more difficult to
conduct the affairs of the co. at a reasonable
profit.” For the present the few big “pro-
ducing” cos. and their artists have the show-
men by the wool; and that is a blessed turn-
ing of the tables, for before the “pictures”
arrived the showmen had the artists by the
wool.

This co. has learned lately to have a great
sympathy for Bildad’s friend Job, of the
land of tJz. For its trials have been these :
Increase of war taxation.
Arrival of electric competition and loss of the
street-lighting contract.
Increase of 3s. per ton in the price of .coal as a
result of Hughes’s usual brilliant settlement, of the
coal strike. • '
Daylight saving and decreased consumption of gas.
The sales of gas and residuals for the half-
year were £1747; for the corresponding half
of 1915, £2427; or, going back before the
war, £2221 for the latter half of 1913. And
as exes, haven’t been cut to correspond, there
was nothing for it but to reduce the div. and
make it p.c. for the year. Now the old
mortgage (£3000) has fallen in, and the co.
has had to get a bank overdraft to pay it
off; and that won’t help profits either. There
seems to be writing on the wall for more
than the Albury Co. to read.
* * * *
The Tasmania State mine at Argent Flat,
Zeehan, is holding out signals of distress.
The show was taken over by the Labor Gov-
ernment about three years ago. Up to the
present about £28,000 has been provided by
Parliament, in addition to £lO,OOO received
from ore sold and royalties. The shaft has
been sunk to 300 ft., and crosscuts put out to
intersect the different lodes. Flaherty’s
lode, Fulton’s lode and No. 8 lode have all
been passed, but none proved payable. No. 4
lode should be reached in a week or so. Un-
less that, or a rise being put up on Fulton’s
lode, proves payable, the mine will be closed
down, the last £2OOO voted by Parliament
having been made available on that condi-
tion. Not that the State would have got
anything directly out of the mine if it had
proved a New Broken Hill, because such a
bad bargain was made when it was taken
over that the leaseowners, by repaying tne
capital spent, could have resumed possession
at any time. «
* * * *
More from the chairman’s speech at the
Mount Elliott meeting in London:—
There are those who will remember the time when
any ore carrying less than 33 p.c. of copper
was unpayable at GToncurry. When roads were first
built the great achievement of shipping away at a
profit 25 p.c. ore was performed. When the rail-
way from Townsville cot through a considerable
quantity of ore assaying from 15 to 20 p.c. was
shipped away. When this company, in coniunction
with the Hampden, built the railway from Cloncurry
to Elliott there were those who said that the pro-
posal to treat 10 p.c. to 12 p.c. ore would leave no
profit at all. And 1 have no doubt there will be
some who will say the same thing about our policy of
treating 5 to 6 p.c. ore. However, we shall see.
To take things one step further and successfully treat
3 to 4 p.c. copper ore only requires cheap coal and
cheap transport, and if the Bowen coalfield can
supply the one, you need have no doubt whatever
that we shall get the other. The railway to the
Bowen coalfield from the coast is now' under con-
sideration. The estimates arc out, and I understand
that it is likely the work will be put irr hand very
soon.
*•*•*■-*
Since rain fell the Pioneer (Tas.) has
resumed sluicing with No. 2 electric plant.
The latest clean-up gave 66 tons of black
tin, and the average output with a full sup-
ply of water is over 10 tons per week. This
is the best done by any co. in Australia; but,
then, the Pioneer has 161,244 issued shares,
and the last four quarterly dividends of Is.
each required £32,248. To keep that up
regularly the net profit on a yearly- output
of 500 tons of black tin has to be £64 10s.
per ton. A high rate of profit is in fact
required to pay moderate dividends; and as
values are declining as the sluicing plants
work down the lead, there is a terrific
struggle to keep working costs below sd.
per yard. More than 40s. per share has
been distributed.

BOOTS have come back again and no mistake.
But such dreams of boots, up-to-date,
dainty, delightful, fit accompaniment to the
flounced taffeta frocks of the day, which
combine smart modernity with old-world charm.
Paris and London both are now tripping to work
and play in short-fronted, high-heeled, suede-
topped boots like the Bostock style here illus-
trated.
It is a smart little boot, there is no doubt
about that, and every hi£ as comfortable as it is
smart. The beautifully fitting last on which it is
made sees to that, and lots of women, who have
never imagined they could wear a short-fronted
style, will be amazed to find how comfortable it
is, how jolly it makes their feet look.
Then for those who want something different,
who prefer shoes perhaps, there is a fine range of
styles to select from, all as up-to-date and charm-
ing as this model, and all safe to wear well and
feel as peculiarly one’s own as if they had been
made to measure.

There was another revolution in a dusty
little office in Little Collins-street, when “His
Imperial Royal Majesty’s Consul-General”
for Australia, A. N. d’Abaza, Esq., turned
democrat, dashed the royal seal into the
safe, and signed himself plain “Consul-
General.” The daring fellow actually told a
Melbourne newspaper man that the revolu-
tion was “the best thing that had ever hap-
pened.” Then he resumed his job with his
coat turned inside out.
“0.K.”: Oue of Melbourne’s most useful
little charitable institutions that deserves an
annual dole from the Government, and
doesn’t get it, is the. Ragged Boys’ Home.
The first of the homes, opened in 1895 -by
Lady Brassey, grew out of the old Latrobe-
street Mission, which had already been deal-
ing with city waifs and strays for 30 years’.
The institution struggles along on a fluctuat-
ing income of considerably less than £2OOO a
year, derived mainly from small individual
subscriptions, gathered in by door-to-
door collectors whose, commissions amount
to more than a fourth of the total annual
outlay. The management otherwise is a pat-
tern in economy. Obviously the work it does
deserves Government and municipal support.
It is “rescue” work. Waifs are trained and
educated at the Homes in Melbourne and
Frankston, or are placed in good homes in
the country. 111-circumstanced urchins are
gathered in and moulded into men. More
than 50 of the, old boys are known to have
enlisted for the, war as soldiers, which sug-
gests that in the future the Homes should
be a steady source of supply for the Aus-
tralian navy. Charities of this sort show a
very clear profit to a country. Even from a
purely business point of view, they are worth
while.

IN BANANA LAND
During the recent deluge at Lyndon, near
Ilfracombe (C.Q. Railway), a record for an
inland downpour was established. The gauge
registered 17 inches in four hours. Then
the pressure in the watering-can eased, and
it 'only leaked four inches in the next 20
hours.
An ardent scientific plant-specialist, Cyril
T. White, has just been appointed to succeed
his uncle, J. F. Bailey, as Queensland Gov-
ernment botanist. Though only 26 White
has a very rare knowledge of Australian
vegetables. He got a good grounding under
his venerated grand-dad, F. Manson Bailey,
and the enthusiasm with which he has wooed
Flora in all parts of the State for the past
half-dozen years entitles him to the billet.
The Ryan Government in Bananaland is
having its fill of by-elections. Immediately
it came into office, Hamilton resigned his
seat to take over the leadership of the Gov-
ernment in the House of Fossils. Pollock
was elected to the vacancy, thus retaining
the seat for Labor. Then on the death of
Dave Bowman, Wilson secured the Vall(*y
seat for the Labor Party. At present Mary-
borough is agitated over a by-election,
caused by the elevation of Alf Jones to the
Leg. Council, in consequence of the appoint-
ment of Bill Hamilton to the Presidency of
that Chamber. Now John Adamson has lured
himself with hopes of a Seat in the Senate
into throwing up his Rockhampton seat,
and there will have to be a by-election for
that division.
John Adamson, who got out of the Queens-
land Labor party over Conscription, has
again forfeited substance for shadow. He
recently resigned his seat in the Assembly
under the impression that he was a certainty
as one of the “National” candidates for the
Senate. But when all the wire-pulling was
over it was found that T. W. Crawford
(president of the Sugar Producers’ Associa-
tion), Mat Reid and H. Spencer Foil (Re-
turned Soldiers and Patriots’ League) had
secured the selection. Foil, by the way, was
Adamson’s private secretary when he was
Minister for Railways.
“Lord Toothpick”: At Enoggera Camp
recently Canon Garland invited 40 Billjims
to tea at the Anzac Club, and 107 of them
rolled up. The popular parson was delighted.
After the spread he addressed the men and
told them that he expected them to stay for
the evening service. “Of course,” he added “the
R.C.’s may go away if they please.” Then
he took a walk. On his return he was
astonished to discover only five left. All the
rest bad suddenly ’verted to Rome.
“M. Jay”: A link in the pioneering
romance of early Queensland history was
severed with the death of William Guilfoyle
in Brisbane the other day. Born in Tipper-
ary 82 years ago, he migrated to Bananaland
in the late ’so’s, and not finding gold in the
streets commenced life with Matilda and
a cheerful disposition. lie was the driver
of the old coach between Ipswich and Bris-
bane when the former town was the metropo-
lis of the State nearly 50 years ago. He
was the ringer in the Jondaryan sheds in the
early ’6o’s, and when he got tired of the
rough-and-tumble of the Bush he settled down
in Brisbane and helped in the erection of the
old Victoria Bridge as a diver. In the first
athletic games held in Queensland over half
a century ago at the Botanical Gardens he
scooped the whole programme—high jump,
long- and short-distance sprints, throwing the
hammer —the lot without as much as half
an hour’s training. •
The Full Bench of the Arbitration
Court delivered an important judgment
in Brisbane last week, when it de-
clared that, under the 1916 Industrial

A . Cairns syndica te, with the scientific
n ,„ ’ ’
ex-parson Campbell at the helm, has got
busy producing paper-pulp from blady
grass, Chinese burr and sida retusa. The
present trouble is to dry the pulp. The
syndicate pays a royalty of Is..per ton to
the State Government, which gave the right
to harvest vegetable products from a strip
of country along the coast south of Cairns.
Memories' of a case that crawled through
Queensland courts on to the Privy Council
have been revived by the passing of Edwin
Charles Bacon, a stock-dealer in a big way.
Bacon, who was Australian-born, once
owned the Diamond Downs station. He was
badly hit by the drought of 1902, and also
by the local judgment in the Bacon v. Pur-
cell argument concerning the delivery of
stock under contract. However, Bacon won
when the dispute went to the Privy Coun-
cil. Of recent times he had lived a life of
meditation in Warwick.
“C.J.M.”: The desperate hunt for a food-
ruiner up North :—•
Cook and Gardener wanted for Gunnawarra
Station. £2 per week wages. White, Black or
Yellow.
Hunger and pains in the pinny drive some
people to revolution, others to colored labor.
“McNorth,” Queensland’s topmost settler:
Miss Beatrice Grimshaw (B. 15/3/T7) writes of
the luck of the man-on-the-land in Papua in being
able to grow, shoot or catch his tucker in these
days of scarce and high-priced food. When supplies_
are overdue and the pantry shows a deficit, the man
in Far Northern Australia needn’t despair either.
At my ranch on Cape York the scrubs abound with
turkeys, pigeons, ducks and other edible fowl, scrub
wallabies and wild pig. Fish of all kinds swarm in
the sea, and at low water fine oysters and crabs can
be bagged. With the aid of a dinghy, a rope, a spear
and a nigger you cun have real turtle soup and meat
every day, and dugong (which tastes like fillet steak
when fresh and like bacon when dried) as often as
you wish. For vegetable food there are wild fruits,
such as red and white “apples,” wangi and grapes,
all edible. If not too tired you can make a garden
by the simple process of sticking plants in a bit of
cleared ground and returning after three or four
months to collect the harvest. Sweet potatoes,
manioc, bananas, maize and a multitude of tropical
things bear quickly and magnificently. The sunny
weather thp.t follows the torrential rains of the
nor’-west season has the forcing effect of a hotbed.
On a piece of land I cleared some time ago-there are
hundreds of bearing paw-paw trees. I didn’t plant
one of them. The birds brought the seeds from
somewhere, and did it for me. After dinner, if you
want to get on the jag, there is abundant fire-
water to be extracted by a simple process from the
cocoanut trees. It’s the real stingo, too. And you
can grow your own tobacco for a smoke between
drinks. The trouble is, of course, that no white
man can live in this land of plenty with the best
climate in the world—that is, no white man except
me, and even I am expecting to be worn down within
the next 100 years or so.
In Queen’s Gardens.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The gardens glowed beneath the sun,
And on the small white flowers
A butterfly had perched to sip
Its share of scented hours.
’Twas shaped of velvet, sable-hued,
And on each outspread wing
The round white discs were outlined with
A blue and royal ring.
And when it settled on a bud
Swift dying out of sight,
The blue fled, leaving it severe
In mourning black and white.
It was a radiant thing, when back
(The sable pricking through)
Came, like a binding troth with Joy,
Again that ring of blue.
A red hibiscus near the path
Lured it like a caress;
The variegated canna showed
In Oriental dress.
With delicate delights of mauve
The bloss’ming hedge was gay,
But underneath its shielding gloom
The turf seemed worn away.
And why those green and pointed blades
Were sere and crushed, 1 knew.
’Twas where a homeless woman lay
A long night through.
Queensland.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, March 24, 1917.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
On Thursday the Mayoress presided at a
meeting called to establish a Bush Nursing
Association. The scheme has been “in the
air” since August, 1914, but money has been
too scarce. Now the residents of Banana,
which is away up between Rockhampton and
Townsville, have been promised by the Home
Secretary a trained nurse for the district,
and with her the Association will make a
start.
Pretty Peggy Ferrier was married in Syd-
ney last week to Ross Philp, son of J.A., the
old newspaper-man. Young Philp was asso-
ciate to Justice Shand, but preferred khaki
to the black gown, and is now receiving con-
gratulations at the artillery camp at Liver-
pool.
Another wedding. Marian Hamilton
Baker and Corporal Claud Saunders com-
mitted it last Saturday, and they are now
showing one another Port Jackson. The
bride’s step-mother rationed about 50 guests
the day before the partnership was signed.
Lorna Dickson, daughter of the .Limes
Dicksons of Wynnum, is flashing the dia-
mond badge of impending servitude. It was
riveted upon her by Leonard Harwood, a
Toowoomba boy.
The girls of New Farm are strenuous and
business-like patriotic workers. Dne of their
efforts raised over 1400. Then they came to
the front with a cookery-book, and the
profits enabled them to buy two rowing-boats
for the use of convalescents at Kangaroo
Point military hospital.
Lena Hammond, our top-side local con-
tralto, was the star performer at the Irish
concert in Toowoomba on St. Pathrick’s
night. One of her pupils, Clarice Cox, sang
from the same rostrum. Miss Ive Norman,
another Hammond aspirant, made the “hit"
at St. Pat’s concert in Brisbane. Both the
young voices are contralto.
J. R. Crane, a popular Rockhampton man,
has dropped south to Hobart, where he will
manage the Union Bank The farewells were
frequent and free from bias.
Killed on February 25, Fred Barbour, son
of G. P. Barbour, headmaster of Toowoomba
Grammar School and formerly of Sydney
Grammar. He left Queensland in December.
1915, and was only 21 at the finish. Rank,
quarter-master-sergeant; a line scholar and
athlete.
H. E. Budden, commissioner for Aus-
tralian Comforts Funds, is in Brisbane. On
Monday the local committee of the Fund
expects some good advice from him at Par-
bury House in return for tea and thanks.
The -Wattle Day League is losing two of
its most energetic workers. The president.
.T. F. Bailey, this morning left for Adelaide
to take charge of the Botanical Gardens;
and the Rutledge lady, who has been the
vice-president, leaves for Melbourne next
month. The League farewelled the abscond-
ers at afternoon-tea on Wednesday.
The “house full” sign is up at the
Women’s College. One student has taken
the track for Sydney because she can’t get
even a shake-down. No one knows when the
permanent buildings are to be erected, and
about £4OO would be needed to make the
present habitation suitable for current needs.
I asked our baker this morning what he
thought should be done (he’s a member of
the P.W.W. or something), and he said:
“Take a thick-’un’s worth uv tickets in the
Eight-Hour Art Union.” I’m still dazed.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Nick” : Billjim’s wife can be as game as
Bill. Near Mount Morgan, a middle-aged
married man heard the call and went with
the Miners’ Corps to Gallipoli. He re-
turned broken in health, and with his wife
took up land. Ljiter Bill’s ailment con-
quered him, leaving his widow to struggle
with the farm. The block was infested with
pear; but Billjemima tackled the job of get-
ting rid of it. The next that was heard
of her was when she wrote to the Shire
Council Complaining that, though she had
got the pest off her own place, every time it
rained pear-leaves were washed down from
higher ground. An appreciative council
promptly remedied that trouble.

THE SECRET OF A
GOOD COMPLEXION.
By MME. ST. CLAIRE,
Any woman nowadays may quickly reju-
venate her complexion at home by a safe and
painless process of gentle absorption. The
days of expensive and dangerous “operations”
are past, and each woman can be her own
beauty doctor. It has been discovered that
ordinary mercolised wax, applied like cold
cream, at night, will cause the thin veil of
lifeless, sallow or blotchy cuticle to gradually
and gently flake off in invisible, flour-like
particles, revealing the fresh, vigorous and
beautiful young skin underneath. Mercolised
wax absorbs only the unhealthy and un-
sightly outer fi’m, and does not affect the
healthy tissue at all.
The process defies detection, and unveils
a lasting and beautiful complexion, which is,
of course, a perfectly natural one. For this
purpose thousands of women are now using
mercolised wax, which may be obtained at
any chemist’s. Naturally 'the process also
removes all such surface blemishes as
freckles, blotches, pimples, tan, liver spots,
etc. It makes the face look years younger
and prettier, and renders the use of face
creams unnecessary.
* * * *
Another valuable piece of information,
which many women will appreciate, concerns
the success of the new treatment for super-
fluous hair. A remedy, for permanently erad-
icating this ugly form of facial disfigurement
now seems to have been found. It is simpli-
city itself. About one ounce of ordinary pure
powdered pheminol, which can be obtained
at any chemist’s, is all that is needed to put
one in the way of completely ridding oneself
of superfluous hair. When applied to the
obnoxious growth this causes it to immedi-
ately disappear, and the continuance of the
recommended treatment is said to finally
destroy the hair roots, so that there will be
no renewal of the growth. [5

To Director, Intellicence a Tourist Bureau, Brisbane, Q.
“Patter”: I don’t want to crab the musical
director, but Sydney Tivoli should take two or
three of his kerosene tins from the drummer.
He’s an energetic chap and wants to give
good value; but the result is that some of
the selections and accompaniments are less
like music than a travelling tinker being
thrown down a flight of steps. It is very
annoying when you want to interrupt a
singer yourself with an interim criticism to
have your remarks drowned by the artist
with the chopsticks.
“C. Ockney”: An English author, object-
ing to the maltreatment of his MS. at the
hands of the London actor-manager who had
accepted it, recently carried his objections
to the Law Courts with successful • results.
The manager was Charles Hawtrey and the
playwright the not entirely unknown Monck-
ton Hoffe. llawtrey had started rehearsals
and advertised the date of production of the
new piece. The title according to Charles
was to be “According to John.” But this
was not the view of the author, who some-
what unexpectedly applied for an injunction
to prevent Hawtrey producing the play,
alleging that the cast wasn’t suitable and
that the title had been altered without his
consent. The Law was sympathetic and
when a week or two later Hoffe’s offering
was staged it bore the author’s original
name, “Anthony in Wonderland.”
“M.P.A.”: Malini, the chubby little con-
jurer lately at Melbourne Athengeum, is a
somewhat different hanky-pankyist.. He is,
first of all, a carpet-bag magician and
secondly a master of suggestion. As a
matter of fact, the whole game is based on
suggestion, so personality plays a great part.
Malini has nothing new in the way of tricks,
and all of ’em are drawing-room wheezes at
that; but he gets more out of his items than
I had thought possible. He is the type of
magician who can simply spiflicate one in a
club or bar-parlor. In a hall his tricks,
being close-up stuff, are lost on the chap who
only parts a nimble bob and is too far aw r ay.
Working in a hall Malini should crowd more
items into the programme, otherwise his
audience will not get enough of him. He
has a tiny fat fist of the make that small
change won’t slip through, and he does the
cleanest work with it that I have seen. On
the other hand, he is the most audacious of
the many deceptionists I have seen at work
—in a different, class from men like Howard
Thurston and Allan Shaw. These are digi-
tal dexterity experts l, who really amount to
card and coin jugglers. Malini is the first
man in the game to pull after-dinner feats
into a hall-show with success, and get a
full 10 minutes out of turns that the aver-
age performer is finished with in a fifth of
the time.
Time was when Sarah Bernhardt suffered
considerable inconvenience from having ham-
pered herself with a husband, whose name,
by the way, was Damala. Thirty years ago
there were some legal proceedings in France
for the purpose of invalidating some theatri-
cal contracts which' Sarah had entered into.
The argument was that, under French law,
a married actress couldn’t make contracts
without her husband’s consent. Sarah over-
came the difficulty somehow. All her life
the masterful woman has had her own way.
When she came to Australia her way was to
have 100 golden sovereigns handed to her by
the management every morning before break-
fast. »
Percy Dix, vaudeville merchant, who
passed out at Newcastle (N.S.W.I lately,
threatened at one time to be a grand duke
in variety enterprise. Some 15 years ago he
ran a circuit in M.L., and dished up
a star programme for his patrons. But he
killed his show by making it too good for the
treasury; and when he tried to prune his
salary-list his public forsook him. Twelve
years ago he left M.L., and put up his sign
at the Victoria Theatre, Newcastle, where lie
compromised with his ideals and kept his
bread buttered.
