mother’s voice ringing in your ear could
not have been written seriously in any coun-
try but Wilson’s. In the inartistic Yankee’s
mind the drama of life is inextricably tangled
up with the ’phone.
A RECIPE FOR ROMEO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
. .the right amount of temperament and
treacle into the part of Romeo.’’—B. S/3/’l7.
, Oh, sugary, e'en molassine
This Dago’s story! Potty
He is at first o’er Rosaline,
And then on Juliet dotty!
Looking as if he fed on tarts,
This most romantic freak’ll
Score all the tricks if he’ll but mix
His temperament with treacle.
In ev’ry scene our Romeo
Must gush with youthful ardor—•
The lolly-merchant of the show,
With no touch of the larder.
Then fresh young critics of to-day
As well as those antique’ll
Gladly admit that he is It —
True temperament and treacle!
VICTORIA.
EARDLEY TURNER.

"|~O HERBERT CONOLLY, who was born at Picton,
New Zealand, in 1865, and was last heard of at
South Yarra, Victoria, in 1909, and to all persons
claiming by, through or under him.
PURSUANT to the directions of the Honorable
Sir Robert Stout, K.C.M.Q., Chief Justice of New
Zealand, under Section 25 of The Public Trust Office
Amendment Act, 1913, you are hereby called upon
to send in to the undersigned on or before the Ist
day of May, 1917, your claim to part of the estate
of Emily Conolly, late of Remuera, Auckland,
married woman, deceased, who died on the 27th
July, 1900, intestate, failing which such estate will
be distributed among the persons entitled thereto
disregarding your claim.
Dated at Wellington this 11th day of January,
1917.
J. W. MACDONALD,
Solicitor to the Public Trust Office,
Wellington, New Zealand.
IN THE SUPREME COURT OF NEW
ZEALAND.
WELLINGTON DISTRICT.
No. 1470.
IN DIVORCE.
Between CHARLES WILSON RIDLEY
Petitioner,
And ELIZABETH RIDLEY Respondent.
TO ELIZABETH RIDLEY formerly of
the City of Wellington but now of address
unknown.
WHEREAS Charles Wilson Ridley claim-
ing to have been lawfully married to you has
filed his Petition against you in the Supreme
Court at Wellington wherein the Petitioner
alleges that you have been guilty of deser-
tion of him for five years and upwards AND
WHEREAS by order of the said Court
made on the 16th day of March 3 017
it was ordered that service of the Citation
extracted with a copy of the Petition herein
be effected by publishing an advertisement
once in the Sydney Bulletin and twice in
a Melbourne Daily Evening Newspaper
containing a summary of the Citation
and Petition. You are therefore notified that
unless within thirty days from the date of
the last publication of this advertisement
you attend at the Registrar’s Office, Supreme
Court, Wellington, and file an answer to
the said Petition this cause will be dealt
with in your absence. A copy of the Citation
and Petition will be supplied on application
to the Supreme Court Office, Wellington.
Dated at Wellington this 21st day of
March. 1917.
(1.5.) W. A. HAWKINS, Registrar.
Mr. C. 11. Treadwell is Solicitor to the
Petitioner whose address for service is at his
Office, No. 4 Panama Street, Wellington.
Note.— Should any person know the
Respondent’s address he is requested to send
this at once to her.

The Inky Way.
Passed out in the U.S.A., Luther Brad-
ley, one of the best-known cartoonists in the
Neutral States. Born in Connecticut in
1553, and educated at Yale, Bradley was
nearly 30 before he discovered that his pen-
cil could earn him a living. He was then
a free-lance on the inky way in Melbourne
■—that was some 30 years back. Afterwards
he was political cartoonist on Melbourne
Paunch. Early in the ’nineties he went back
to Uncle Sham’s country, and since 1899
had been chief artist on Chicago Daily
News. For the first two years of the pre-
sent European .earthquake, Bradley’s car-
toons supported President M ilson s view
that the pen is mightier than the sword as
well as a durn sight safer; but lus recent
work has whooped for America’s entrance
into the war.
* * *
The British “Bradshaw’s Guide,” which
recently reached its 1900th issue, announces
a rise in price from sixpence to a shilling as
the result of war conditions. The original
Bradshaw was a cute Quaker who in the
early years of railway development foresaw
that a handy guide to the various railway
lines would be good business. Its Quaker
origin is still stamped on its face. The
latest number to hand is dated‘T irst month,
1917.” Quakers object to the "pagan
names” of the months that are in common
use - . . *
“Wart”: On March 12 the Launceston
Examiner was three-quarters of a century
old. In its earliest days it appeared only
on Saturday afternoons; six months later a
Wednesday issue was added; in 185rf it
came out tri-weekly ; in December, 1N u, it
was printed daily. It is claimed to be one
of only about four papers in Australia that
can look back 75 years and see themselves
under the same names that they carry to-uay.
* * *
“Old Hand”: First published on March
12 1842 the 75-year-old Launceston Exam-
iner is easily the oldest of the Tasmanian
newspapers. Its first issue gave the news
of the birth of the late King Edward, which
had happened four months before; but it has
got a little more rapid since then. Though
a provincial sort of sheet, it has not beei
unprogressive. Seventeen years ago it began
to battle for the development of the Speck s
water-power, and editor Prichard never let
the subject sleep once it had been raised.
The Examiner’s offices, too, are as handy
and well-arranged, on a small scale, as any-
thing in Australia. # „
“S.T.S.”; A year or so ago someone writ-
ing iii The Bulletin called attention to the
snobbishness of English short-story writers,
who still pictured every officer a thoroughbred,
every private-an illiterate person, although
the rush into the ranks has made the modern
private quite likely to be the superior of Ins
officer in education, wealth and everything
else but “influence.” Surely this is at least
as true in the Australian army. Yet our
writers have settled down to make eveiy
Australian private call himself a bloke,
swear with every sentence, say wuz tor
“was,” and drop the letter h all over the
trenches. English writers are under no
such delusion. They tend to make every
Anzac an improbably godlike person, the
ideal short-story hero. But even that is
far better than the pride-of-the push theory.
Why should our stay-at-home humorists be
allowed to earn Is. 6d. by assuring the
world that all the better men than themselves
who have enlisted are illiterateV (This pro-
test is not intended for Dennis, whose Ginger
Mick is a line and recognisable type.. It is
meant for those who have got into the
careless habit of imagining a Ginger Mick
inside every suit of khaki that hasn t buttons
on the shoulder.) # #
It isn’t the sort of thing y#u expect of
Melbourne Argus : — 1
Young unmarried couple want unfurnished flat, or
small house; Hawksburn, .South Yarra, Hast Mel-
bourne ; must be select. Argus office.
The dear old thing must have forgiven
everything else on account of that reserva-
tion—“must be select.”

“Copy Boy” : One ,of the most promising
of war babies is The Stretcher, born in
the camp of the Camel Brigade Field Ambu-
lance at Seymour (Vic.). The verse, photo-
graphs. articles and drawings are of quite
respectable merit, the bandaging of punctu-
ation wounds and rendering of first-aid to
rocky lines having been carefully attended
to by editor Charlie Barrett, who was a
Melbourne Herald sub-editor before turning
from the blue pencil to the Red Cross. It
is understood that the next number of The
Bulletin’s esteemed contemporary will be
brought out in Egypt.

IV. H. Mudford, the editor who raised Lon-
don Standard to the highest pinnacle of
prosperity and lived to see it extinguished
in gloom and disaster, accumulated a tidy
fortune of £65,288, He bequeathed four-
twentieths of his estate to the D°S S> Home
and three- twentieths to the Newspaper Press
doesn’t sound too complimen-
tary to his old journalistic colleagues. Hos-
pitals and the Society for the Prevention of
Cruelty to Animals .will feed on most of
what is left.

Thomas Flavell, who died the other day
in the Cold Country, aged 79, was an Angli-
can parson in Maoriland for a quarter of a
century— mostly at Merivale. He was also
inspector, of church schools in Christchurch
diocese, and ran an Anglican paper down
that way for some years. Since 1904 he
had been a.Devonshire vicar, following the
inky track in his spare time in Macmillan’s
and other English magazines.

beginning to “spat”, in Moreton Bay. This
kind of language was altogether too coarse
for the cub, who made it that the bivalves
were commencing to “expectorate” in More-
ton Bar. And it got through.

A TRIBUTE TO REXONA
from Somewhere in France
TROOPER
BERT LYON
(from a photo).
£
It is with great pleasure that I sit
in my dug-out to write you a few
lines in praise of your wonderful
Ointment, Rexona. It was first intro-
duced to me by a friend many years
ago, while I was still a schoolboy.
I had met with an accident and had
a had leg for three years. It would
•not heal, no matter what I used. 1
was simply tired of buying things
when a mate of mine said. “You get
a tin of Rexona, it is a marvellous
ointment.” I did so, and never
regretted it. I have been a constant
user ever since, for it cured my leg
in six weeks, hardly leaving a scar.
I have recommended it to many,
and, when leaving W.A. for service
abroad, I took two tins with me,
and have always had a tin sent
out every month since. I have car-,
ried it through Egypt, Gallipoli,
and now I have it in France.
In Egypt and on Gallipoli It
was very good; one suffered many
sores owing to the septic poison
in the earth, and also to shortage
of washing water, but needless
to .say I suffered none of these,
owing to my carrying Rexona. Here
in France it does great work; when
one is in the wet for hours it is
very easy to get. chilblains, etc., but
Rexona soon banishes these. I met
a French girl who had a very bad
rash on her hand. It would not
heal, so I gave her some Rexona,
and it soon disappeared. She was
greatly pleased. I have since given
her a tin.
Many males have given It great
praise out here, so I thought it mj
duty to let you know this.
I am. Yours sincerely,
(Sgd.)
No. 2078 Trooper BERT LYON,
2nd Anznc Headquarters,
8.E.F., France

Night on Our Continent.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ON sea-worn cliffs, thrust forward,
The twilight drops its cloak. „
As winging swiftly shoreward
The last gull seeks its folk.
The sky and sea seem blended.
Each merging into each ;
The waves, their journey ended,
Fall down upon the beach.
Brown arms steel-muscled, grimly
Their long sweeps landward ply :
A sail, greyed too, shines dimly
Against grey sea and sky.
A ceaseless dirge of waters
Sounds ever south and north:
And in the east Night’s daughters,
Come stealing shyly forth.
From realms of beauty speeding
In unseen chariot-cars
They come, Queen Venus leading
Her maid-of-honor stars.
The land and sea are kneeling
In love and reverence bent;
Star after star revealing
New realms of wonderment.
As when some royal rover
At length reclaims his own, •
Vast Night, his exile over,
Ascends his kingly throne.
On Toil that aches for leisure,
On hearts that long for calm,
He pours itt ample measure
The largesse of his balm.
With tender touch he closes
Tired eyes in sweet content;
And 'neath his sway reposes
Our mighty continent.
Oh, that some master-maker
Of pictured scenes once more
Would paint Night’s crested breaker
Upon Night’s mystic shore!
The little streams that prattle,
The full floods flaked with white ;
The herds of camping cattle,
Horns gleaming through the night;
Wild steers and heifers rushing
In swift, disordered ranks,
Fleet hoofs the dead leaves crushing.
Tall horsemen at their flanks ;
A drover by his camp-fire,
Red flames that sink and leap,
And on his breast a vampire
That will not let him sleep;
Wide spaces robed in sable,
Tall gumtrees, gaunt and dead ;
Fair children round a table.
Dark rafters overhead ;
Night-riders keenly eyeing
Tree-shadows, dense and black :
A hunted horseman flying
Along a mountain track :
A slush lamp dimly burning,
A woman weeping there,
Eyes wide, and grave, and yearning-
Near by a vacant chair;
Swart diggers worn and beaten.
Brown hands and buried head ;
A last, dry crust half eaten,
Hope done, and promise fled ;
Two lovers softly Wooing,
A sliprail gate, a creek
Its noiseless way pursuing,
Cheek lightly pressed on cheek.
Oh, that some great magician,
Sbme master-brush were sent
To paint with Rembrandt vision
Night on our continent!
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Regarding “The Age.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PROFITS in groceries run in half-
pennies. It is the quantity that
tells. This is the difference between
the grocer and the chemist. The chemist’s
costs run in halfpennies, but people do not
buy medicine like they do sardines and
pickles, otherwise you would get your
“mixtures” cheaper.
Phupps, the grocer on the corner, had a
small customer in one early morning.
“Muvver says to gimme a Age. and not to
forget to say please”—putting a penny on
the counter.
Phupps silently handed over the morning
paper, and put the penny in the till. Busi-
ness went on in fits and starts for about an
hour, when a gentleman came in shirt-sleeves
and slippers for some middle-cut of bacon
and half a pound of butter, which Phupps
served with deference. While he was cutting
the bacon the small customer returned with
the crumpled-up Age.
“Muvver says it isn’t the Age she wants,
but an aig, and not to forget to say she’s
sorry.”
“Tell yer mother with my compliments ter
teach yer to speak correctly,” said Phupps
sarcastically. “And also tell ’er with my
compliments not ter keep the paper till she
gets the guts outer the war news before she
remembers it’s a egg she wants.”
Turning to the gentleman in shirt-sleeves
and slippers he commented : “The people in
this distric’ is full of points. If I might say,
it is not a selec’ neighborhood, with all due
respec’ to you. sir. We ’ave some rum ’uns
’ere, and that’s a fact; but the mother and
father of this child takes the biscuit.”
He handed an egg to the child, and the
child left. Phupps leaned his two hands on
top of the bacon knife, the front of which
rested on the counter.
“It’s ’ard enough to make a livin’ out of
groceries at any place, but there’s no points
in it in this part. The mother and father of
that little child is holy terrors, and there’s
no other word that fits ’em. This dodge of
a Age and a egg is, comparatively-speakin’,
a new one. Y’see, they’ve borrowed the paper
till people won’t stand ’em any more, and
now they’re gettin’ it on the egg dodge. This
is the third time they’ve come it on me. It
nseter be butter. The child comes in and
says: ‘ ’Alt' pound er the best butter, please.
When they say ‘please’ I ought ter be on me
guard. There’s always some point if they’re
extry polite. Well, later on in comes the kid
again, and says: ‘Mother says the best
butter.’ ‘Well,’ I says. 4 ’aven’t I give her
the best butter?’ ‘Mother says it’s only fit
for axle-grease, en if y’aven’t got any better
to give me the money back?’ When I weighs
the butter I finds it’s two ounces short.
Now, I puts it to you, sir, ’ow is it possible
to make a livin’ and keep respectable?"
“It’s hard, certainly,” admitted the cus-
tomer.
“It’s not on’y butter, but it’s spuds, too —
anything, in fact, that something can be
pinched from ; and that’s anything that’s not
in tins. And it’s worse if the man or the
Woman comes in themselves. They go round
tastin’ things till they've eaten a meal, en
liftin’ up the lids of the biscuit-tins, till I’ve
’ad to keep the biscuits in the back part of
the shop. That’s ’ow it is you don’t see any-
thing you can eat anywhere ’andy to cus-
tomers.
“The man is known to the ’awkers as‘The
Glutton.’ They live in that old red ’ouse —
No. 69. It is their own ’ouse, and they own
the place next door, which they let for six
shillin’s a week. They live on that. ’Ow .'
The Lord only knows. You see him sittin’
out at the front door all day lookin’ for
’awkers, and he shouts for ’em to come up in
a ’orrible voice you can hear five blocks
away. He eats one of everything on the
cart, and if his wife is near he calls her out
to eat a sample of each as well, and also give
some to the child, en then they won’t buy
anything. The ’awkers used to swear
’•orrible at first, till they found they was back-
numbers in swearin’, and now you never see
tt ’aAVker come near the street. They’re afraid
of him.
“It’s not on’y th’ ’awkers is afraid of him.
Everybody is afraid of ’im, and in conse-
quence property ain’t what it was. Once the
street was quite uppish, but now people lives
in the ’ouses who have to take things as they
find ’em, and most of them can’t pay their
way, and it’s made business bad, and the
places they live in bein’ their own property
there’s no way of gettin’ rid of them.”
At this point Mr. Phupps lowered his
voice to almost a whisper, and spoke in a
more earnest tone.
“All this is bad enough, but —I don’t like
to mention such things, but he’s a 1.W.W..
he is. I’m a broad-minded Wesleyan myself,
fly broad-minded I mean if you tells me
you go to a church I won’t ask you what
church you goes to. This man we’re just
discussin’ tries ter get into conversation with
men who pass his house so he can argue if
there’s a supreme Bein’, and if lie can’t make,
’em agree with ’im he wants to fight ’em. en
livin’ so close to my shop don’t tend to make
business any better. ’Alt' a pound of butter
I think you said, sir?” he broke off hurriedly.
A woman with hot hair and a vehement
manner entered the shop.
“A word with you, Mr. Phupps,” she said
in a determined voice.
“In a minute, ma’am, in a minute.” Mr.
Phupps’s manner had become distinctly
uneasy. “Is there anything else you require,
sir
“What’s this you’ve bin tollin’ me kid? I
want a immedjit answer.”
“Really, Mrs. ”
“None of yer missusing, Phupps. Do y
think becos y’ keeps a shop that yer a multi-
millionaire and .able to lord it over the rest
of us like serfs? Let me tell y’ me ’usband
is a property-owner in this street, and if [e
wants a Age he can give his penny for it
with the rest of ’em. I’m not in the ’abit of
saying ‘egg’ when I mean ‘Age.’ ”
“Nobody does, me good woman,” said
Phupps. “They always say ‘Age’ when they
mean ‘egg,’ en that’s what I object to.”
“Well, I won’t have insinuations thrown
up at me and me ’usband, and that’s straight.
When it comes to character, it’s an’ ’orse of
a different color. Nobody’s goin’ ter say
that toeVe not respectable without goin’ on
their bended knees an’ apologisin’.”
“I didn’t say you was not respectable,
ma’am.”
“No, not in words, but y’ meant it. It’s
bin said before, but never a second time be
the same person. And who are you, I’d like
to know, you bald-’eaded, snuffle-bustin’,
dampin’-the-sugar cheat! Everybody knows
what you are. You’re the talk of the street,
y’ old pot-bellied apology for a man !”
Mr. Phupps was beginning to get warm
under the personalities.
“There’s one thing, me good woman,” he
said with dignity—“l’m not a atheist.”
“Now you’ve done it, Phupps,” cried the
woman, jumping up and down with wrath.
“I quite expect to ’ave me own character
torn to shreds when I come into the shop to
defend myself, but don’t you dare to cast
reflections on me ’usband. Don’t you dare!
Don’t you dare!”
“Will you leave my shop, please?” asked
Mr. Phupps.
“Now you done it, Phupps,” screamed the
woman, with pointed finger. “I didn't come
into your shop to raise a row. but now I’ll
tell you what I think of you. You!”
“Kindly leave me shop,” said Phupps,
firmly, with a look of steel in his usually
mild eyes.
“Yes, I’ll go, never to put a foot in it
again,” and with one last jump up and down
she whirled from the premises.
“As I was sayin’.” remarked Mr. Phupps,
after a few seconds, “this is not a selec’
neighborhood, and when you ’ave to do busi-
ness in it unpleasant things will occasionally
occur. Let me see, ‘ ’Alf a pound of bacon,
middle cut, it was, if I made no mistake, and
’alf a pound of butter?’ That will be”
' A big man with a creased face suddenly
pushed against the air into the shop. The
creases showed more darkly for want of
washing, and the man himself seemed to
radiate combative energy.
“Wot’s the bother, Phupps?” he asked in a
grating voice.
Phupps wilted. You could see him wilt.
He took up the bacon-knife by force of habit
and stuck it point down and rested both
hands on top of the handle.
“If I might say it, Mr. Donkin, yer missus
just come in and got unreasonably excited,
but nothing to make a fuss about. This
gentleman was here, and will tell you”
“I want no talk, you little skunk! You
can talk to wbmen, you measly pimple; but
(Continued on next page.)

Do you look so well as you once did? Do you feel so well? If not, have you sus-

soon tell on the looks—soon age the sufferer; the skin becomes sallow, the. eyes, lose

affected by kidney poisons ; the sufferer grows moody, listless, irritable, feels in every-

Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills succeed in all cases of Kidney and Bladder trouble
because they are a true kidney medicine —they don’t act on the bowels, but give all their
healing powers to the kidneys, helping them in their great blood-purifying work. Doan’s
Pills begin at once to strengthen and tone up the tired kidney membranes, and help the
kidneys to drive out the clogging kidney poisons that cause aching back and pains in the
loins, urinary troubles and gravel, nervousness, dizzy spells and heart trouble, dropsy,
and the fatal Bright’s disease. Our neighbor’s word, given here is good proof of how
truly the genuine Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills will help the sufferer.

“Really, Mr. Donkin,” said Mr. Phupps,.

“I’ve always let you alone because I

He leaned over the counter and took Mr.

violently. Mr. Phupps turned red at the
touch and looked excited.
“Now, what am I?” asked the big man.
Mr. Phupps was genuinely angry now. “I
know what sort of a ’nostic you are,” he
shouted. “You’re not a agnostic. You’re a
cross between a AGE-nostie and a egg-nostic.”
Donkin fell back with astonishment. It
seemed preparatory to actually exterminat-
ing the grocer. Before he recovered speech,
however, Phupps darted round the counter
wildly waving the bacon knife and shouting,
“Get outer me shop l Go on ! Get outer me
shop !”
Donkin looked as if he would if he could,
but Phupps had cut off his communications
by coming out by the end near the door.
One moment Donkin looked uncertain
whether he would bluff it or fight it out, but
the wild waving of the bacon knife decided
the question.

ROBERT BROTHERS.
The Rising of the Moon.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BILL SMITH, casual Australian and
electrician at the Opera House,
slowly walked up the stage to a small
wooden box, holding an electric globe and
faced with a disc, which was sus-
pended betweeli two vertical wires that
reached the roof.
From the dressing-rooms, which abutted
on the stage, came the rumble of bass and
the clearness of soprano against the commo-
tion of voluble Tuscan and .unadorned Aus-
tralian ; and the noise of the scenery, moving
heavily into place, competed with the orches-
tra tuning its instruments under the stage.
A scene depicting a mountain-top, with a
ponderous cloud effect, was lowered from the
“flies” for use as a back cloth, as Bill, in-
different to the atmosphere of temperamental
excitement associated with the opening night
of Italian Grand Opera, casually inspected
the box, which played an important part as
the moon.
To conveniently assist a harmonious
bestowal of her hand upon that lover who
was capable of the more passionate serenade,
it was necessary to the action of the opera
that the moon should appear when melodi-
ously summoned by the soprano.
The work of slowly raising the moon was
Bill’s; and in the hope of smoother working
the Italian stage-manager had marked three
lines on the back of the scene, laboriously
explaining to Bill that, upon a certain pas-
sage in the soprano’s solo, the moon would
be raised to the first line,' level with the
mountain-top; then, when the basso (who
was the villain, naturally) pledged his troth,
the moon, raised to the next line, would be
obscured by the cloud; finally, lifted higher
still, it would cause the tenor to burst into
triumphant melody as the clear moonlight
flooded the betrothal scene.
A painted gauze transparency let into the
scene helped the illusion.
“Eet ees importe!” the stage-manager
warned.
“It’ll be right, cully,” responded Bill, who
nevertheless felt that much would be left in
the hands of the gods.
The not-unpleasant dissonance from
under the stage increased in volume, and
died down; and the expectant tension was
broken by a loud voice, declaring his in-
tention of “jobbin’ the Dago,” while an
infuriated stage-manager, handicapped by
limited Australian, consoled himself by
snapping his fingers at a grinning stage-
hand and calling him a “deenkum cow !”
A sudden silence, settling over the
theatre as the conductor took his stand,
was felt on the stage. Then a jangled burst
of harmony drew a reverent gasp from the
weirdly-attired singers.
A chubby figure in peasant costume,
walking past Bill, touched his arm, and,
blowing a kiss airwards, rapturously ex-
claimed : “Ah. dat ees museec!”
“My oath!” grinned Bill. He switched
on the illuminant in the moon box as the
little soprano rustled round the scene.
“Da moon—you char-rge of, signor?” she
smiled.
“Call me Bill, lady,” he smiled in return.
“Quando —eet go up, make I cantellera-
mente. Cosi” —— fixing her dark eyes on
Bill’s abashed gaze she trilled the cue that
brought the moon peeping above the moun-
tain.
“I c’d listen to yer all night,” he mur-
mured as the song finished.
His eyes followed her as she tripped
down the stage, leaving him vaguely dis-
turbed. He was conscious of a jumbling
effect of flowering gums, purple distances
and singing birds.
The heavy-moustached basso, dressed in
vari-colored garb as a brigand, clanked
imperiously to the moon, and, clutching his
sword, threw his cloak around him with a
long-drawn “Ah-h-h !”
“Good-night, mate,” Bill affably greeted
him.
Frowningly the basso emitted another
“Ah-h-h !”
“ ’Ow’s she bumpin’?” asked Bill, feeling
the need of conversation. /'
“Quando io fatto”
“Hey, wait a bit. Now come agen !”
“Leve dela lune. Dela lune”—he pointed
to the moon. “Quando”
“Oh-h, I got yer. Yer gimme the cue to
push ’er up!”
“Io fatto”
“Wot’s the cue, though?”
“Io cantelleramente”
“Gorstrewth, turn it up!” Bill was
befogged. Making signs of raising the
moon he yelled : “W’en-w’eno do moono go
up?”
“Va via— I-go—sing !” Breathing deeply,
he rumbled a few bars of his song. A
light broke in. upon Bill. “Righto, leave
it ter me, mate. . Up she goes, w’en yer sing
‘La da da diddle omp !’ ” Majestically bow-
ing his thanks, which Bill returned, the
basso clanked away.
A tense silence fell as the overture fin-
ished. The singers, moving restlessly about
the wings, awaited their call.
Then the sickening thought that he had
forgotten the cue that raised the moon for
the soprano flashed into Bill's mind. Rush-
ing round from behind the scene, in an
attempt to find her, he crashed into the
tenor, a glorious creature, with pink and
white cheeks, who fluently poured a string
of native maledictions on the head of the
cretino.
“Beg pardon, ’Orace.”
Regaining his poise, the tenor pointed to
the moon.
“You dela June—up, eh? Io fatto cantel-
lera”
“I know,” interrupted Bill. “All yous
blokes ’ave been puttin’ that over me.
Wot’s the cue?”
“Ah-h, da basso seeng—no good! La
lune eet under-r go da cloud. lo—me seeng.
Ah-h, beautiful!” He blew a kiss skywards.
“No cloud —eet shine.”
“Yes, but wot partic’lar ‘lum tiddely on
pom’ do yer sing?”
The tenor blankly gazed at Bill for a
moment, then caught the drift of his
question.
“Ah—beautiful!” He drew himself up,
and extending his hands, broke the silence
with an expressive “La Donna”
From all parts of the stage came angry
cries.
“Go on, ’Orace,” urged Bill. “Don’t let
’em bluff yer.”
The tenor, resenting an imperious sh-sh
from the stage-manager, engaged him in
animated altercation, which only ceased
when the bell signalling the curtain-rise
sounded.
Bill tensely listened when the applause
greeting the soprano died away. Every note
she sang seemed familiar with the air
trilled behind the scene.
“Might as well chance it now,” he
decided, raising the moon.
A wild-eyed stage-manager breathlessly
rushed from the prompt corner, and
besought Bill to “Pull eet down !” Down
came the moon !
“You ar-re so soon,” he cried. “Now I
wait —I tell you.”
As the moon reappeared a laugh came
from the auditorium. Clutching Bill’s arm
he hissed : “You keel a. dees scene—l keela
you. So !” He thumped his heart.
“I’ll ’ave to dot yuh one,” remarked Bill,
“if y’ get too fresh.”
The rumbling notes of the basso now
broke in upon the lighter voice, and mark-
ing the line that would bring the moon
under the cloud, Bill vainly sought to dis-
tinguish the “la da da diddle omp” that
had been impressed on him. An imperative
“Queek!” broke his indecision, and Bill
excitedly gave the moon a sudden jerk,
which sent it above the cloud. Realising
that if left in that position it would inter-
fere’ with the serenade that followed, he
pulled it downwards to the marked line.
The song suddenly ceased, and the audience
greeted the action with a roar of laughter
that disconcerted the basso. The rolling
eye and white teeth of the stage-manager
showed round the scene, and Bill, growing
desperate under the exacting circumstances,
rolled his sleeves in a threatening manner.
The frantic Italian, however, kept his dis-
tance, contenting himself with a heartfelt
fluency of curse.
“Look 'ere, lad,” warned Bill. “Some?
body’ll get a crack on the nose ter-night.”
The lilting melody of the tenor, desiring
a brighter operatic moon, was disregarded
by the muttering electrician, who grimly
eyed the invocating stage-manager.
“Up-a ! Up-a !! He seeng how briglit-a
da moon!” The stout peasant clutched
Bill in a fever of excitement. “Up-a, fool
—queek!” he shrilled, fumbling about the
wires in a vain endeavor to raise the
troublesome orb.
Above the crash of cymbal and blare of
brass a bedlam of excited Tuscan was
heard, and Bill dispassionately planted a
straight left on the chin of the
peasant. And how surrounded by a jabber-
ing chaos of alien tongue, he seized the
wires that controlled the raising and lower-
ing of the moon.
“Yer want ’er up?” lie jerked the wire.
“Well, up she goes! Now down she comes!
Up! Down!’’ lie suited the action to the
word. u Down! Up!"
The spectacle of an acrobatic moon
proved too much for the audience, which
let itself go.
The stage-manager rang down the curtain
and joined the infuriated singers, headed by
the basso with drawn sword, who were
advancing on Bill.
Seizing a brace —an iron rod, about four
feet in length, used for clamping heavily-
built scenery to the floor —Bill awaited the
onslaught.
“Gorblime, there’ll be a few white men
sellin’ de steak an’ de oyst’ after this.
Come on!” he yelled:—“if yer lookin’ for
trouble!”
The stage-manager, forcing his way
through the crowd, out of range of the
brace, gesticulated in stammering frenzy.
“Out-a ! Sack-a!. .. .DeeschaUgc—you—
you....porco!”
“Gretino!” shrieked the tenor.
“Cano!” roared the basso.
“So’s yer oV man!" replied Bill.
Holding the brace in a convenient posi-
tion he strolled through the opening made
by the scampering crowd, followed by dire-
ful threats. Reaching the stage-door, Bill
turned and delivered a homely address on
the circumscribed area of vocation—outside
the selling of fish—permitted the Latin races
generally.
RICHARD BELLAIRS.

There are Three Marked
Advantages in the Use
of these Dies.
Dies.
The Dies are opened or closed by a single movement of the lever on the side of
the stock. When a thread has been cut to the required length, the operator
pushes the lever, thus throwing the Dies open, clear of the thread, and the whole
tool is quickly lifted off .the finished work. When free of the work, the reversing
of the lever closes the Dies exactly to size again, ready to cut another thread.
ADVANTAGES:—
(1) The time usually wasted in running back over the finished thread is saved
—about one-third of the total time.
(2.) The marring or stripping of the threads, caused by chips crowding into
the Die teeth in turning back, is avoided.
(3) The life of the Die is almost doubled, as turning back over the thread in the
old way causes much wear of the Dies.
